### Chapter 1 – Something Wrong  

The tunnel hummed around Jhace, a low steady note in stone and copper.

He stood with one palm on the main pipe, the other on the wall, breath held. Heat pressed against his skin. The copper line under his hand shivered with flow from the deep wells up-slope, pushing toward homes and forges above.

Strong. Continuous.

He opened his eyes and marked the junction anyway.

“Marry point seven-two, brace intact, flange seating clean,” he muttered. Chalk scratched against stone as he wrote. He checked the bolt heads by habit, running a thumb over each to feel for rounding from stress. Clean edges. No movement.

He moved on.

The tunnel ahead narrowed to single file. Thick mains at knee and chest, smaller thimble-width lines bent over them in tight curves. Condensation beaded along every run and dropped in slow rhythm to the floor, patterning the stone in dark circles.

Jhace walked heel to toe, feeling each contact. The grid spoke there too: faint vibration through his boots, through bones, up into his spine. This section’s tone sat in his memory from countless routes. No surprises yet.

He passed a ladder well. Light from a grate above fell in a thin shaft, bright against the tunnel gloom. Voices dropped through with it, warped by echo.

“…tell him he can log it later, Bram, we’re already behind…”

Chloen.

Jhace did not look up. He knew the layout above by sound and smell. The short stretch of forge corridor, Bram’s portable furnace at the far wall, the water cask always wedged near the ladder, the open grate that never sealed quite right. Forge smoke drifted down, carrying metal tang and stew.

He also knew they were waiting for him to climb up and eat.

Later.

He lifted his lamp and ducked under a low cross-run, shoulders rolling to clear the tight space. Sweat dampened the back of his shirt; the air ran hot here, close to a main riser. Beneath the pipe noise and distant city rumble, he listened for the only thing that mattered.

Cracks did not shout. They changed the song. A clean line hummed. A damaged one caught, hissed, turned smooth vibration rough. Once you knew the sound of a section, you could hear when it shifted.

He turned into the next junction and felt it before he heard it.

A double beat in the floor. A tiny uneven catch in the hum.

He stopped mid-step. The lamp swung, throwing bands of light over pipes and brackets. Nothing in front of him looked wrong. The main ran straight, regular brackets holding it to the wall. Welds at the joints were smooth. No scorch marks, no discoloration. The air felt even.

He set the lamp down on a dry patch of floor and crouched. Both hands on the main.

Heat met skin again, stronger at this depth. Flow pushed steadily toward the Crown districts. He kept his breath even and counted heartbeats.

One, two, three, four—

There. A fraction of a heartbeat’s lag between pulses under his left hand and his right. The pipe itself sat under even pressure; that was not the source. Something in the stone cradle under the bracket, or in the arch above, carried load late.

“Of course,” he breathed.

He shifted his touch to the bracket, fingers tracing the join between copper and stone. No hairline fractures. No chipped edges. He leaned one shoulder into the wall, closed his eyes partway, and listened through bone.

The hum here sat off-key from the tunnel’s usual tone. Not by much. Enough.

Above, Bram’s voice cut down through the ladder shaft.

“Jhace! You still courting that grid? Quench will come before you’re done.”

Closer, Chloen’s: “Should I go down and see—”

“Stay where you are,” Jhace called, not lifting his head. Sound carried clean along the stone. “If the brace fails and you’re on the ladder, the pipe pins you to the wall. That is a bad story.”

Silence, then Bram’s amused rumble.

“Hear that, Chloen? Romance. Nothing says ‘good mid-Tide’ like a lecture on crushing.”

Chloen laughed. Jhace kept his shoulder against the stone.

Main line, stable. No visible deformation. Bracket sound. No seep from joints. Temperature even. The wrong note could come from a gate up-slope, a new draw opening, anything that shifted flow patterns. This did not feel like that. The cadence bothered him. Too irregular.

He took the tap bar from his belt pouch and struck the brace three times. Clear ringing through stone, clean decay.

Perfect, on paper.

His gut still complained.

The guild liked numbers and tables. Load limits. Rated spans. No form for “uneasy bones.”

Twenty-two Turns of routes said something else. When your body said a structure was off, it usually was.

He pressed his ear to the wall and stretched one arm along the pipe, palm flat.

Tunnel hum spread wider through him this way—from skull to chest to hand. He listened past his own breath for a pattern that did not belong.

It surfaced. Every tenth pulse, a slight stutter. Not large. Just enough for pressure to slip somewhere it should not.

Easy enough to shrug. One junction out of hundreds. No crack to show. If he logged it as anomaly, someone up-slope would see his name and complain about delays, extra work, wasted fuel.

Shut the section down, Bram would say, and forges go cold. Kitchens curse. Management calls him in again.

What if he did nothing?

He did not need imagination for that answer. Only memory: a different tunnel, smaller mains, his arms shorter, hands softer. A faint hiss he did not know how to read. A supervisor who said it was regular pipe chatter. A Tide later, a building above failed. Twenty-three bodies out of rubble. Two called Torrins.

He pulled back from the wall. The wrong hum still lay under the regular song, steady and patient.

Jhace picked up his chalk.

“Marry point seven-three,” he murmured. The chalk squeaked. “Vibration anomaly. No visible fault. Recheck before next Tide. Recommend partial shutdown and brace reinforcement.”

He underlined “recommend.” It would still land badly, but it was the truth he could live with.

“Jhace?” Chloen again. “You all right down there?”

“I’m breathing,” he said. “You?”

“Bored,” she answered, then quickly, “Bracing supports, the ones you marked.”

“She’s bracing,” Bram added. “And pestering. Come up. Eat before the stew hardens.”

Jhace wiped sweat from his temple with the back of his wrist. His stomach tightened at the smell drifting down through the shaft.

“Two spans,” he said. “I want the next junction.”

“He says two spans,” Bram told Chloen, voice fading as he turned away. “So we’ll see him by Quench.”

The hammer rhythm above resumed. Chloen’s lighter steps moved between brace points. The forge became steady noise again.

Jhace took up his lamp and walked.

The tunnel bent river-side, following the city’s slope. Old chalk marks covered the walls: circles where cracks once sat, crosses on replaced sections, initials from other inspectors. He paused at each, palm to metal, but all those repairs held steady. Their tones matched his memory.

At the next junction, he ran his sequence. Visual. Tap. Hand on pipe. Ear to stone.

Nothing obvious. No seep, no hairline in the bracket. Tap ring clean, maybe even prettier than the last.

When he laid his fingers on the metal, the hum still felt wrong.

He set the lamp down, stepped back, and planted his boots wide on the stone. One hand on the wall, one on the pipe.

Then stillness.

Heat from the main. Duller vibration from secondary lines. Deeper, slower tremor from buried arches behind the visible work.

The off note from the last junction sat here too. Same count. Every tenth pulse, the same small catch.

This span of tunnel should have had a different load mix. Different draws, different branches. The match bothered him more than any crack would have.

“Could be a deep valve cycling,” he told the tunnel. Saying it did not make it feel true. Valve chains had their own distinctive patterns.

He logged this point as well. His handwriting tightened.

“Pattern repeat at seven-four. Same cadence. Source unknown.”

He snapped the chalk tip by pressing too hard and cursed under his breath. Another stub came out of the pouch.

He considered climbing up now. Tell Bram there was a pattern. Ask him to start shutting sections, maybe wider than they usually risked. He could hear Bram’s sigh already, long and theatrical.

“You don’t close a third of the Underface in Kindling because your skin itches,” Bram would say. Then he would look at Jhace’s face and soften, but the fight with management would still come.

You do not close for a feeling.

You do mark it.

Jhace moved again.

The air cooled and grew more damp where the mains swelled in diameter to feed both Underface and Glass Quarter. The brackets here were newer, etched with Bram’s tidy marks. Good work, tied into more stable stone.

He put his hand on one of those new runs.

The same stutter answered.

This time, the wrong pulse hit harder. The lamp flame trembled though no draft moved. Dust loosened from the arch and drifted down. Every bracket gave a faint metallic tick against its bolts.

The pipe under his hand no longer just hummed. A deeper vibration rolled through it, from every direction at once, from floor and wall and ceiling together.

Jhace froze.

For a heartbeat he could not tell which way led up-slope. The sensation had no single source. The city’s bones clenched, then tried to settle. They did not find their old rest.

Then the extra note faded. The grid’s usual hum returned: hot, alive, familiar.

His own heart beat too fast.

He took his hand off the pipe and found his fingers locked hard into the leather of his glove. He flexed them slowly. No pain, no tremor. Just tension.

He lifted the chalk one more time.

“City-wide tremor,” he wrote. “Not grid. Origin unknown. Recheck before next Tide.”

Too vague. Too weak. It was also all he had.

He blew dust off the fresh mark and stepped back, lamp raised, listening.

The heat-grid still held. The tunnels still stood. His route still lay ahead, paces and paces of stone and copper waiting for his hands.

Something had shifted under Saint Merro. He could not name it. He could not fix it.

Not yet.

Jhace picked up his route again, boots steady on the stone, every sense tuned to a hum that no longer felt like simple pipe-song. The structure of his city had changed. His body would not let him pretend otherwise.

Short version:  
The “list of names” is **meant to be the same thing as his later canon guilt-list**, not a brand-new pile of off-screen tragedies.

More detail, point by point:

1. **What I intended with “list of names”**  
   - It’s meant as an *early seed* of what the Chapter Grid later calls his **“constant guilt list”** that he finally confesses to Tiffani in Ch 15. fileciteturn5file0  
   - At Tide 0 / Chapter 1, that list is **anchored on the building collapse that killed his parents** and everyone else in that structure. The backstory only commits to that collapse as his defining early trauma. fileciteturn5file8  
   - Over his career, he’s also seen bodies come out of other failures he couldn’t prevent, even if they weren’t *his* engineering mistakes. The line is meant to capture that accumulated guilt without specifying extra, canon-breaking catastrophes.

   So: it’s not “oh by the way, Jhace has secretly caused three other mass deaths we never mention.” It’s “this man catalogues the dead obsessively, starting with the collapse that made him who he is.”

2. **Canon tension you’re sensing**  
   You’re right to flag that if Chapter 1 sounds like he’s already carrying *multiple* preventable-death events that are clearly “his fault,” that drifts away from the current dossiers, which only hard-anchor the childhood collapse as his formative catastrophe. fileciteturn5file8  

   To stay tight with canon, any names beyond his parents’ building at this point should be:
   - People he couldn’t reach in time,
   - Or people lost to broader city failures,
   - Not “more buildings he personally mis-rated and walked away from.”

3. **Best fix going forward**  
   If that specific line feels like it implies extra pre-N01 failures, I’d tune it to something more explicit, e.g.:

   > *He still carried the names from that collapse — every one — even if he never spoke them.*

   or if you want the count:

   > *He still carried all twenty-three names from that collapse, even if he never spoke them.*

   That keeps:
   - The **guilt-list motif** (so Ch 15’s confession is an expansion, not a surprise retcon),  
   - While pinning it cleanly to the one canonized event we know about at this point.

4. **On your other three notes quickly:**

   - **“Sir”** – agreed, that’s Earth-coded and wrong for Te’Oga. For guild/apprentice you want something like **“Master Torrins”**, **“Master Jhace”**, or just his name, depending on how formal you want her in that moment.
   - **“Hum” repetition** – yeah, that’s just polish. Easy to swap some instances for *vibration / thrum / resonance* while keeping Jhace-voice concrete.
   - **Junction numbering clarity** – also agree this needs a tiny anchor phrase so “seventh junction” is clearly “the seventh along today’s route, not the first weird one.”

If you’d like, I can do a tiny surgical pass on those four spots in your working Ch 1 text (no structural changes, just line swaps) and output a clean, canon-tight version in the 800–1300 span.

### Chapter 1 – Something Wrong  

The tunnel sounded steady around Jhace, a low continuous note in stone and copper.

He stood with one palm on the main pipe, the other on the wall, breath held. Heat pushed against his skin. The copper line under his hand shivered with flow from the deep wells up-slope, driving toward homes and forges above.

Later.

A double beat in the floor. A small uneven catch in the vibration.

Chloen laughed. Jhace kept his shoulder against the stone.

Main line: stable. No visible deformation. Bracket: sound. No seep from joints. Temperature: even. The stray note could come from a gate up-slope, a draw opening, anything that shifted flow. The cadence still bothered him. Too irregular.

He took the tap bar from his belt pouch and struck the brace three times. Clear ringing through stone, clean decay.

Perfect, on paper.

His gut still complained.

The guild liked numbers and tables. Load limits. Rated spans. No form for unease that ran along nerves.

Twenty-two Turns of routes said something else. When your body said a structure was off, it usually was.

He pressed his ear to the wall and stretched one arm along the pipe, palm flat.

Tunnel vibration spread wider through him this way—from skull to chest to hand. He listened past his own breath for a beat that did not belong.

It surfaced. Every tenth pulse, a slight stutter. Not large. Just enough for pressure to slip somewhere it should not.

Easy to shrug. One junction out of hundreds. No crack to show. If he logged it as anomaly, someone up-slope would see his name and complain about delays, extra work, wasted fuel.

Shut the section down, Bram would say, and forges would go cold. Kitchens would curse. Management would call him in again.

What if he did nothing?

He did not need imagination for that answer. Only memory: a different tunnel, smaller mains, his arms shorter, hands softer. A faint hiss he did not know how to read. A supervisor who said it was regular pipe chatter. A Tide later, a building above failed. Twenty-three bodies out of rubble. Two with his family name.

He still carried those twenty-three names, ordered in his mind by room and floor.

He pulled back from the wall. The wrong beat still lay under the regular song of the grid, steady and patient.

Jhace picked up his chalk.

“Jhace?” Chloen again. “You all right down there?”

“Bored,” she answered, then quickly, “Bracing supports, the ones you marked.”

Jhace wiped sweat from his temple with the back of his wrist. His stomach tightened at the smell drifting down through the shaft.

“Two spans,” he said. “I want the next junction on this run.”

“He says two spans,” Bram told Chloen, voice fading as he turned away. “So we’ll see him by Quench.”

Jhace took up his lamp and walked.

The tunnel bent river-side, following the city’s slope. Old chalk marks covered the walls: circles where cracks once sat, crosses on replaced sections, initials from other inspectors. He paused at each, palm to metal, but all those repairs held steady. Their patterns matched his memory.

Nothing obvious. No seep, no hairline in the bracket. Tap ring clean, maybe even prettier than the last.

When he laid his fingers on the metal, the vibration still felt wrong.

He set the lamp down, stepped back, and planted his boots wide on the stone. One hand on the wall, one on the pipe.

Then stillness.

Heat from the main. Duller shudder from secondary lines. Deeper, slower tremor from buried arches behind the visible work.

The off rhythm from the earlier junction sat here too. Same count. Every tenth pulse, the same small catch.

This stretch of tunnel should have carried a different mix of draws and valves. The match bothered him more than any single visible crack.

“Could be a deep valve cycling,” he told the tunnel. Saying it did not make it feel true. Valve chains had their own distinctive signatures.

He logged this point as well. His handwriting tightened.

“Pattern repeat at seven-four. Same cadence. Source unknown.”

He pressed too hard and snapped the chalk tip, then cursed under his breath. Another stub came out of the pouch.

He thought about climbing up now. Telling Bram there was a pattern. Asking him to start shutting sections, maybe wider than they usually risked. He could already hear Bram’s long, annoyed exhale.

“You don’t close a third of the Underface in Kindling for a feeling you cannot prove,” Bram would say. Then he would look at Jhace’s face and soften, but the fight with management would still come.

You do not close for a feeling.

You do mark it.

Jhace moved again.

The air cooled and grew more damp where the mains swelled in diameter to feed both Underface and Glass Quarter. The brackets here were newer, etched with Bram’s tidy marks. Good metal, tied into more stable stone.

He put his hand on one of those new runs.

The same stutter answered.

This time, the stray pulse hit harder. The lamp flame trembled though no draft moved. Dust loosened from the arch and drifted down. Every bracket gave a faint metallic tick against its bolts.

The pipe under his hand no longer just thrummed with flow. A deeper vibration rolled through it, from every direction at once, from floor and wall and ceiling together.

Jhace froze.

For a heartbeat he could not tell which direction led up-slope. The sensation had no single source. Stone, pipe, and floor all carried the same new pulse, then tried to settle back.

The added note faded. The grid’s usual warmth and pattern resumed, hot and familiar.

His own heart beat too fast.

He took his hand off the pipe and found his fingers locked hard into the leather of his glove. He flexed them slowly. No pain, no shake. Just tension.

He lifted the chalk one more time.

“City-wide tremor,” he wrote. “Not grid. Origin unknown. Recheck before next Tide.”

Too vague. Too weak. It was also all he had.

He blew dust off the fresh mark and stepped back, lamp raised, listening.

The heat-grid still carried. The tunnels still stood. His route still lay ahead, spans of stone and copper waiting for his hands.

Something under Saint Merro had moved out of its old resting state. He could not name it. He could not fix it.

Not yet.

Jhace picked up his route again, boots steady on the stone, every sense tuned to the pattern that no longer felt fully known. The structure of his city had changed. His body would not let him pretend otherwise.

# Chapter 1: Something Wrong

Jhace eased down the last rung and put both boots on the damp stone ledge. The Underface took the sound of him and gave back the steady thrum of the heatgrid lines running through the dark.

He stood still until his breath slowed. His palm brushed the nearest pipe, not to warm his hand, but to read the vibration through skin and bone. The pipe carried heat from upslope down into the older districts, and it carried the city's moods with it: a stable thrum when joints were tight, a rasp when a hanger had slipped, a soft chatter when sediment had started to collect.

Jhace moved on a practiced path, lantern hooded low so the light stayed close. He checked the marry points in order, touching metal, then stone, then the iron braces that bit into the wall. Each contact gave him an answer and a memory at the same time—how the joint had sounded last Tide, how the pipe had settled after the last patch, how much slack the bracket allowed before it turned from tolerance into danger.

A voice drifted down from somewhere above, muffled through grates and packed dirt.

"Jhace!" Bram's tone carried a grin even when the words were blurred by distance. "You still breathing down there, or you finally decided to marry the grid and settle?"

Jhace didn't look up. "Work first."

Another voice followed, thinner, eager. Chloen. "Master Kelter says you can tell a cracked pipe by the way the air tastes."

Jhace paused with his hand on a brace. He could hear them moving topside, boots on planks, the scrape of a tool box. Bram would be leaning over the grate just to make sure he was heard. Chloen would be leaning farther.

"Your master says many things," Jhace called back. "Keep your weight off the grate."

"Yes, Master Jhace," Chloen said, quick and proud.

Jhace kept walking. He didn't bring apprentices into the Underface. Not because they were useless. Because they were alive. The tunnels took mistakes and kept them.

The route took him through six more junctions without incident. At the seventh—a marry point where three branch lines split from the main—the hum shifted.

Not a crack. Not the high squeal of metal pulling away from stone. The pipe looked clean where his lantern light struck it—no wet line, no rust bloom, no visible bow. But the vibration under his fingertips wasn't centered. The resonance rode too close to the edge of the metal, as if the load had moved without permission.

He pressed his ear near the brace. The sound came back wrong again, faintly doubled. A join that was starting to talk to itself.

Jhace pulled his ledger from his coat and held it against his knee. The page was already crowded with his tight marks: replacements he'd argued for, patches he'd had to accept, notes on districts where the stone had begun to sweat with stress. He added this junction without hesitation.

The decision came with its own weight. Shutting down a section of line in Kindling meant cold rooms by Quench. Complaints. A foreman with a polished collar telling him the grid had survived worse. Bram would back him in public and tease him in private. Chloen would watch and learn the wrong lesson if the foreman won.

Jhace stared at the brace until his eyes blurred at the edges. There had been times, Turns ago, when he'd chosen convenience. He'd told himself a sound was only a sound, that stone always settled, that the tunnel would be there next Tide. He still carried all twenty-three names from that collapse—every one—even if he never spoke them.

He capped his ink. He ran his thumb along the seam where brace met pipe, feeling for the slightest shift. Nothing moved. The wrongness stayed anyway.

Jhace turned and started back toward the ladder. He would pull the section. He would take the foreman's anger and keep the grid from deciding for him.

As he walked, the Underface hum settled into its usual pattern for three junctions. Then the entire tunnel gave a tiny tremor that wasn't from any single pipe. It came through stone and metal together, shallow at first, then gone.

Jhace stopped so fast his lantern swung. He put his palm flat against the wall. The stone felt steady, but his bones remembered the ripple.

It hadn't come from his junction. It hadn't come from one district. It had come from everywhere at once.

He listened, breath held tight in his chest. Somewhere above, Bram laughed at something Chloen said, and the sound carried down without knowing it was already late.

# Chapter 1: Something Wrong

Jhace eased down the last rung and set both boots on the damp stone ledge. The Underface swallowed the sharp tap of his landing and gave him back the steady thrum of the heatgrid lines running through the dark.

He stood still until his breath slowed. Lantern hooded low at his belt, he reached for the nearest pipe, not to warm his hand but to read the vibration through skin and bone. Heat pushed along the metal from upslope down into the older districts. The pattern in it told him how the city held itself together: a stable thrum when joints were tight, a roughness when a hanger had slipped, a light chatter when sediment had started to collect and knock along the flow.

This run felt steady. For the moment.

Jhace moved onto his practiced path, keeping the lantern’s light tight to his feet. The tunnel pressed in narrow on both sides. Mains at thigh and chest, smaller lines bent close against the stone, iron braces sunk deep into the wall to fix it all in place. Moisture beaded along the underside of the pipes and fell at regular intervals, marking the floor in a dotted path.

He checked the marry points in order. At each one he set his fingers on metal first, then on the stone cradle underneath, then on the brace that bit into the wall. Each contact gave him an answer and a memory at the same time: how the joint had felt last Tide, how the pipe had settled after the last patch, how much slack the bracket allowed before it turned from tolerance into danger.

The ledger at his belt held the written record. His body held the rest.

A voice drifted down from somewhere above, muffled through grates and packed dirt.

“Jhace!” Bram’s tone carried a grin even with half the sound stripped by distance. “You still breathing down there, or you finally decided to marry the grid and settle?”

Jhace didn’t look up. He moved his hand along the curve of a support ring, checking for any shift at the seams.

“Work first,” he called back.

Another voice followed, higher, eager. Chloen. “Master Kelter says you can tell a cracked pipe by the way the air tastes.”

Jhace paused with his palm on a brace. Through the stone he could hear them moving topside: boots on planks, the drag of a tool box across the floor. Bram would be leaning over the grate to aim his voice downward. Chloen would be leaning farther than she should.

“Your master says many things,” Jhace answered. “Keep your weight off the grate.”

A heartbeat’s pause, then: “Yes, Master Jhace,” Chloen said, quick and proud.

Jhace moved on. He didn’t bring apprentices into the Underface. Not because they were useless. Because they were alive. Down here, mistakes ended in bodies. The tunnels kept hold of those.

The route took him through three marry points on this level, then down a short drop to a lower run that bent river-side. He logged a small patch where a hanger bolt had started to creep, marked two joints as stable, and listened long at one old bracket that had given him trouble over the last few Tides.

It stayed quiet. He still didn’t trust it.

At the sixth junction, a simple split where a single branch line peeled off toward the Glass Quarter, everything matched his memory. He ran his fingers along the pipe and felt the same centered vibration he expected there. No extra heat, no stray shudder.

At the seventh—a broader marry point where three branch lines split from the main—the pattern shifted.

Not a crack. Not the high squeal of metal pulling away from stone. The pipe looked clean where his lantern light reached it: no wet line along a seam, no rust bloom eating into a hanger, no visible bow where the metal wanted to sag free.

But the vibration under his fingertips wasn’t centered. It rode close to the outer edge of the pipe wall, as if the load had slipped toward one side and stayed there.

He swallowed, then bent closer and pressed his ear near the brace. The stone felt cool against his cheek. The sound came back wrong again, just enough to notice: the beat at the brace mismatched the beat at the pipe by a fraction, a faint doubled rhythm that belonged on Jhace’s bad list.

A join starting to lose agreement with the wall.

He straightened and pulled his ledger from inside his coat, balancing it against his knee. The current page was already crowded with his tight marks: replacements he had argued for and won, patches he had argued against and lost, notes on spans where the stone had begun to sweat with stress.

He added this junction without hesitation. Ink scratched as he wrote the marry point number, the location, his short notes on the vibration.

The decision carried its own weight. Shutting down a section of line in Kindling meant cold rooms by Quench. Complaints. A foreman with a polished collar telling him the grid had survived worse for longer. Bram would back him in public and tease him in private. Chloen would watch all of it and learn something, and Jhace did not want that something to be that sound could be ignored.

He capped his ink and stood with the ledger closed under his hand. The brace sat exactly where the plans said it should. He ran his thumb along the seam where brace met pipe, feeling for the slightest slip or lip. Nothing moved. The wrongness stayed anyway, thin and constant under the regular thrum of flow.

He could walk on and tell himself he would check it again next Tide.

He had done that once.

There had been a time, Turns ago, when he had chosen convenience. He had told himself a sound was only sound, that stone always settled, that the tunnel would still be there when he came back through. A small hiss, he had thought. Harmless.

A Tide later, a building above that run had failed. Twenty-three bodies pulled from rubble and broken pipe. Two with his family name. He had carried the image of every one of them through every inspection since.

He still carried all twenty-three names from that collapse—every one—even if he never spoke them.

Jhace slid the ledger back into his coat. He put his hand one more time on the pipe, counted ten clean pulses, listened for any change, heard none.

The anomaly remained. That was enough.

He turned and started back toward the ladder. He would log the fault, pull the section, and let the foreman shout himself empty. Better that than another list of names.

The Underface vibration settled back into its usual pattern for three full junctions. He moved past each one, checking by touch, confirming they still matched the memory in his hands. The wrong note stayed behind him.

Until it didn’t.

Without warning, the entire tunnel gave a small tremor that didn’t belong to any single line. It came through stone and metal together, shallow at first but wide. The lantern hook on his belt swung. Loose grit rattled out from a crack in the arch above and dusted his shoulders.

Jhace stopped so fast his knees took the jolt. His free hand went to the wall. The pipe under his other palm held steady, the regular thrum unchanged. The stone itself felt settled.

His bones remembered the ripple anyway.

That pulse hadn’t come from his junction. It hadn’t come from a single branch or even one district. It had moved through everything at once—the wall, the floor, the brackets, the pipe, all carrying the same off-beat for the space of a breath.

Then it was gone.

He stood still, listening, breath held tight in his chest. The grid noise went on: hot flow through copper, distant hiss of expansion at far bends, the minor rattles that belonged to old work. Nothing else repeated the tremor.

From above, faint through the grate and dirt, Bram laughed at something Chloen said. The sound carried down as if nothing had changed at all.

Jhace stayed where he was, fingertips on stone and metal, counting pulses and waiting for the world under Saint Merro to decide whether that had been one stray shudder or the start of something that wanted to keep repeating.

---

# N01 – Jhace Prose Voice & Line-Level Style Guide

**Scope:**  
This document defines the *scene-level* and *sentence-level* voice for **Jhace POV** in N01, matching the tone, detail, and rhythm of Chapter 1: “Something Wrong.”

Use this as the standard whenever writing or revising in Jhace’s tight third-person POV.

---

## 1. Core POV: How Jhace Thinks

**Mode:** Tight third-person limited. The narration only knows what Jhace knows, notices what he would notice, and cares about what he would care about.

**Jhace’s mental priorities:**

1. **Structure first.**
   - He reads the world as load paths, supports, failure points.
   - Everything filters through stability, risk, and maintenance.

2. **Sensation as data.**
   - Touch, vibration, pressure, weight, temperature, and sound are his primary channels.
   - Smell and sight are secondary and mostly used to confirm or refine what he already felt.

3. **Emotion = suppressed background process.**
   - Feelings are implied by behavior, choices, and sensory focus, not named directly.
   - He will think in terms of “risk,” “failure,” “tolerances,” not “fear” or “sadness.”

4. **History in the hands, not in exposition.**
   - Past events appear as *embedded associations*: “This bracket had given him trouble over the last few Tides,” not full flashback paragraphs.
   - The childhood collapse surfaces as specific details: **count of bodies, building collapse, two Torrins**, not vague trauma monologues.

---

## 2. Sentence Rhythm & Diction

**Overall texture:**  
Plain, concrete, workmanlike. No ornament. Rhythm varies between short, clipped sentences and slightly longer ones that stack observations in sequence.

### Sentence rules

- **Default:** Short to medium length.  
  - 7–18 words is typical.
- **Use longer sentences** only to:
  - Stack **sequential checks** (touch metal → check stone → listen).
  - Walk through a **decision with consequences**.
- **Avoid:** Long, looping, lyrical sentences that wander from the physical moment.

### Diction rules

- Verbs should be **physical, precise, and anchored in the environment:**
  - *set, brace, press, fix, notch, mark, settle, creep, slip, bow, carry, push, strain*.
- Nouns should be **material and specific:**
  - stone, pipe, bracket, brace, joint, hanger, seam, grit, dust, ledger.
- Abstract words are allowed when they arise from his job:
  - *load, tolerance, margin, pattern, anomaly, failure, risk*.

---

## 3. Sensory Hierarchy & Immersion

When entering or moving through a scene in Jhace POV, prioritize in this order:

1. **Vibration / Structure**
   - How the floor feels under his boots.
   - How the pipe feels under his hand.
   - How the wall carries or dampens sound.

2. **Temperature**
   - Heat from mains and risers.
   - Coolness of stone.
   - Shifts in temperature that signal load or flow changes.

3. **Sound**
   - Thrum, hiss, chatter, squeal, ring, tick.
   - He maps sound patterns to system health.

4. **Touch (non-structural)**
   - Sweat on skin, damp cloth, grit under fingers.

5. **Smell & Sight**
   - Used to confirm: forge smoke, stew, rust smell, discoloration on pipe.
   - Sight is for checking alignment, seams, moisture, cracks.

**Guideline:**  
Never describe a space in purely visual terms. Fold in at least **two non-visual senses** in every structural beat (touch + sound, heat + vibration, etc.).

---

## 4. Lexicon & Hard Constraints

These still apply:

- **No Earth unit systems.**
  - Use: Tide, Kindling, Quench, Turn, span, pace, grain, stone, Weight, etc.
- **No Earth-specific culture markers.**
  - No “sir” as generic honorific; use setting-appropriate forms (e.g. *Master Jhace*).
- **No metaphors/similes.**
  - No “like,” “as if,” “as though” to compare things.
  - No personification that implies thought/intent where there is none.
- **Te’Oga lexicon for time and work.**
  - Kindling / Pouring / Shaping / Striking / Quench where appropriate.
  - Use local work language: brace, marry point, Underface, Glass Quarter, etc.

---

## 5. Detail Level: What to Zoom In On

### Zoom in deeply when:

- Jhace is:
  - Checking a joint, marry point, bracket, or any failure-risk.
  - Sensing an anomaly in vibration or pattern.
  - Deciding whether to shut down a line.

**In these moments:**

- Break the action into **micro steps**:
  - Place palm → count pulses → compare to memory → cross-check via ear/stone → decide → log.
- Show his **mental checklist** as specific observations, not bullet-point thinking:
  - “No seep, no bow, temperature even, bracket seated.”

### Zoom out when:

- Moving between junctions.
- Covering routine checks without incident.
- Transitioning between Underface locations.

Use 1–2 summary sentences to skip uneventful spans, then drop back into detail at the next anomaly or decision point.

---

## 6. Internal Thought Style

**Jhace’s thoughts:**

- Tend to be **short, declarative, and functional.**
  - “Work first.”  
  - “That is enough.”  
  - “He did not trust it.”

- Prefer **negative decisions over direct emotions.**
  - Not: *He felt afraid.*  
  - Use: *He did not trust the quiet. The last time he had trusted it, twenty-three bodies had followed.*

- Reference the collapse with **numerical and structural detail**, not melodrama:
  - “Twenty-three bodies from that building. Two with his family name. He still carried the count.”

Avoid rhetorical questions unless they are strictly practical (“What if he did nothing?”).

---

## 7. Dialogue Style for Jhace

When Jhace speaks:

- Few words.
- Precise, mildly dry, sometimes grim.
- Often instructive or cautionary.

Examples:

- “Work first.”
- “Keep your weight off the grate.”
- “That is a bad story.”
- “You do not close a third of the Underface for a feeling you cannot prove. You do mark it.”

He does **not** explain his feelings. He explains **risks** and **outcomes**.

---

## 8. Anti-Repetition Strategy (Word Rotation)

We want to avoid distracting repetition without losing Jhace’s technical precision.

### 8.1 “Hum” and related terms

Use a **small controlled set** and rotate based on nuance:

- **hum** – baseline background sound of the grid.
- **thrum** – stronger, more insistent pulse, often closer to mains.
- **vibration** – general physical movement, especially under touch.
- **resonance** – when structural elements carry and shape the vibration.
- **pattern** – full combined sense of the tunnel’s behavior across senses.
- **pulse** – discrete beats/units he can count.

**Rule:**  
In any **short span (1–2 paragraphs)**, avoid repeating the *same* key noun or verb more than twice unless repetition is intentional for emphasis.

### 8.2 Anonymous “pipe/stone/wall/tunnel”

Rotate where natural but do not sacrifice clarity:

- Pipe: *pipe, line, main, branch, run.*
- Stone: *stone, wall, arch, cradle, bed.*
- Tunnel: *tunnel, run, span, level, section.*

Avoid forcing variation where it breaks technical accuracy. The grid is allowed to sound technical even when a word repeats across a page; just avoid tight clustering of obvious repetition.

---

## 9. Pattern Template for Jhace Scenes

For new Jhace scenes, you can follow this basic internal pattern:

1. **Anchor in environment via sensation**
   - 1–2 sentences: boots on stone, temperature, grid sound.

2. **Routine check**
   - He moves through a sequence of joints / braces / supports.
   - Sensory detail keyed to his hands, ears, bones.

3. **Deviation**
   - He notices an anomaly in **pattern** (vibration, beat, load distribution).
   - Describe the anomaly in structural terms, not mystical ones.

4. **Investigation**
   - Micro-actions: touch, ear to stone, tap test, visual check, count pulses.
   - Internal assessment: matching vs deviating from memory.

5. **Decision**
   - Log it / shut down / escalate / keep moving.
   - He thinks through **cost vs risk**, not feelings vs feelings.

6. **Exit note**
   - Environment settles or fails to settle.
   - A final sensory or structural oddity that pushes the story forward
     (e.g. a city-wide tremor that comes through everything at once).

---

If you want, you can literally label this file:

> **N01_Jhace_Prose_Voice_Guide.md**

and treat it as the authoritative micro-style reference whenever this GPT writes Jhace POV.

## Chapter 2: Glass That Listens

The sky over the Glass Quarter held steady blue when Tiffani stepped onto the observatory platform, but the instruments said otherwise.

She checked them in order. Always in order.

The mounted theodolite first: brass arcs polished, sighting tube clear, all three clamps tight. She nudged the central spindle with two fingertips and watched the pointer settle exactly on the etched baseline mark in the stone. No drift. Good.

The main logbook stand sat to her left, open to a fresh page. Yesterday’s columns lined the facing sheet in tight, precise script: phase, cloud bands, refraction spread, color shift, anomaly class. Three notations circled. She let her gaze rest on the circles only long enough to acknowledge them, then moved on.

The small prism racks at the platform’s edge waited next, each glass piece resting in its cradle. She lifted one, turned it against the light, checked for hairline fractures. None. The air tasted clean, kiln-smoke from the workshop below held to a faint trace.

Everything in place. Everything as it should be.

Except it wasn’t. That was the point.

She set the prism back and looked up. From this height the city stepped down toward the river in layered stone and roof-tile, Underface smoke rising in thin streams from chimneys. Above that, nothing but sky. No storm fronts, no strange cloud formations, no visible cause for the way starlight had started to misbehave in Pouring across the last few Anneals.

“Kindling, refraction pass,” she said under her breath. Saying it anchored her. “Baseline confirmation.”

She leaned into the theodolite, eye to the tube. The upper atmosphere cut into neat rings by the instrument’s crosshairs. She swept slowly, matching each fixed star mark to the etched scale, testing for deviation.

One subdivision high. Half a subdivision low. Within expected range. The sort of flicker she would once have called noise and left unmarked.

She did not leave it unmarked now.

Tiffani straightened, shifted her weight, and realized she had planted one heel on the carved low wall and the other on the stand’s base. Half-perched, half-balanced, nowhere near a normal stance. Pella teased her about that. She stepped down before anyone could come up the stair and see.

Ink. Record. Then doubt.

She dipped the nib, shook off the excess, and wrote in a narrow hand:

> Tide 0, Kindling mid-span. Upper rings nominal. Minor fluctuation at star-line Seven-Three. Repeat of last six Tides within ± one subdivision.  
> Pattern… persistent.

She paused on the last word. Persistent meant repeatable. Repeatable meant real. Real meant it could hurt someone.

And if it was real, why had no one else put it into their ledgers?

Because no one else was paid for this, she reminded herself. Because the guild assigned her here to test lens clarity, not watch the sky for ghosts.

Bootsteps on the stair broke the thought.

“Merrow?” A man’s voice. Too brisk to be Pella. “Merrow, you up there?”

She sighed, capped the ink, and turned. Two guild messengers shouldered through the hatch, pushing the heavy door wider than necessary. Their seals hung bright at their throats: glass shard emblems, polished far more than any working artisan’s would be.

“I am,” she said. “Do you need the ovens?”

“The ovens need you,” the older one answered. “Master Corlis wants to know when his commission panes will be cooled and measured. He says you promised them by Quench this Tide and you’re up here staring at clouds.”

“Not clouds,” Tiffani said. She kept her tone neutral. Flat ended in arguments. Light ended in dismissal. “Upper refraction patterns.”

“Upper what matters less than lower coin.” The younger messenger glanced at the instruments with the polite disinterest of someone looking at an unfamiliar tool they would never touch. “You were hired for precision glass, not who-knows-what with the sky.”

Tiffani folded one arm, resting her fingers on the edge of the logbook page so she wouldn’t tap. “The ovens are progressing within schedule. If the temperature holds within range for the next two spans, the panes can move to annealing before Pouring. I checked the gradients myself.”

“Then go check them again.” The older man spread his hands, showing a sort of weary patience. “The guild needs those panes installed. The Crown wants its new council windows before the cold settles. Your little side study can wait.”

A few Turns ago she would have apologized, closed the book, gone down without argument. She had done that with Marisse. The memory sat at the back of her throat whenever someone said “side study” in that tone.

“It isn’t a side study,” she said. “The pattern has repeated across more than a full set of Tides. The refraction shift is small but compounding. If it continues, it will affect long-range navigation lenses, signal mirrors, tower fires—”

“Has it broken anything yet?” the younger messenger asked.

“No.”

“Then it’s not our task yet.” He smiled, and the shape of it carried no malice. Just confident ignorance. “We work on what breaks. That’s how we keep people warm.”

Corlexi had once said something similar in a council debate. She had used the word “efficiency.” He had used “priorities.” She had left angrier than she’d admitted to anyone, then stayed late to adjust an instrument alignment instead of sleeping.

Tiffani watched the messengers for three breaths. They fidgeted. They wanted an answer they could carry back down and hand to someone else, clean and simple.

“The ovens are fine,” she said at last. “You may tell Master Corlis I will deliver the panes within the promised span range. If he wishes to complain about the exact count of breaths between now and then, he may climb up here and measure them himself.”

The older one huffed. The younger one blinked, then nearly smiled.

“Fine,” the older said. “Just remember who funds this platform.”

They thumped back down the stair, voices fading into the shop noises below. Tiffani exhaled slowly and turned back to the theodolite.

“Who funds is not the same as who needs,” she murmured. “People need the sky to behave.”

She lifted her gaze. The blue overhead looked unchanged. Clean, untroubled, empty. The sort of view that made people assume the world above them was simple.

Her hands moved without conscious instruction, adjusting the instrument’s angle to the exact mark she had circled yesterday. Seven-Three star-line. Outer band. There.

She leaned in. Sighted again.

The star should have met the crosshair at the same point it always did. It missed by more than a subdivision this time. Two and a half. She blinked, reset, checked for dust on the lens, checked for grit under the foot of the stand. The stone under it felt solid. No grit. No visible reason.

Her pulse climbed. Not fear yet. Anticipation. Mild dread wrapped in curiosity.

“Repeat,” she told herself. “If it repeats under controlled adjustment, it’s real. If it doesn’t, check for your own error first.”

She rotated the scope through a full sweep, then returned to the mark. The star sat high again, stubborn, out of place.

Tiffani wrote faster now.

> Deviation at Seven-Three increased to +2.5 subdivisions. Instrument base checked. No slippage.  
> Conclusion (tentative): source external.

She underlined tentative twice. A visible reminder. She would not leap to certainty just because she wanted the pattern to mean something.

The hatch door creaked again.

“Master Tiffani?” Pella’s head appeared, curls already frizzing from the kiln heat below. She held a covered bowl in one hand, the other gripping the rail. “You’ve been up here since early Kindling. I brought stew.”

“That was not necessary,” Tiffani said, which was different from saying she didn’t appreciate it.

Pella pushed the door wide with her hip and stepped carefully onto the platform, balancing the bowl. “You say that every Tide I bring you food. I have collected data. At this point it’s a predictable pattern.”

“Dangerous talk,” Tiffani said. “People who notice patterns get stuck apprenticed to impossible women.”

Pella smiled. “Everyone else downstairs thinks you’re impossible. I think they’re imprecise.” She glanced at the logbook. “Any exciting numbers?”

“Exciting isn’t usually the goal,” Tiffani said. She hesitated, then added, “But yes. The outer refraction ring is drifting. Not much. Enough to matter, eventually.”

Pella set the bowl on the stand’s lower shelf, careful not to touch any tools. “Matter how?”

“Signal clarity. Star charts. Anything that relies on fixed points.” Tiffani rubbed ink from her thumb with her other fingers. “If the drift compounds, navigators will misjudge distance. Mirror codes will smear. Lens arrays—”

“Could explode?” Pella’s eyes widened. “Or just be wrong?”

“Wrong is its own kind of explosion.” The words slipped out sharper than she intended. She softened them. “It may be nothing catastrophic. I don’t know yet. That’s why I’m watching.”

“Because no one else is,” Pella said quietly.

Tiffani did not answer that. She picked up the spoon, blew across the stew’s surface, and tasted. Warmth spread through her chest. Too salty, slightly over-thick, probably stretched from yesterday’s pot, but warm. She took another mouthful.

Pella watched her eat like someone checking off a task on a mental list.

“You can go back down,” Tiffani said once she’d swallowed. “I’ll be careful on the ladders.”

“I know,” Pella said. “You’re always careful. I’ll still come back before Pouring to make sure you’ve moved at least once.”

Tiffani made a vague gesture that could be taken as agreement. Pella laughed softly and slipped away, closing the hatch behind her.

Alone again, the sounds of the city rose: hammer on metal from the Underface forges, muted shouts from merchants in the nearer streets, the thick exhale of the main kiln below as it vented. All of it ordinary. All of it sitting under a sky that had started to disobey her measurements.

She crouched beside the theodolite and pressed her palm briefly to the stone where its feet anchored. Cool, steady, no tremor. The wrongness was not in the platform.

“Noise killed Marisse,” she said, barely audible. Naming it put shape to it. “Noise, or my refusal to treat it as signal.”

That oven’s wall had whispered for half an Anneal. Tiny shifts in heat. Tiny cracks that hadn’t yet shown at the surface. Marisse had asked if it worried her. Tiffani had said no. To reassure. To keep on schedule. To be efficient.

In the investigation notes they had called it material fatigue.

She called it the cost of deciding that a pattern did not matter.

Tiffani set her eye back to the instrument.

The sky had shifted again.

The star at Seven-Three sat even higher now, pulled out of alignment by something unseen. Not just that star. The next along the ring too. The distortion formed a curve through the upper band, as if something heavy had passed through and dragged light sideways in its wake.

She tracked along the curve, following the faint smearing of brightness. It led her toward the zenith, then beyond, where the instrument’s range ended.

Metal brushed her teeth. That was the first physical warning. A taste on her tongue, sharp and thin, with no source she could name. The hair on her arms tingled. Every glass surface around her picked up a faint, steady vibration: the prism racks, the spare lens plates, even the stand’s ink bottle. They all resonated together at a pitch just below hearing.

Tiffani stepped back from the scope, head tilting. She did not move fast. Fast wasted observation.

“New state,” she whispered. “Document, then move.”

Her hand shook once as she reached for the pen. Not fear, she told herself. Adrenaline. Excitement. The distinction didn’t matter to ink.

> Sudden spike. Uniform vibration in all glass on platform. Metallic taste present. Curve of refraction displacement moving across upper band. Source not visible to naked eye.

She set the pen down because the light changed.

A bright line burned across the upper air, so far above the highest tower that it cut no shadow on the buildings below. It bent as it moved, not in a simple path, but following the same curve her measurements had traced. The blue of the sky around it changed shade, slightly deeper along its path, as though the material of the world itself had thickened there.

No metaphor, she reminded herself. Describe what is.

White brightness. Sharp-edged. Moving faster than any cloudbank, faster than a flock’s scatter. The air around her pressed inward for a breath and then eased, leaving her lungs slow to catch up.

This was not noise. This was the opposite of noise.

The glass hum deepened. One of the prisms jittered in its cradle. Tiffani reached out and steadied it on reflex, fingers splaying to keep it from hopping free.

“Enough,” she said aloud. To the glass, to herself, to no one. “Seen enough. Move.”

She snatched the logbook shut, arms already planning the path down: hatch, ladder, narrow stair beside the kiln stack, side door onto the street. Corlis would complain, and the messengers would roll their eyes, and Corlexi would raise one precise brow if she presented this without ten more pages of confirmation.

None of that mattered if people were under a sky about to do something none of them had planned for.

Tiffani tucked the book under one arm, checked that the ink bottle would not spill, and ran for the hatch.

The metallic taste thickened as she descended. Somewhere beyond the roofs, beyond the edge of her instruments, the world had added a new variable. She did not know its name yet.

She only knew it would not be ignored.

---

# Chapter 2: Glass That Listens

Tiffani checked the instruments in order. Always in order.

The mounted theodolite first: brass arcs clean, sighting tube clear, clamps tight. She tapped the central spindle with two fingertips and watched the pointer settle exactly on the etched baseline mark in the stone. No drift.

The main logbook stand sat to her left, open to a fresh page. Yesterday’s columns lined the facing sheet in tight script: phase, cloud bands, refraction spread, color shift, anomaly class. Three notations circled. She noted them, then moved on.

Prism rack at the platform’s edge: each glass piece resting in its cradle. She lifted one, tilted it into the light, checked for hairlines. None. The air held only a faint trace of kiln-smoke from the workshop below. Nothing that should interfere with a clean reading.

Everything as it should be.

Except the numbers from the last few Tides said otherwise. That was why she was up here instead of in front of a furnace.

“Kindling, refraction pass,” she murmured. The words set the frame. “Baseline confirmation.”

She leaned into the theodolite, eye to the tube. The upper atmosphere sat in neat rings behind the crosshairs. She swept slowly, matching fixed star marks to the etched scale, checking for deviation.

A quarter subdivision high on one. Half low on the next. Within what she would once have called noise.

She did not call it noise anymore.

Tiffani straightened and reached for the logbook.

> Tide 0, Kindling mid-span. Upper rings nominal. Minor fluctuation at star-line Seven-Three. Repeat of last six Tides within ± one subdivision.  
> Pattern appears persistent.

She underlined persistent. Persistent meant repeatable. Repeatable meant real. Real meant it could affect something someone depended on.

If it was real, why hadn’t anyone else put it in their ledgers?

Because no one else was assigned to watch the sky for this. Because she was officially here to test lens clarity for Crown commissions, not chase atmospheric misbehavior.

Bootsteps on the stair cut through the thought.

“Merrow?” A man’s voice floated up. Too brisk to be Pella. “Merrow, you up there?”

“Yes,” she called. “Platform is occupied. Come through slowly; mind the stands.”

The hatch pushed open. Two guild messengers squeezed onto the observatory, seals bright at their throats. Their boots came far too close to the nearest tripod foot for her comfort.

“The ovens need you,” the older one said. “Master Corlis wants to know when his council panes will be cooled and measured. You promised by Quench. He says you’re up here staring at clouds.”

“Upper rings,” Tiffani said. She kept her tone level. “Refraction patterns. The ovens are within schedule. If temperature holds range, the panes move to annealing before Pouring.”

“Then go check them again.” The younger messenger’s gaze slid across the instruments with mild disinterest. “You were hired for precision glass, not sky-things.”

Sky-things. Efficient.

“It isn’t a side pastime,” Tiffani said. “The pattern has repeated across more than a full set of Tides. The refraction shift is small but compounding. If it continues, it will affect long-range lenses, signal mirrors, tower fires—”

“Has it broken anything yet?” the younger asked.

“No.”

“Then it isn’t our task yet,” the older said. He spread his hands in a patient gesture. “We work on what has already failed. That is what keeps people warm.”

Corlexi had said something similar, once, in a council session. Different words, same hierarchy: coin and visible damage first, unproven risk later. Preferably never.

The messengers watched her, waiting for a simple answer.

“The ovens are stable,” she said at last. “You may tell Master Corlis I will meet the promised span range. If he wishes to measure each breath between now and then, he can climb up and help.”

The younger almost smiled. The older huffed.

“Fine,” he said. “Just remember who pays for this platform.”

They left, boots heavy on the stair. When the hatch closed, the observatory felt cleaner by three heartbeats.

“Who pays isn’t the same as who needs,” she said under her breath. “Everyone needs the sky to behave.”

She turned back to the theodolite and set the angle back to Seven-Three. Outer band. The spot she had circled in her mind so often she could find it without checking the scale.

She leaned in. Sighted.

The star should have met the crosshair at the same point it always had. It missed by more than a subdivision this time. Two full marks, and a fraction.

She blinked. Reset. Checked for dust on the lens glass. None. Checked the stand’s feet: all three in firm contact with stone, no grit under the pads. No visible reason.

Her pulse climbed a little. Not fear. Not yet. Just the sense of a pattern moving faster than she’d projected.

“Repeat,” she told herself. “If it repeats under controlled adjustment, it’s real. If not, check for your own error first.”

She rotated the scope through a full sweep to clear her bias, then came back to Seven-Three. The star sat high again. Same offset.

Tiffani wrote, faster this time.

> Deviation at Seven-Three increased to +2 subdivisions. Instrument base confirmed stable.  
> Tentative conclusion: source external to instrument.

She underlined tentative twice. A visible reminder. She would not leap to certainty to justify her attention. That had cost enough once.

The hatch creaked again.

“Master Tiffani?” Pella’s head appeared, curls frizzed from kiln heat. She held a covered bowl in one hand, fingers tight on the rail with the other. “You’ve been up here since early Kindling. I brought stew.”

“That was not necessary,” Tiffani said. Which was not the same as unwelcome.

Pella edged onto the platform, careful of every stand. She had learned. “You say that every Tide. I have gathered data. It’s a stable pattern.”

“Dangerous habit,” Tiffani said. “People who notice patterns get assigned to impossible mentors.”

Pella smiled. “Everyone else downstairs says you’re impossible. I think they lack resolution.” Her gaze slid to the logbook. “Anything interesting?”

“Interesting isn’t usually the goal.” Tiffani hesitated. “But yes. The outer refraction ring is drifting. Slowly. Enough to matter for long-distance work if it keeps going.”

“Matter how?”

“Signal clarity. Star charts. Navigation lenses.” Tiffani flexed her ink-stained fingers. “If fixed points aren’t fixed, errors spread. Ships miss their targets. Beacon codes blur.”

Pella’s expression turned serious. “Are you going to tell the council?”

“When I have more than two points of drift and some glass humming,” Tiffani said. “For now, I watch. And eat stew, apparently.”

“You should start with the second,” Pella said. “Watching is easier if you don’t fall over.”

She set the bowl on the low shelf, careful not to touch the instruments. Tiffani took the spoon, tasted. Warm, too salty, clearly stretched from yesterday’s pot. Good enough. Heat spread through her chest.

“Thank you,” she said.

“You’re welcome.” Pella watched long enough to confirm at least three spoonfuls, then nodded. “I’ll come back before Pouring. If you have not moved, I will claim the right to complain louder than Master Corlis.”

“You already do,” Tiffani said.

Pella left with a grin, closing the hatch behind her.

Alone again, the city sounds rose: hammer on metal from Underface forges, market voices carrying from the streets, the deep exhale of the main kiln below as it vented. All ordinary.

She set the bowl aside and crouched to touch the stone where the theodolite feet anchored. Cool. Steady. No tremor.

The wrongness was not in the platform.

“Noise killed Marisse,” she said quietly. “Or my decision to call signal noise.”

That oven wall had whispered: small heat shifts, fine cracks not yet visible at the surface. Marisse had asked if it worried her. Tiffani had said no, to keep on schedule. Efficient. Brave, she’d thought at the time.

The investigation had labeled it material fatigue.

She called it the cost of pretending uncertainty meant safety.

Tiffani rose and brought her eye back to the scope.

The deviation had grown again.

Seven-Three sat higher. The next star along the ring also sat off-mark now, the two points forming the start of a curve through the upper band. When she traced along it, other stars near that implied path showed subtle smear—brightness stretched by a fraction of a division.

Something heavy in the upper air, bending light. Nothing visible to the naked eye.

Her teeth tasted of metal. That was new.

She stepped back from the scope. Her tongue picked up a sharp, thin tang with no source she could name. The hair on her forearms prickled. Glass around her picked up a faint vibration: prisms, spare lens plates, even the ink bottle. All resonated at a pitch that sat just below hearing.

“New state,” she whispered. “Document, then move.”

Her hand shook once as she took up the pen. She anchored her wrist against the stand to steady it.

> Sudden spike. Uniform vibration in all glass on platform. Metallic taste present. Curve of refraction displacement across upper band. Source not visible to eye.

She set the pen down because the light changed.

A bright line carved across the upper air, far above any tower. It did not fall straight; it followed the same curved path her measurements had suggested, cutting a narrow wound of white through the blue. The sky along that path darkened by a shade, as if some thickness had increased there.

No metaphor, she told herself. Describe only what you can confirm.

White. Moving fast. Upper band only. No contact with ground. No sound delay she could separate from city noise.

The glass vibration deepened. One prism hopped in its cradle. She caught it on reflex, palm over its top.

“Enough,” she said aloud. “Seen enough. Move.”

She snapped the logbook shut. Corlis, Corlexi, the messengers—all of them could argue about priorities later. Right now there was a new variable intersecting the world, and she was, as far as she knew, the only one with written evidence of its approach.

Tiffani tucked the book under her arm, checked that the ink bottle would not spill, and headed for the hatch.

The metallic taste thickened as she started down the narrow stair. Behind her, on the platform, every piece of glass still hummed.

Above Saint Merro, the sky had changed state. She did not know its name yet.

She only knew she would not call it noise.

Tiffani checked the instruments in order. Always in order.

Except the numbers from the last few Tides said otherwise. That was why she was here instead of in front of a furnace.

“Kindling, refraction pass,” she murmured. Saying it set the frame. “Baseline confirmation.”

She leaned to the theodolite, eye to the tube. The upper atmosphere sat in neat rings behind the crosshairs. She swept slowly, matching fixed stars to the etched scale, searching for deviation.

She did not call it noise now.

Tiffani straightened and reached for the logbook.

> Tide 0, Kindling mid-span. Upper rings nominal. Minor fluctuation at star-line Seven-Three. Repeat of last six Tides within ± one subdivision.  
> Pattern appears persistent.

Sky-things. Efficient.

“Has it broken anything yet?” the younger asked.

> Deviation at Seven-Three increased to +2 subdivisions. Instrument base confirmed stable.  
> Tentative conclusion: source external to instrument.

The hatch creaked again.

“Master Tiffani?” Pella’s head appeared, curls frizzed from kiln heat. A covered bowl sat balanced in one hand, the other white-knuckled on the rail. “You’ve been up here since early Kindling. I brought stew.”

“That was not necessary,” Tiffani said. It was as close as she let herself come to saying thank you before eating.

Pella eased onto the platform, placing her feet between stand legs with careful practice. “You say that every Tide. I have gathered evidence. It’s a stable pattern.”

“Dangerous habit,” Tiffani said. “People who notice patterns get tied to impossible mentors.”

“Useful isn’t usually the goal.” Tiffani hesitated. “But yes. The outer refraction ring is drifting. Slowly. Enough to matter for long-distance work if it continues.”

“Matter how?”

“Signal clarity. Star charts. Navigation lenses.” Tiffani flexed ink-stained fingers. “If fixed points slide, errors spread. Ships miss their bearings. Beacon codes blur.”

“Are you going to tell the council?” Pella asked.

“When I have more than two points of drift and some glass humming,” Tiffani said. “For now, I watch. And accept stew.”

“That last part improves all work.” Pella set the bowl on the lower shelf, careful not to touch any tools.

Tiffani took the spoon. The stew was warm, a little too salty, clearly stretched from yesterday’s pot. Heat spread through her chest and eased the tightness under her ribs.

“Thank you,” she said.

“You’re welcome.” Pella waited until Tiffani had swallowed three spoonfuls, then nodded, satisfied. “I’ll come back before Pouring. If you haven’t moved, I reserve the right to complain louder than Master Corlis.”

“You already do,” Tiffani said.

Pella left with a small grin, closing the hatch.

Alone again, city sound rose: hammer on metal from Underface forges, distant market calls, the deep exhale of the main kiln below as it vented. All ordinary.

She set the bowl aside and crouched to press her palm to the stone under the theodolite feet. Cool. Steady. No tremor.

The wrongness was not in the platform.

“Noise killed Marisse,” she said quietly. “Or my choice to call signal noise.”

That oven wall had whispered for half an Anneal: small heat shifts, fine cracks still buried under glaze. Marisse had asked if it worried her. Tiffani had said no, to keep schedule, to sound confident.

The investigation named it material fatigue.

She named it the cost of assuming uncertainty meant safety.

Tiffani stood and returned her eye to the scope.

The deviation had grown again.

Seven-Three sat higher. The next star along the ring had shifted too. Together they marked the start of a curve through the upper band. When she traced along that implied path, other stars near it showed slight smear. Brightness stretched by fractions.

Something was pulling at the upper light. Nothing visible to the naked eye.

Her teeth tasted of metal.

She stepped back from the scope. A thin, sharp tang sat on her tongue with no source she could name. The hair on her forearms prickled. Glass around her picked up a faint vibration: prisms, spare lens plates, even the ink bottle. All resonated at a pitch just below hearing.

“New state,” she whispered. “Document, then move.”

Her hand shook once as she lifted the pen. She braced her wrist on the stand.

> Sudden spike. Uniform vibration in all glass on platform. Metallic taste present. Curve of refraction displacement across upper band. Source not visible to eye.

She set the pen down.

A bright line appeared across the upper air, far above any tower. It did not travel straight; it followed the same curved path her measurements had suggested, cutting a narrow white band through the blue. Along that band, the sky’s color deepened by a shade.

She watched its motion in small, fixed steps. White. Fast. Upper band only. No shadow reaching the buildings below. No clear sound she could pull apart from city noise.

The vibration in the glass deepened. One prism hopped in its cradle. She caught it with her palm, steadying it until the motion eased.

“Enough,” she said aloud. “Seen enough. Move.”

She snapped the logbook shut. Corlis, Corlexi, the messengers—none of their complaints mattered as much as getting this into the right hands before dismissal settled in around it.

Tiffani tucked the book under her arm, checked that the ink bottle would not spill, and went for the hatch.

The metallic taste thickened as she started down the narrow stair. Above, on the empty platform, the glass still hummed.

Over Saint Merro, the sky had changed state. She did not know its name yet.

She knew only that she would not call it noise.
