> **N01_Tiffani_Prose_Voice_Guide.md**

---

# N01 – Tiffani Prose Voice & Line-Level Style Guide

**Scope:**  
Defines scene- and sentence-level voice for **Tiffani POV** in N01, using Chapter 2 (“Glass That Listens”) as the baseline.

Use this whenever writing or revising any tight-third scene from Tiffani’s perspective.

---

## 1. Core POV: How Tiffani Thinks

**Mode:** Tight third-person limited. Narration is aligned to Tiffani’s mind: what she notices, how she frames it, and how she reasons.

**Her mental priorities:**

1. **Patterns and deviations.**
   - The world presents as data: measurements, trends, variance from expected behavior.
   - She’s always asking: *Is this within range? Is this noise or signal?*

2. **System implications.**
   - She leaps from a small anomaly to its possible downstream effects.
   - Thoughts often move: *observation → measurement → consequence*.

3. **Self-audited thinking.**
   - She questions her own conclusions in real time.
   - She labels states: *tentative*, *baseline*, *pattern*, *noise*.

4. **Emotion behind control.**
   - Care and fear are present but wrapped inside procedure and analysis.
   - She rarely names feelings directly; she describes their physical footprint and impact on reasoning.

---

## 2. Sentence Rhythm & Diction

**Overall texture:**  
Analytical, layered, more verbal than Jhace but still lean. Sentences can run longer, especially where she stacks conditions or tracks causality.

### Sentence rules

- **Default length:** Medium; 10–22 words is common.
- **Short sentences** for:
  - Conclusions: “She did not call it noise now.”
  - Observed facts: “Her teeth tasted of metal.”
- **Long sentences** for:
  - Describing procedures.
  - Walking through cause and effect.
  - Mapping patterns and implications.

### Diction rules

- Core vocabulary is **measurement and classification**:
  - *phase, refraction, deviation, subdivision, baseline, pattern, noise, drift, range, stability, gradient, source, state, external, internal, compounding.*
- Verbs tend to be **cognitive or technical** rather than purely physical:
  - *check, confirm, measure, compare, project, classify, adjust, repeat, record, conclude.*
- Physical verbs remain simple and clear:
  - *lean, tap, press, steady, tilt, set, lift.*

Avoid poetic or decorative phrasing. Her language is clear and precise rather than ornate.

---

## 3. Sensory Hierarchy & Immersion

Tiffani is less body-first than Jhace, but still strongly sensory, especially around **glass** and **light**.

Priority for description:

1. **Visual / optical**
   - Star positions, arcs, rings, curves, lines, brightness, smear, color shift.
   - Instrument status: lens clarity, hairlines in glass, alignment, scale marks.

2. **Measurement / abstract sense**
   - Subdivision counts, ranges, variance, repetition over Tides.
   - She pairs sensory input with a mental number or range: “within ± one subdivision.”

3. **Kinesthetic / touch**
   - Hands on stone, instruments, glass.
   - Physical confirmation: stand feet on stone, lack of grit, coolness/warmth.

4. **Taste / internal sensation**
   - Metallic taste as an early warning.
   - Mouth, tongue, heartbeat, tension.

5. **Sound**
   - Humming glass, kiln exhale, city noise; used more as context and escalation than baseline.

**Guideline:**  
In important beats, pair the sensory detail with a **measurement concept** (e.g., “Two subdivisions high and holding,” “small but compounding shift”).

---

## 4. Lexicon & Global Constraints

Same world constraints apply:

- **No Earth units or references.**
  - Use: Tide, Kindling, Pouring, Quench, Anneal, spans, etc.
- **No metaphors or similes.**
  - No “like,” “as if,” “as though” for comparisons.
  - No figurative comparisons at all; describe observed properties directly.
- **No strong personification.**
  - Avoid language that gives intent to inanimate things.
  - Acceptable: “The kiln vented,” “The glass vibrated.”  
  - Avoid: “The sky tried to hide something,” “The oven begged to be heard.”

Use Te’Oga-specific social and work language: guild, Crown, Glass Quarter, Underface, council, ovens, platforms, Anneals, etc.

---

## 5. Detail Level: Where to Zoom In

### Zoom in deeply when:

- Tiffani is:
  - Running a **measurement sequence**.
  - Identifying a **pattern** or a **break in pattern**.
  - Recalling Marisse’s accident and its methodological implications.
  - Interpreting the sky or glass behavior.

**Techniques when zoomed in:**

- Break the procedure into named steps:
  - Check instrument → confirm baseline → record deviation → label conclusion (tentative/final).
- Explicitly mark logic:
  - *“If it repeats under controlled adjustment, it’s real. If not, look for your own error first.”*

### Zoom out when:

- She traverses physical space without new information (stairs, corridors).
- Routine work proceeds without anomaly.
- Interactions with others are simple confirmations or status reports.

Use a few clean sentences to summarize and jump to the next point where data changes.

---

## 6. Internal Thought Style

Tiffani’s internal thoughts:

- Often in **short, declarative fragments**, used to label or correct.
  - “Not fear. Yet.”
  - “Sky-things. Efficient.”
- Frequently **self-instructive**:
  - “Repeat. If it repeats, it’s real.”
  - “Document, then move.”
- Emotion appears as **diagnosed state**:
  - She may note pulse, tension, shaking hands, then classify it: *adrenaline, anticipation*.

She references Marisse’s death in **methodological** terms:

- “Noise killed Marisse. Or my choice to call signal noise.”
- “The investigation named it material fatigue. She named it the cost of assuming uncertainty meant safety.”

Avoid indulgent grief monologues. Her guilt is real but channeled into stricter process.

---

## 7. Dialogue Style for Tiffani

When Tiffani speaks:

- More words than Jhace, but still efficient.
- Often clarifying or reframing:
  - “It isn’t a pastime. The pattern has repeated across more than a full set of Tides.”
- Uses **conditional and consequence language**:
  - “If it continues, it will affect long-range lenses, signal mirrors, tower fires.”
- Slight dry humor when she feels safe:
  - “People who notice patterns get tied to impossible mentors.”
  - “Watching is easier if you don’t fall over.”

She avoids direct emotional admissions, especially to authority. With Pella, the edges soften but stay controlled.

---

## 8. Anti-Repetition Strategy

We want to keep her technical language consistent, but avoid distracting repetition in tight spans.

### 8.1 Core measurement terms

These form a **small controlled set**:

- *noise, signal, pattern, drift, deviation, shift, range, baseline, anomaly, state, source, external, internal, compounding, persistent.*

**Rules:**

- Within a **single paragraph**, avoid repeating the *exact same* keyword more than twice unless it’s deliberate emphasis (e.g., everything revolves around *noise vs signal* in a short burst).
- Over a scene, allow consistent reuse of key terms; she is a specialist with a tight lexicon.

### 8.2 Instrument terms

Keep technical clarity first; vary only where it doesn’t confuse:

- Theodolite: *instrument, scope, tube, stand* depending on context.
- Glass: *prism, pane, lens, plate, glass*.
- Logging: *logbook, record, entry, column.*

Don’t force synonyms that weaken the sense of a professional, repeatable process.

---

## 9. Tiffani Scene Template

For most Tiffani scenes, you can loosely follow this pattern:

1. **Instrument / environment check**
   - She runs through tools or conditions in a defined order.
   - Establish baseline: what “normal” looks like.

2. **Initial anomaly**
   - Small deviation in measurement.
   - She notes it, records it, labels it (tentative, minor, within range).

3. **Pattern recognition**
   - She compares to previous Tides or entries.
   - “Pattern appears persistent” moment.

4. **Social friction**
   - Someone interrupts: guild messenger, council voice, Pella.
   - Conflict between **“urgent visible tasks”** and her **“invisible risk”** concerns.

5. **Escalation of anomaly**
   - Deviation increases; new signals appear (glass vibration, taste, visual changes).
   - She re-tests, re-validates instrument, rules out her own error.

6. **Trauma echo / methodological vow**
   - Brief, concrete reference to Marisse and what she misclassified.
   - Recommitment: *will not call this noise.*

7. **Decision**
   - Document the state.
   - Choose who to tell or where to go next.
   - Small physical exit beat (moving toward hatch, stair, council, etc.)

---

## 10. Quick “Voice Check” Phrases

If a passage feels off, check against these example lines:

- “She did not call it noise now.”
- “Persistent meant repeatable. Repeatable meant real. Real meant it could hurt someone.”
- “If it repeats under controlled adjustment, it’s real. If not, look for your own error first.”
- “Noise killed Marisse. Or her decision to call signal noise.”
- “When I have more than two points of drift and some glass humming, I’ll speak to the council.”

If the prose could naturally accommodate lines like these without clashing, you’re in the right voice space for Tiffani.

---

If you want, next step we can do a similar micro-guide for **Bram** so all three main POVs have locked-in production style docs.

# Chapter 1: Something Wrong (revised)

Jhace eased down the last rung and set both boots on the damp stone ledge. The Underface took the sharp tap of his landing and returned the steady thrum of the heatgrid lines running through the dark.

He stood still until his breath slowed. Lantern hooded low at his belt, he reached for the nearest pipe. Not for warmth. For vibration. Skin to metal. Bone to hum.

Heat pushed along the line from upslope into the older districts. The pattern told him how the city held itself: a stable thrum when joints were tight, a roughness when a hanger had slipped, a light chatter when sediment started to collect and knock along the flow.

This run held steady.

For the moment.

Jhace moved onto his practiced path, keeping the lantern’s light tight to his feet. The tunnel pressed narrow. Mains at thigh and chest. Smaller lines bent close against stone. Iron braces sunk deep to fix it all in place. Moisture beaded under the pipes and fell in regular taps, dotting the floor.

He checked the marry points in order. At each one he set fingers on metal first, then on the stone cradle, then on the brace that bit into the wall. Each contact gave him an answer and an older echo: how the joint had felt last Tide, how the pipe had settled after the last patch, how much slack the bracket allowed before it turned from tolerance into danger.

The ledger at his belt held the marks. His hands held the rest.

A voice drifted down from above, muffled through grates and packed dirt.

“Jhace!” Bram’s tone carried a grin even with half the sound stripped by distance. “You still breathing down there, or you finally decided to marry the grid and settle?”

Jhace didn’t look up. He moved his hand along the curve of a support ring, checking for any shift at the seams.

“Work first,” he called back.

Another voice followed, higher, eager. Chloen. “Master Kelter says you can tell a cracked pipe by the way the air tastes.”

Jhace paused with his palm on a brace. Through stone he could hear them moving topside: boots on planks, the drag of a tool box. Bram would be leaning over the grate to aim his voice downward. Chloen would be leaning farther than she should.

“Your master says many things,” Jhace answered. “Keep your weight off the grate.”

A heartbeat’s pause. “Yes, Master Jhace,” Chloen said, quick and proud.

Jhace moved on. He didn’t bring apprentices into the Underface. Not because they were useless. Because they were alive. Down here, mistakes ended in bodies. The tunnels kept those.[3]

The route took him through three marry points on this level, then down a short drop to a lower run that bent river-side. He logged a patch where a hanger bolt had started to creep, marked two joints as stable, and listened long at an old bracket that had given him trouble over the last few Tides.

It stayed quiet.

He still didn’t trust it.[3]

At the sixth junction—a simple split where a branch line peeled off toward the Glass Quarter—everything matched his memory. He ran fingers along the pipe and felt the centered vibration he expected there. No extra heat. No stray shudder.

At the seventh—a broader marry point where three branch lines split from the main—the pattern shifted.

Not a crack. Not the high squeal of metal pulling away from stone. The pipe looked clean where his lantern light reached: no wet line along a seam, no rust bloom eating into a hanger, no visible bow where the metal wanted to sag free.

But the vibration under his fingertips wasn’t centered. It rode close to the outer edge of the pipe wall, as if the load had slipped toward one side and stayed there.

He swallowed, then bent and pressed his ear near the brace. Stone cooled his cheek. The sound came back wrong again, just enough to catch: the beat at the brace mismatched the beat at the pipe by a fraction, a faint doubled rhythm that belonged on his bad list.

A join starting to lose agreement with the wall.

He straightened and pulled his ledger from inside his coat, balancing it against his knee. The page was crowded with tight marks: replacements he’d argued for and won, patches he’d argued against and lost, notes on spans where stone had begun to sweat with stress.

He added this junction without hesitation. Ink scratched the marry point number, the location, the note on vibration.

The decision carried weight. Shutting down a section of line in Kindling meant cold rooms by Quench. Complaints. A foreman with a polished collar telling him the grid had survived worse for longer. Bram would back him in public and tease him in private. Chloen would watch all of it and learn something. Jhace did not want that something to be that sound could be ignored.

He capped the ink. The brace sat where the plans said it should. He ran his thumb along the seam where brace met pipe, feeling for slip or lip. Nothing moved. The wrongness stayed, thin and constant under the regular thrum of flow.

He could walk on and tell himself he would check it again next Tide.

He had done that once.[3]

Turns ago, he had chosen convenience. He had told himself a sound was only sound, that stone always settled, that the tunnel would still be there when he came back through. A small hiss. Harmless.[3]

A Tide later, a building above that run had failed. Twenty-three bodies pulled from rubble and broken pipe. Two with his family name. He carried the list through every inspection since.[3]

He still carried all twenty-three names. Even if he never spoke them.[3]

Jhace slid the ledger back into his coat. He put his hand one more time on the pipe, counted ten clean pulses, listened for change, heard none.

The anomaly remained. That was enough.

He turned and started back toward the ladder. He would log the fault, pull the section, and let the foreman shout himself empty. Better that than another list of names.[3]

The Underface vibration settled back into its usual pattern for three junctions. He moved past each one, checking by touch, confirming they still matched the memory in his hands. The wrong note stayed behind him.

Until it didn’t.

Without warning, the entire tunnel gave a small tremor that didn’t belong to any single line. It came through stone and metal together, shallow at first but wide. The lantern hook on his belt swung. Loose grit rattled out from a crack in the arch above and dusted his shoulders.

Jhace stopped hard enough that his knees took the jolt. His free hand went to the wall. The pipe under his other palm held steady, the regular thrum unchanged. The stone felt settled.

His bones remembered the ripple anyway.

That pulse hadn’t come from his junction. It hadn’t come from a single branch or one district. It had moved through everything at once—the wall, the floor, the brackets, the pipe—carrying the same off-beat for the space of a breath.

Then it was gone.

He stood still, listening, breath held tight in his chest. The grid noise went on: hot flow through copper, distant hiss of expansion at far bends, the minor rattles that belonged to old work. Nothing else repeated the tremor.

From above, faint through grate and dirt, Bram laughed at something Chloen said. The sound carried down with no change in it at all.

Jhace stayed where he was, fingertips on stone and metal, counting pulses and waiting for the world under Saint Merro to decide whether that had been one stray shudder or the start of something that wanted to keep repeating.

# Chapter 2: Glass That Listens (revised)

Tiffani checked the instruments in order. Always in order.

The mounted theodolite first: brass arcs clean, sighting tube clear, clamps tight. She tapped the central spindle with two fingertips and watched the pointer settle on the etched baseline mark in the stone. No drift.

The logbook stand sat to her left, open to a fresh page. The facing sheet held tight script from the last several Tides: phase, cloud bands, refraction spread, color shift, anomaly class. Three entries were circled. She noted them without letting her attention stick.

Prism rack at the platform’s edge: each glass piece resting in its cradle. She lifted one, tilted it into the light, checked for hairlines and ripples. None. The air held a faint trace of kiln-smoke from the workshop below. Everything where it belonged.

Except the numbers said otherwise.

“Kindling, refraction pass,” she murmured. “Baseline confirmation.”

She leaned to the theodolite, eye to the tube. The upper atmosphere sat in neat rings behind the crosshairs. She swept slowly, matching fixed stars to the etched scale, searching for deviation. A quarter subdivision high. Half low.

Once, she would have called that noise and moved on. She did not call it noise now.[3]

Tiffani straightened and reached for the logbook.

Tide 0, Kindling mid-span. Upper rings nominal. Minor fluctuation at star-line Seven-Three. Repeat of last six Tides within one subdivision.

Pattern appears persistent.

She underlined persistent. Persistent meant repeatable. Repeatable meant real. Real meant it could reach a person who depended on glass to be honest.[3]

If it was real, why hadn’t anyone else written it down? Because no one else was assigned to sit on this platform and count these shifts. Because the guild said she was here to test lens clarity for Crown commissions, not watch the sky for trouble.

Bootsteps on the stair broke the thought.

“Merrow?” A man’s voice rose through the hatch. Too brisk to be Pella. “Merrow, you up there?”

“Yes,” she called. “Platform is in use. Come through carefully; mind the stands.”

The hatch pushed open. Two guild messengers edged onto the observatory, seals bright at their throats. Their boots landed too close to a tripod foot.

“The ovens need you,” the older one said. “Master Corlis wants to know when his council panes will be cooled and measured. Promised by Quench, he says. You’re up here staring at clouds.”

“Upper rings,” Tiffani said. Her tone stayed level. “Refraction patterns. The ovens are within schedule. If temperature holds range, the panes move to annealing before Pouring.”

“Then go check them again.” The younger messenger glanced over the instruments with quick, shallow interest. “You were hired for precision glass, not sky work.”

Efficient phrasing. Unhelpful.

“It isn’t a pastime,” Tiffani said. “The pattern has repeated across more than a full set of Tides. The refraction shift is small and compounding. If it continues, it will affect long-range lenses, signal mirrors, tower fires—”

“Has it broken anything yet?” the younger asked.

“No.”

“Then it isn’t our task yet,” the older said. His hands spread in a weary, reasonable gesture. “We work on what has already failed. That is what keeps people warm.”

Corlexi had said something close to that once in council: priorities, efficiency, visible damage first. She had gone home afterward and realigned half the workshop’s instruments instead of sleeping.

The messengers watched her, waiting for something simple.

“The ovens are stable,” she said. “Tell Master Corlis I will meet the promised span range. If he wishes to count each breath between now and then, he can climb up and help.”[1]

The younger almost smiled. The older huffed.

“Fine,” he said. “Just remember who pays for this platform.”

They left, boots heavy on the stair.

When the hatch closed, the air on the platform felt easier to pull in.

“Who pays isn’t the same as who needs,” she said under her breath. “Everyone needs the sky to behave.”

She turned back to the theodolite and set the angle to Seven-Three. Outer band. The spot she had circled enough times that her hands could find it without checking the scale.

She leaned in. Sighted. The star should have met the crosshair where it had met it every prior pass.

It missed by more than a subdivision this time. Two full marks and a fraction.

She blinked. Reset. Checked the lens: no dust, no smear. Checked the stand’s feet: all three flat on stone, no grit under the pads. Nothing loose.

Her pulse picked up. Not fear. Not yet. A pattern gaining speed.[3]

“Repeat,” she told herself. “If it repeats under controlled adjustment, it’s real. If not, find your own error first.”[3]

She rotated the scope through a full sweep, then came back to Seven-Three. High again. Same offset.

She wrote with more pressure than before.

Deviation at Seven-Three increased to +2 subdivisions. Instrument base confirmed stable.

Tentative conclusion: source external to instrument.

She underlined tentative twice. A reminder not to run ahead of the data. That habit had a cost.[3]

The hatch creaked again.

“Master Tiffani?” Pella’s head appeared, curls frizzed from kiln heat. A covered bowl sat balanced in one hand, the other tight on the rail.

“You’ve been up here since Kindling opened. I brought stew.”[1]

“That was not necessary,” Tiffani said.

Pella eased onto the platform, placing her feet between stand legs with practiced care. “You say that every Tide. I have gathered evidence. It’s a stable pattern.”

“Dangerous habit,” Tiffani said. “People who notice patterns get tied to impossible mentors.”[3]

“Everyone downstairs says you’re impossible,” Pella said. “I think they lack resolution.” Her gaze dipped to the logbook. “Anything useful?”

“Useful isn’t usually the goal.” Tiffani hesitated. “But yes. The outer refraction ring is drifting. Slowly. Enough to matter for long-distance work if it continues.”

“Matter how?”

“Signal clarity. Star charts. Navigation lenses.” Tiffani flexed ink-stained fingers. “If fixed points slide, errors spread. Ships miss their bearings. Beacon codes blur.”

“Are you going to tell the council?” Pella asked.

“When I have more than two points of drift and some glass humming,” Tiffani said. “For now, I watch. And accept stew.”

“That last part improves all work.”

Pella set the bowl on the lower shelf, careful not to touch any tools. Tiffani took the spoon. The stew was warm, a little too salty, stretched from yesterday’s pot. Heat spread through her chest and eased the tightness under her ribs.

“Thank you,” she said.

“You’re welcome.” Pella waited until Tiffani had swallowed three spoonfuls, then nodded, satisfied. “I’ll come back before Pouring. If you haven’t moved, I reserve the right to complain louder than Master Corlis.”

“You already do,” Tiffani said.

Pella left with a small grin, closing the hatch.

Alone again, the city sound rose: hammer on metal from Underface forges, distant market calls, the deep exhale of the main kiln below as it vented. All ordinary.

Tiffani set the bowl aside and crouched to press her palm to the stone under the theodolite feet. Cool. Steady. No tremor. The wrongness wasn’t in the platform.

“Noise killed Marisse,” she said quietly. “Or my choice to call signal noise.”[3]

That oven wall had whispered for half an Anneal: small heat shifts, fine cracks still buried under glaze. Marisse had asked if it worried her. Tiffani had said no, to keep schedule, to sound certain. The investigation had named it material fatigue. Tiffani had named it the cost of assuming uncertainty meant safety.[3]

She stood and returned her eye to the scope. The deviation had grown again. Seven-Three sat higher. The next star along the ring had shifted too. Together they marked the start of a curve through the upper band. When she traced along that implied path, other stars near it showed slight smear. Brightness stretched by fractions.

Something was pulling at the upper light. Nothing she could see without glass.

Her teeth tasted of metal. She stepped back from the scope. A thin tang sat on her tongue with no source she could name. The hair on her forearms prickled.

Glass around her took on a faint vibration: prisms, spare lens plates, even the ink bottle. The pitch sat just below hearing.

“New condition,” she whispered. “Write. Then move.”[1]

Her hand shook once as she lifted the pen. She braced her wrist on the stand.

Sudden spike. Uniform vibration in all glass on platform. Metallic taste present. Curve of refraction displacement across upper band. Source not visible to eye.

She set the pen down.

A bright line cut across the upper air, far above any tower. It did not travel straight; it followed the curve her marks had suggested, a narrow white band through blue. Along that band, the sky’s color deepened by a shade.

She watched its motion in fixed steps. White. Fast. Upper band only. No shadow reaching the buildings below. No sound she could separate from the city’s noise.

The vibration in the glass deepened. One prism hopped in its cradle. She caught it with her palm and held it down until the motion eased.

“Enough,” she said aloud. “Seen enough.”

She snapped the logbook shut, tucked it under her arm, checked the ink bottle, then went for the hatch. The metallic taste thickened as she started down the narrow stair.

Above, on the empty platform, the glass still hummed. Over Saint Merro, the sky had changed. She did not have a name for it. She had the marks. She had the taste. She had the decision not to dismiss it.[3]

She did not call it noise.[3]

Nice, this pass is *really* snug to the constraints. You’re basically there; I just see a couple of tiny spots that still break our “no similes / no personification” rule.

## Chapter 1 – tiny fixes

### 1. Remove the “as if” simile

**Current:**

> But the vibration under his fingertips wasn’t centered. It rode close to the outer edge of the pipe wall, **as if** the load had slipped toward one side and stayed there.

**Suggested:**

> But the vibration under his fingertips wasn’t centered. It rode close to the outer edge of the pipe wall, the way it felt when load had slipped toward one side and stayed there.

(Still a comparison, but now it’s a *known physical case* rather than a simile construction.)

---

### 2. Remove “wanted to sag free” personification

**Current:**

> ...no rust bloom eating into a hanger, no visible bow where the metal wanted to sag free.

**Suggested:**

> ...no rust bloom eating into a hanger, no visible bow where the metal had started to sag away from true.

---

### 3. Remove “wanted to keep repeating” personification

**Current:**

> ...or the start of something that **wanted** to keep repeating.

**Suggested:**

> ...or the start of a pattern that might keep repeating.

---

## Chapter 2 – tiny fixes

### 4. Remove “whispered” from the oven wall (personification)

**Current:**

> That oven wall had **whispered** for half an Anneal: small heat shifts, fine cracks still buried under glaze.

**Suggested:**

> That oven wall had changed in small ways for half an Anneal: slight heat shifts, fine cracks still buried under glaze.

(or, if you want a bit more edge:)

> That oven wall had shown small warnings for half an Anneal: slight heat shifts, fine cracks still buried under glaze.

Both stay literal and physical.

---

With those four micro-edits, both chapters are:

- Fully lexicon-safe
- Free of similes and strong personification
- Locked into Jhace-body-first / Tiffani-analytical voices

# Chapter 3: The Sky Breaks  

The wall moved before Jhace did.

Stone shifted under his palm, a deep grinding that did not belong. The tunnel’s usual thrum broke into a jagged pulse. The pipe beside him threw heat sideways, hard enough that his fingers slipped.

Then the ground hit.

The whole Underface lurched. Jhace slammed into the wall. His lamp broke free and swung wild on its hook. Bolts shrieked in their brackets. Somewhere upslope a main line roared as pressure slammed through it, a raw tearing sound that cut through bone.

“Off the ladders!” he shouted. “Now!”

Dust exploded from the arch above, a sudden blind cloud. The tunnel narrowed to sound and impact: stone cracking, metal twisting, a rush of falling grit that stung his eyes. He tasted old ash and something new under it, a sharp tang on his tongue.

“Chloen!” Bram’s voice answered from the ladder shaft. “Move!”

Jhace staggered toward the shaft by habit, one hand on the wall to keep direction straight. The usual grid vibration had turned uneven, rolling in waves. A brace ahead of him groaned. He wrapped both arms around it and braced his feet.

The first wave passed. The tunnel did not settle.

Another shudder rolled through. The arch above the shaft gave a hard, hollow crack.

“Off!” Jhace bellowed. “Both of you, off that metal!”

Boots hit stone. A heartbeat later the ladder’s top anchor ripped out of the arch. Iron rungs slammed against the wall, sparks flaring where they struck pipe. The whole frame came down in a rattle of metal, choking the shaft with noise.

Something heavier followed.

A cross-beam from the upper structure tore loose and dropped through the open shaft mouth. Jhace saw only its shadow in the dust. It hit the tunnel floor with a wet, wrong sound and a short scream cut clean in half.

The dust settled enough to show Chloen under the beam.

It had caught her across one leg just above the knee. The rest of her lay clear. Her hands clawed at useless floor. She dragged in air, then let it out in a hoarse sound that barely reached words.

“Jh—” She bit it off. Breath shook in her chest.

Bram skidded across the stone, dropped to his knees at her side. Blood already seeped under the beam’s edge, dark against the damp floor.

Jhace reached the fallen timber in three strides. It had been a support in the shaft’s upper frame, thick as his chest, one end splintered, the other still buried in the ceiling rubble. The weight sat full on Chloen’s leg.

He planted feet, set both hands under the beam’s edge, and heaved.

Nothing moved.

Muscle in his back lit with pain. The beam gave no answer. The arch above answered instead: another cracking line, the soft crumble of stone. If he kept forcing, he would bring the rest of the shaft down on all three of them.

“Jhace,” Bram snapped. “Stop. You’ll bring the whole thing.”

Chloen’s fingers dug into the floor. She wasn’t making sound now. Her face had gone gray, lips pulled back from her teeth.

“How bad?” Jhace asked.

“Crushed,” Bram said. He didn’t waste words on comfort. “We get this off in the next few breaths, she might keep the limb. We fail, she bleeds out or the bone cuts through. Or it all comes down. Good choices.”

Another tremor rolled through the tunnel. Smaller this time, but enough to rattle loose grit from the arch. The beam bounced once, then settled heavier.

Chloen bit down on her sleeve. Her eyes found Jhace’s. There was fear there, and trust she had no business giving him.

He saw another shaft in his memory. Smaller brace. Smaller him. Quiet hiss he had called harmless. Twenty-three bodies later.

Not again.

“Bram,” he said. “Pull when I tell you. Straight back. Keep her spine clear.”

“Jhace—”

“Now or we lose her,” Jhace said. He set the words flat. “Ready.”

Bram moved without further argument. He slid his arms under Chloen’s shoulders and upper body, planting his own boots wide.

Jhace spat dust out, wiped his palms once on his trousers, and set his hands on the beam again. This time he did not try to lift yet. He let his skin settle to the rough wood, feeling the indentation where impact had crushed fibers. He put one knee down to lower his center. The old grid thrum still ran under everything, but a new pulse rode on top of it, sharp and irregular.

He focused on the weight on that one point.

Hold. Just long enough. Do not move the world, only keep this from dropping.

Heat built in his palms. At first he thought it was friction from his own grip, then it sharpened, sinking through skin into bone. The pressure under his hands changed character, no longer simple mass but something pushing back at him.

Copper-gold light spilled out around his fingers.

Jhace flinched, but he did not pull away. The light came from his own skin, thin streams running into the wood grain and along it. It spread into the shattered end, then down into the stone on either side, tracing a pattern he did not understand. The beam under his hands vibrated with it, a high, tight resonance inside the deeper shake of the tunnel.

His arms locked. Every part of him from shoulders to jaw clenched. Breath left his lungs and did not come back clean.

“Now,” he ground out.

Bram hauled. The beam did not rise much. It did lighten. Jhace felt the load shift through his hands, through his forearms, into his chest, then out again. Sweat ran into his eyes. His teeth ached. For a few breaths the world narrowed to the hot line where his flesh met wood.

Then Chloen slid free.

Her leg came out from under the beam in a rush. The limb looked wrong—swollen already, skin split, blood running—but not as much bone under skin as he expected. Copper-gold traces licked across the bruised flesh for a heartbeat, then sank out of sight.

She screamed once, then sagged against Bram’s hold, panting.

“Got you,” Bram said, voice rough. “Got you. Stay with me.”

Jhace let the beam go.

The copper-gold light snapped out. All at once the weight he had been holding threw itself back into the wood. The beam dropped half a finger-width and settled, sending a new shallow crack into the floor stone but no full collapse.

Pain slammed into his hands. Not the sharp cut of a tool, but a wide burn that reached from fingertips to elbow. His fingers curled without his consent. Tremor ran through both arms.

“What,” Bram said. He stared at Jhace’s hands. “What did you—”

The world answered for him.

A deeper shock rolled through the Underface, not a tremor this time but a single, enormous pulse. The tunnel lights flared and went out. The pipe noise jumped in pitch, then steadied at a harsh new register. A low roar came from everywhere at once, from stone and air and the open shaft above.

The air pressed inward for several breaths, then snapped out again.

Jhace hit the floor on one knee. Pain in his hands fought with a new sensation in his chest, a heavy pressure that did not belong to breath. He looked up on instinct.

Through the shaft mouth he saw the sky.

Not a patch of blue. A slice of white so bright it hurt, cutting across the visible strip. The color at its edges warped. The usual clean outlines of rooftops bent, their edges drawn inward toward that moving line. Fine dust rained down, glittering faintly copper in the light.

Then the strip of sky vanished as more rubble slid across the gap and the shaft choked.

He heard screams above. Not just one street. Many.

“Out,” Jhace said. The word scraped his throat. “We have to get out.”

“Chloen can’t climb,” Bram said.

“Then we carry,” Jhace said. He forced his hands open. Pain flared fresh. “Find the emergency hatch. The one two junctions river-side. That run ties to street level. We move now.”

Bram stared for half a heartbeat longer, then shifted Chloen’s weight across his shoulders. Her injured leg hung at a wrong angle, but when it swung against his chest she didn’t scream. She just hissed and clamped her jaw, eyes unfocused.

Jhace pushed himself up. The tremor in his hands made the stone blur. He set his shoulder to the beam as they passed, testing. It felt stable enough for now.

They ran.

The tunnel around them had changed. Some braces had shifted; some chalk marks he knew by memory now lay under fresh dust. Twice they had to climb over fallen pipe. Once a side wall had buckled inward, leaving only a narrow gap. Jhace shoved at the broken edge until it gave them a passage, ignoring the way his palms complained.

By the time they reached the hatch, his breath tore in his chest. The metal door had jammed in its frame. He set both hands to it and shoved. For a moment a faint copper-gold shimmer traced the hinges. The frame groaned and moved half a span. Bram got a boot into the gap and kicked it wider.

They spilled into the surface.

The street outside looked wrong. Stone fractured in long, uneven lines. A whole building front across the way had peeled open, its face sheared off, rooms exposed to air. Dust drifted in slow curtains. People ran in broken patterns, some toward the damage, some away.

Above all of it, far higher than any tower, the white line cut the sky.

From here he could see more of it. It did not fall. It passed overhead, impossibly long, light bending along its edges. Fine metallic dust hung in its wake, catching sun in a faint copper shimmer that clung to the air.

He had no word for it. He had work.

Part of the building to their right groaned. The lower corner had sunk, its load shifted onto a single main support near where the street met the wall. A knot of people huddled at its base, half in the doorway, half in the open.

“Back!” Jhace shouted, voice raw. “Away from that wall!”

Some obeyed. Some didn’t. A woman with glass dust in her hair stood near the edge of the crowd, turned upward, staring. A logbook clutched to her chest. Tiffani, though he didn’t know her name yet.

The support post under the building’s corner split with a harsh crack. Stone above it began to fold. Blocks shifted, one after another, strain passing down the line.

If that post went, the whole corner would follow. The doorway would crush anyone still in reach. Many still stood too close.

Jhace’s hands burned. His fingers shook. His body already felt used past its proper limits.

Not again.

He ran for the failing post.

“Jhace!” Bram shouted behind him. “You can’t—”

Jhace dropped to his knees at the base of the support, where stone met foundation. Hairline fractures spidered out from the contact point. Dust leaked in slow streams. He pressed both palms flat against the cracked surface.

Hold. Long enough for them to run. That was all.

The world narrowed again.

Heat surged out of him in a hard wave. Copper-gold light burst around his fingers, brighter than before. It drove into the fractures, tracing every line through the stone. From his hands it spread upward into the post and sideways into adjoining blocks, chasing stress lines and filling them with glow.

The post trembled under his touch. For a breath he felt every load path in the structure, every place weight had shifted. It was too much. The sense of connection ran from his palms into his shoulders and chest, down his spine. Vibration filled his skull.

He dug his fingers into the stone. Muscles in his forearms locked. His breath turned shallow and ragged.

“Move!” Bram bellowed. “Everyone away! Now!”

Feet pounded the broken street. Out of the corner of his eye, Jhace saw the crowd pull back. The woman with the logbook staggered but did not fall. Her gaze locked on the glowing stone around his hands, eyes wide. Glass shards at her feet picked up the same copper-gold tone and hummed.

The building’s lean slowed.

The main support still wanted to fail. Cracks pressed against his hands. Stone above tried to fold further. He pushed against it with nothing but his body and whatever this new thing was, the warmth that refused to stop flowing.

His vision narrowed at the edges. Sound dulled. The copper-gold light brightened until it washed out detail, only masses and lines left.

“Jhace,” Bram’s voice came from far away. “That’s enough. She’s clear. They’re clear. Let it go.”

He tried to answer and couldn’t. His jaw wouldn’t move.

He held for three more heartbeats.

Then his arms stopped obeying.

The light cut out. The support dropped a fraction, then caught on whatever temporary balance he had helped it find. Stone dust cascaded in a fresh curtain. A few blocks fell and shattered harmlessly in the empty doorway. The building groaned, then settled into a new, broken shape that did not crush anyone.

Jhace’s body tipped sideways.

He hit the street on his shoulder. His hands twitched against the stone. Tremor ran uncontrolled from fingertips to elbow, a fine violent shake he couldn’t still. Pain bloomed there, deep and throbbing.

The sky tilted. The white line overhead had already passed, leaving only the strange haze.

Everything darkened.

—

Sound came back first. Shouts, weeping, the distant ring of tools. Someone calling orders. The grid’s low thrum, altered but present.

Then weight: a rough blanket over his chest, stone under his back.

Then his hands, still shaking.

Jhace opened his eyes.

He lay on the broken street, propped against a chunk of fallen masonry. Dust coated his clothes. Copper-gold smears marked the stone near his hands, fading by the breath. His fingers jerked in small, useless motions.

A shadow crossed his face.

He blinked up at a woman standing over him. Dark hair pulled back in a knot, streaked with fine glass dust and copper shimmer. Logbook still clutched to her chest with white-knuckled grip. Her eyes moved over him in quick, precise passes: hands, arms, chest, the stone behind his back, the faint traces of glow still seeping into the cracks he’d held.

Shock sat in her face. Behind it, calculation.

“What did you do?” she asked.

Her voice held no awe. Only demand.

Jhace swallowed. His throat felt raw. His hands would not stop moving.

“I held,” he said. The words came out low and rough. “It should have fallen.”

Their gazes locked for a breath. The city groaned around them. The grid’s hum ran at a new pitch under the noise.

The world had changed. His body knew it. So did her eyes.

Neither of them had a name for any of it yet.

# Chapter 3: The Sky Breaks (revised)

The wall moved before Jhace did.[1]

Stone shifted under his palm. A deep grind that did not belong. The tunnel’s thrum broke into a jagged pulse. The pipe beside him threw heat sideways hard enough that his fingers slipped.[1]

Then the ground hit.[1]

The Underface lurched. Jhace slammed into the wall. His lantern broke free and swung wild on its hook. Bolts shrieked in their brackets. Somewhere upslope a main line roared as pressure slammed through it, a tearing sound that went through bone.[1]

“Off the ladders!” he shouted. “Now!”[1]

Dust burst from the arch above, a blind cloud. The tunnel narrowed to sound and impact: stone cracking, metal twisting, grit falling in a hard rush that stung his eyes. He tasted old ash and something new under it, a sharp tang on his tongue.[1]

“Chloen!” Bram’s voice came from the ladder shaft. “Move!”[1]

Jhace staggered toward the shaft by habit, one hand on the wall to keep direction. The grid vibration had turned uneven, rolling in waves. A brace ahead of him groaned. He wrapped both arms around it and braced his feet.[1]

The first wave passed. The tunnel did not settle.[1]

Another shudder rolled through. The arch above the shaft gave a hard, hollow crack.[1]

“Off!” Jhace bellowed. “Both of you, off that metal!”[1]

Boots hit stone. A heartbeat later the ladder’s top anchor ripped out of the arch. Iron rungs slammed against the wall, sparks flaring where they struck pipe. The whole frame came down in a rattle of metal, choking the shaft with noise.[1]

Something heavier followed.[1]

A cross-beam tore loose from the upper structure and dropped through the open mouth of the shaft. Jhace saw only a bulked shadow in dust. It hit the tunnel floor with a wet, wrong sound. A short scream cut off.[1]

The dust thinned enough to show Chloen under the beam.[1]

It caught her across one leg above the knee. The rest of her lay clear. Her hands clawed at the floor. She dragged in air and let it out in a hoarse sound that barely reached words.[1]

“Jh—” She bit it off. Breath shook in her chest.[1]

Bram skidded across stone, dropped to his knees at her side. Blood seeped under the beam’s edge, dark against the damp floor.[1]

Jhace reached the timber in three strides. Thick as his chest. One end splintered. The other still buried in ceiling rubble. The weight sat full on her leg.[1]

He planted his feet, set both hands under the beam’s edge, and heaved.[1]

Nothing moved.[1]

Muscle in his back lit with pain. The beam gave no answer. The arch above answered instead—another cracking line, the soft crumble of stone. If he kept forcing, he would bring the rest of the shaft down on all three of them.[1]

“Jhace,” Bram snapped. “Stop. You’ll bring the whole thing.”[1]

Chloen’s fingers dug into the floor. She wasn’t making sound now. Her face had gone gray. Lips pulled back from her teeth.[1]

“How bad,” Jhace said.[1]

“Crushed,” Bram said. No comfort. “We get it off in the next few breaths, she keeps the limb. We fail, she bleeds out or the bone cuts through. Or it all comes down. Good choices.”[1]

Another tremor rolled through. Smaller, but enough to rattle grit from the arch. The beam bounced once and settled heavier.[1]

Chloen bit down on her sleeve. Her eyes found Jhace’s. Fear. And trust she had no right to spend.[4]

Jhace saw another shaft in his head. A smaller brace. A quiet hiss he had called harmless. Twenty-three names after.[4]

Not again.[4]

“Bram,” he said. “Pull when I tell you. Straight back. Keep her spine clear.”[1]

“Jhace—”[1]

“Now or we lose her.”[1]

Bram moved. He slid his arms under Chloen’s shoulders and upper body and planted his boots wide.[1]

Jhace spat dust, wiped his palms once on his trousers, and put both hands on the beam again. This time he did not try to lift. He let skin settle to rough wood. He felt the crushed fibers where impact had flattened grain. He dropped one knee to stone and lowered his center.[1]

The old grid thrum still ran under everything. A sharper pulse rode on top of it.[1]

He fixed on the weight at one point.[2]

Hold. Long enough.[2]

Heat built in his palms. At first it felt like friction. Then it sharpened and sank through skin into bone. The pressure under his hands changed. Mass turned into resistance. Something pushed back.[2]

Copper-gold light spilled out around his fingers.[2]

Jhace flinched and did not pull away. The light came from his own skin, thin streams running into the wood grain. It spread into the shattered end and into the stone on either side, tracing stress he did not know how to name. The beam vibrated with it, a tight resonance inside the deeper shake of the tunnel.[2]

His arms locked. Shoulder to jaw clenched. Breath left and did not come back clean.[2]

“Now,” he got out.[1]

Bram hauled. The beam did not rise much. It did lighten. Jhace felt the load shift through his hands, up his forearms, into his chest, then out again. Sweat ran into his eyes. His teeth ached. For a few breaths the world narrowed to the hot line where flesh met wood.[2]

Then Chloen slid free.[1]

Her leg came out from under the beam in a rush. It looked wrong—swollen, skin split, blood running—but not as much bone under skin as he expected. Copper-gold traces ran across the bruised flesh for a heartbeat, then sank out of sight.[2]

She screamed once and sagged against Bram’s hold, panting.[1]

“Got you,” Bram said, voice rough. “Got you. Stay with me.”[1]

Jhace let go.[2]

“What,” Bram said. He stared at Jhace’s hands. “What did you—”

The tunnel answered.

A deeper shock rolled through the Underface. Not a tremor. A single, enormous pulse. The tunnel lights flared and went out. Pipe noise jumped in pitch, then steadied at a harsh new register. A low roar came from everywhere at once—stone and air and the open shaft above.

The air pressed inward for several breaths, then snapped out again.

Jhace hit the floor on one knee. Pain in his hands fought with a heavy pressure in his chest that did not belong to breath.

He looked up.

Through the shaft mouth he saw the sky. Not blue. A slice of white so bright it hurt, cutting across the visible strip. Color at its edges warped. Rooftop outlines bent. Fine dust rained down, glittering faintly copper in the light.

Then rubble slid across the gap and the shaft choked.

He heard screams above. Not one street. Many.

“Out,” Jhace said. The word scraped his throat. “We get out.”

“Chloen can’t climb,” Bram said.

“Then we carry.”

Jhace forced his hands open. Pain flared fresh. Tremor kept going.

“Emergency hatch,” he said. “Two junctions river-side. That run ties to street.”

Bram shifted Chloen’s weight across his shoulders. Her injured leg hung wrong, but when it bumped against his chest she didn’t scream. She hissed and clamped her jaw, eyes unfocused.

Jhace pushed up. The tremor in his hands made the stone edges blur. He set his shoulder to the beam as they passed, testing. It felt stable enough for the breaths it would take to clear.

They ran.

The tunnel had changed shape. Some braces had shifted. Some chalk marks lay under fresh dust. Twice they climbed over fallen pipe. Once a side wall buckled inward, leaving a narrow gap. Jhace shoved at broken stone until it gave them passage, ignoring the way his palms complained.

By the time they reached the hatch, his breath tore in his chest. The metal door had jammed in its frame. He set both hands to it and shoved.

For a breath, a faint copper-gold shimmer traced the hinges. Heat stung up his forearms. The frame groaned and moved. Pain sharpened in his palms.

Bram got a boot into the gap and kicked it wider.

They spilled onto the surface.

The street looked wrong. Stone fractured in long, uneven lines. A building front across the way had peeled open, its face sheared away, rooms exposed. Dust drifted in slow curtains. People ran in broken patterns, some toward the damage, some away.

Above all of it, far higher than any tower, the white line cut the sky.

From here he could see more of it. It did not fall. It passed overhead, impossibly long, light bending along its edges. Fine metallic dust hung in its wake, catching light in a faint copper shimmer that clung to the air.

Jhace had no word for it. He had work.

The building to their right groaned. The lower corner had sunk. Its load had shifted onto a single main support near where street met wall. A knot of people huddled at its base, half in the doorway, half in the open.

“Back!” Jhace shouted. “Away from that wall!”

Some obeyed. Some didn’t. A woman with glass dust in her hair stood near the edge of the crowd, turned upward, staring. A logbook clutched to her chest. Jhace did not know her name.

The support post split with a harsh crack. Stone above it began to fold. Blocks shifted in a chain, strain passing down the line.

If that post went, the whole corner followed. The doorway crushed anyone still within reach. Too many still stood too close.

Jhace’s hands burned. His fingers shook. His body already felt used past what it was meant to do.

Not again.

He ran for the failing post.

“Jhace!” Bram shouted behind him. “You can’t—”

Jhace dropped to his knees at the base of the support, where stone met foundation. Hairline fractures spidered out from the contact point. Dust leaked in slow streams. He pressed both palms flat against the cracked surface. Skin to stone.

Hold. Long enough for them to run.

Heat surged out of him. Copper-gold burst around his fingers, brighter than before. It drove into the fractures, tracing each line through the stone. From his hands it spread upward into the post and sideways into adjoining blocks, chasing stress and filling it with glow.

For a breath he felt every load path. Every place weight had shifted. It was too much. The sense of connection ran from his palms into shoulder and chest and down his spine. Vibration filled his skull.

He dug his fingers into stone. Forearms locked. Breath turned shallow and ragged.

“Move!” Bram bellowed. “Everyone away! Now!”

Feet pounded broken street. The crowd pulled back. The woman with the logbook staggered but did not fall. Her gaze fixed on the glow around his hands, wide and sharp. Glass shards at her feet picked up the copper-gold tint and hummed.

The building’s lean slowed.

The main support still wanted to fail. Cracks pressed against his hands. Stone above tried to fold further. He pushed back with body and heat and will, nothing clean about it.

His vision narrowed at the edges. Sound dulled. Copper-gold brightened until it washed detail away. Only masses and lines remained.

“Jhace,” Bram’s voice came from far off. “That’s enough. They’re clear. Let it go.”

Jhace tried to answer. His jaw would not move.

He held for three more heartbeats.

Then his arms stopped obeying.

The light cut out. The support dropped a fraction, then caught on a new, broken balance. Stone dust cascaded in a fresh curtain. A few blocks fell and shattered in the empty doorway. The building groaned and settled into a shape that did not crush anyone.

Jhace tipped sideways.

He hit the street on his shoulder. His hands twitched against stone. Tremor ran uncontrolled from fingertips to elbow, violent and fine. Pain bloomed deep and throbbing.

The sky tilted. The white line overhead had already passed, leaving haze.

Everything darkened.

—

Sound came back first. Shouts. Weeping. The ring of tools. Someone calling orders. The grid’s low thrum, altered but present.

Then weight: a rough blanket over his chest, stone under his back.

Then his hands. Still shaking.

Jhace opened his eyes.

He lay on broken street, propped against fallen masonry. Dust coated his clothes. Copper-gold smears marked the stone near his hands, fading by the breath. His fingers jerked in small, useless motions.

A shadow crossed his face.

A woman stood over him. Dark hair pulled back in a knot, streaked with fine glass dust and copper shimmer. A logbook clenched to her chest. Her eyes moved over him in quick passes: hands, arms, the stone behind his back, the faint traces of glow still seeping into cracks he had held.

Shock sat on her face. Behind it, calculation.

“What did you do?” she asked.

Her voice held no awe. Only demand.

Jhace swallowed. His throat scraped. His hands would not stop moving.

“I held,” he said. “It should have fallen.”

Their gazes locked for a breath. The city groaned around them. The grid’s hum ran at a new pitch under the noise.

The world had changed. His body knew it. So did her eyes. Neither of them had a name for any of it yet.

One constraint issue to fix
“fingers slipped” / “dropped a finger” is fine, but “a finger” is functioning as a measurement unit; the validator flags Earth-measurement words broadly (inch/foot are explicit, but the safest in N01 is to stay in-body without treating it as a unit). Easiest fix: change “dropped a finger” to “dropped a breath’s worth” is wrong (time), so instead use a pure visual/physical phrasing: “dropped a small fraction” or “dropped and settled” (no unit).​

Two micro-suggestions (optional)
You still have a mild personification: “The tunnel answered.” If you want fully literal, swap to “Another pulse hit.” (same beat, no agency).​

The Soulpulse aftermath checklist wants vein-line visibility at least briefly during/after use in Ch 3 (even if Jhace doesn’t understand it yet). One sentence can cover it without changing events, e.g. after the first release: “Copper-gold lines lit along his forearms for a breath, then faded, leaving his hands shaking.”​

# Chapter 3: The Sky Breaks

The wall moved before Jhace did.

Stone shifted under his palm. A deep grind that did not belong. The tunnel’s thrum broke into a jagged pulse. The pipe beside him threw heat sideways hard enough that his fingers slipped.

Then the ground hit.

The Underface lurched. Jhace slammed into the wall. His lantern broke free and swung wild on its hook. Bolts shrieked in their brackets. Somewhere upslope a main line roared as pressure slammed through it, a tearing sound that went through bone.

“Off the ladders!” he shouted. “Now!”

Dust burst from the arch above, a blind cloud. The tunnel narrowed to sound and impact: stone cracking, metal twisting, grit falling in a hard rush that stung his eyes. He tasted old ash and something new under it, a sharp tang on his tongue.

“Chloen!” Bram’s voice came from the ladder shaft. “Move!”

Jhace staggered toward the shaft by habit, one hand on the wall to keep direction. The grid vibration had turned uneven, rolling in waves. A brace ahead of him groaned. He wrapped both arms around it and braced his feet.

The first wave passed. The tunnel did not settle.

Another shudder rolled through. The arch above the shaft gave a hard, hollow crack.

“Off!” Jhace bellowed. “Both of you, off that metal!”

Boots hit stone. A heartbeat later the ladder’s top anchor ripped out of the arch. Iron rungs slammed against the wall, sparks flaring where they struck pipe. The whole frame came down in a rattle of metal, choking the shaft with noise.

Something heavier followed.

A cross-beam tore loose from the upper structure and dropped through the open mouth of the shaft. Jhace saw only a bulked shadow in dust. It hit the tunnel floor with a wet, wrong sound. A short scream cut off.

The dust thinned enough to show Chloen under the beam.

It caught her across one leg above the knee. The rest of her lay clear. Her hands clawed at the floor. She dragged in air and let it out in a hoarse sound that barely reached words.

“Jh—” She bit it off. Breath shook in her chest.

Bram skidded across stone, dropped to his knees at her side. Blood seeped under the beam’s edge, dark against the damp floor.

Jhace reached the timber in three strides. Thick, near his chest-width. One end splintered. The other still buried in ceiling rubble. The weight sat full on her leg.

He planted his feet, set both hands under the beam’s edge, and heaved.

Nothing moved.

Muscle in his back lit with pain. The beam gave no answer. The arch above answered instead—another cracking line, the soft crumble of stone. If he kept forcing, he would bring the rest of the shaft down on all three of them.

“Jhace,” Bram snapped. “Stop. You’ll bring the whole thing.”

Chloen’s fingers dug into the floor. She wasn’t making sound now. Her face had gone gray. Lips pulled back from her teeth.

“How bad,” Jhace said.

“Crushed,” Bram said. No comfort. “We get it off in the next few breaths, she keeps the limb. We fail, she bleeds out or the bone cuts through. Or it all comes down. Good choices.”

Another tremor rolled through. Smaller, but enough to rattle grit from the arch. The beam bounced once and settled heavier.

Chloen bit down on her sleeve. Her eyes found Jhace’s. Fear. And trust he had not earned.

Jhace saw another shaft in his head. A smaller brace. A quiet hiss he had called harmless. Twenty-three names after.

Not again.

“Bram,” he said. “Pull when I tell you. Straight back. Keep her spine clear.”

“Jhace—”

“Now or we lose her.”

Bram moved. He slid his arms under Chloen’s shoulders and upper body and planted his boots wide.

Jhace spat dust, wiped his palms once on his trousers, and put both hands on the beam again. This time he did not try to lift. He let skin settle to rough wood. He felt the crushed fibers where impact had flattened grain. He dropped one knee to stone and lowered his center.

The old grid thrum still ran under everything. A sharper pulse rode on top of it.

He fixed on the weight at one point.

Hold. Long enough.

Heat built in his palms. At first it felt like friction. Then it sharpened and sank through skin into bone. The pressure under his hands changed. Mass turned into resistance. Something pushed back.

Copper-gold light spilled out around his fingers.

Jhace flinched and did not pull away. The light came from his own skin, thin streams running into the wood grain. It spread into the shattered end and into the stone on either side, tracing stress he did not know how to name. The beam vibrated with it, a tight resonance inside the deeper shake of the tunnel.

His arms locked. Shoulder to jaw clenched. Breath left and did not come back clean.

“Now,” he got out.

Bram hauled. The beam did not rise much. It did lighten. Jhace felt the load shift through his hands, up his forearms, into his chest, then out again. Sweat ran into his eyes. His teeth ached. For a few breaths the world narrowed to the hot line where flesh met wood.

Then Chloen slid free.

Her leg came out from under the beam in a rush. It looked wrong—swollen, skin split, blood running—but not as much bone under skin as he expected. Copper-gold traces ran across the bruised flesh for a heartbeat, then sank out of sight.

She screamed once and sagged against Bram’s hold, panting.

“Got you,” Bram said, voice rough. “Got you. Stay with me.”

Jhace let go.

The copper-gold snapped out. The weight threw itself back into the wood. The beam dropped and jolted the stone, then settled, sending a shallow crack through the floor but no full collapse.

Pain hit his hands. Not a cut. A wide burn from fingertips to elbow. His fingers curled without consent. Tremor ran through both arms. Copper-gold lines followed the veins in his forearms for a breath, then faded, leaving only the shake.

“What,” Bram said. He stared at Jhace’s hands. “What did you—”

Another pulse hit.

A deeper shock rolled through the Underface. Not a tremor. A single, enormous surge. The tunnel lights flared and went out. Pipe noise jumped in pitch, then steadied at a harsh new register. A low roar came from everywhere at once—stone and air and the open shaft above.

The air pressed inward for several breaths, then snapped out again.

Jhace hit the floor on one knee. Pain in his hands fought with a heavy pressure in his chest that did not belong to breath.

He looked up.

Through the shaft mouth he saw the sky. Not blue. A slice of white so bright it hurt, cutting across the visible strip. Color at its edges warped. Rooftop outlines bent. Fine dust rained down, glittering faintly copper in the light.

Then rubble slid across the gap and the shaft choked.

He heard screams above. Not one street. Many.

“Out,” Jhace said. The word scraped his throat. “We get out.”

“Chloen can’t climb,” Bram said.

“Then we carry.”

Jhace forced his hands open. Pain flared fresh. Tremor kept going.

“Emergency hatch,” he said. “Two junctions river-side. That run ties to street.”

Bram shifted Chloen’s weight across his shoulders. Her injured leg hung wrong, but when it bumped against his chest she didn’t scream. She hissed and clamped her jaw, eyes unfocused.

Jhace pushed up. The tremor in his hands made the stone edges blur. He set his shoulder to the beam as they passed, testing. It felt stable enough for the breaths it would take to clear.

They ran.

The tunnel had changed shape. Some braces had shifted. Some chalk marks lay under fresh dust. Twice they climbed over fallen pipe. Once a side wall buckled inward, leaving a narrow gap. Jhace shoved at broken stone until it gave them passage, ignoring the way his palms complained.

By the time they reached the hatch, his breath tore in his chest. The metal door had jammed in its frame. He set both hands to it and shoved.

For a breath, a faint copper-gold shimmer traced the hinges. Heat stung up his forearms. The frame groaned and moved. Pain sharpened in his palms.

Bram got a boot into the gap and kicked it wider.

They spilled onto the surface.

The street looked wrong. Stone fractured in long, uneven lines. A building front across the way had peeled open, its face sheared away, rooms exposed. Dust drifted in slow curtains. People ran in broken patterns, some toward the damage, some away.

Above all of it, far higher than any tower, the white line cut the sky.

From here he could see more of it. It did not fall. It passed overhead, impossibly long, light bending along its edges. Fine metallic dust hung in its wake, catching light in a faint copper shimmer that clung to the air.

Jhace had no word for it. He had work.

The building to their right groaned. The lower corner had sunk. Its load had shifted onto a single main support near where street met wall. A knot of people huddled at its base, half in the doorway, half in the open.

“Back!” Jhace shouted. “Away from that wall!”

Some obeyed. Some didn’t. A woman with glass dust in her hair stood near the edge of the crowd, turned upward, staring. A logbook clutched to her chest. Jhace did not know her name.

The support post split with a harsh crack. Stone above it began to fold. Blocks shifted in a chain, strain passing down the line.

If that post went, the whole corner followed. The doorway crushed anyone still within reach. Too many still stood too close.

Jhace’s hands burned. His fingers shook. His body already felt used past what it was meant to do.

Not again.

He ran for the failing post.

“Jhace!” Bram shouted behind him. “You can’t—”

Jhace dropped to his knees at the base of the support, where stone met foundation. Hairline fractures spidered out from the contact point. Dust leaked in slow streams. He pressed both palms flat against the cracked surface. Skin to stone.

Hold. Long enough for them to run.

Heat surged out of him. Copper-gold burst around his fingers, brighter than before. It drove into the fractures, tracing each line through the stone. From his hands it spread upward into the post and sideways into adjoining blocks, chasing stress and filling it with glow.

For a breath he felt every load path. Every place weight had shifted. It was too much. The sense of connection ran from his palms into shoulder and chest and down his spine. Vibration filled his skull.

He dug his fingers into stone. Forearms locked. Breath turned shallow and ragged.

“Move!” Bram bellowed. “Everyone away! Now!”

Feet pounded broken street. The crowd pulled back. The woman with the logbook staggered but did not fall. Her gaze fixed on the glow around his hands, wide and sharp. Glass shards at her feet picked up the copper-gold tint and hummed.

The building’s lean slowed.

The main support still wanted to fail. Cracks pressed against his hands. Stone above tried to fold further. He pushed back with body and heat and will, nothing clean about it.

His vision narrowed at the edges. Sound dulled. Copper-gold brightened until it washed detail away. Only masses and lines remained.

“Jhace,” Bram’s voice came from far off. “That’s enough. They’re clear. Let it go.”

Jhace tried to answer. His jaw would not move.

He held for three more heartbeats.

Then his arms stopped obeying.

The light cut out. The support dropped a small distance, then caught on a new, broken balance. Stone dust cascaded in a fresh curtain. A few blocks fell and shattered in the empty doorway. The building groaned and settled into a shape that did not crush anyone.

Jhace tipped sideways.

He hit the street on his shoulder. His hands twitched against stone. Tremor ran uncontrolled from fingertips to elbow, violent and fine. Pain bloomed deep and throbbing.

The sky tilted. The white line overhead had already passed, leaving haze.

Everything darkened.

—

Sound came back first. Shouts. Weeping. The ring of tools. Someone calling orders. The grid’s low thrum, altered but present.

Then weight: a rough blanket over his chest, stone under his back.

Then his hands. Still shaking.

Jhace opened his eyes.

He lay on broken street, propped against fallen masonry. Dust coated his clothes. Copper-gold smears marked the stone near his hands, fading by the breath. His fingers jerked in small, useless motions.

A shadow crossed his face.

A woman stood over him. Dark hair pulled back in a knot, streaked with fine glass dust and copper shimmer. A logbook clenched to her chest. Her eyes moved over him in quick passes: hands, arms, the stone behind his back, the faint traces of glow still seeping into cracks he had held.

Shock sat on her face. Behind it, calculation.

“What did you do?” she asked.

Her voice held no awe. Only demand.

Jhace swallowed. His throat scraped. His hands would not stop moving.

“I held,” he said. “It should have fallen.”

Their gazes locked for a breath. The city groaned around them. The grid’s hum ran at a new pitch under the noise.

The world had changed. His body knew it. So did her eyes. Neither of them had a name for any of it yet.
