[ASSISTANT]
# Chapter 4: After the Shudder  

Jhace woke to stone under his shoulders and a smell of boiled herbs.

For a breath he thought he was still in the street. Then the sounds sorted: not collapse, but low talk. Cloth rustling. Metal in basins. Somewhere a child crying, then hushed. No roar in the walls, only a roughened grid thrum under everything.

His hands hurt.

He tried to flex his fingers. Tremor ran from fingertips to elbow in a fine, constant shake. Pain followed, a dull burn along bone. Someone had wrapped rough cloth around his palms; the bandage edges scraped.

“Stay down,” a voice said. Bram.

Jhace turned his head. He lay on a low pallet in a side room off a wider tunnel. The ceiling sat higher than an Underface run, stone mortared in regular blocks. Someone had cleared space and laid out more pallets in two rows. A triage nook, then. He recognized the top of a ladder shaft in the next room, the one near Bram’s corridor.

Bram sat on an overturned crate a pace away, elbows on his knees, face drawn. A bruise had started along one cheekbone, dark under dust. He held a clay cup in both hands.

“How long,” Jhace said. His voice came out rough.

“Since late Striking,” Bram answered. “We’re in Tide One now. Kindling’s moving. You were out for most of a phase.”  

Jhace pushed himself up onto an elbow. The room spun once and steadied. His stomach rolled; he ignored it.

“Chloen,” he said.

“Alive.” Bram’s mouth twitched. “Leg is… odd. She should have lost it. Healer says crushed bone, torn flesh. But when they cleaned it, some of the worst breaks were already knitting. Still bad. Not that bad. She’s under enough chew-root to sleep through a collapse.”

Jhace’s gaze slid past him. Chloen lay three pallets down. Her leg was splinted from hip to ankle, wrapped in clean cloth, stained through in a few places. Her chest rose and fell slow and even. Copper shimmer clung faintly at the edges of the bandages.

Jhace’s fingers jerked. Memory came in pieces: timber weight, heat in his hands, copper-gold sliding into bruised flesh for a flash.

He swallowed.

“Do not do that,” Bram said quietly. “Do not put that on your list. She’s breathing because of you. Whatever else that was.”

Jhace looked down at his own hands. The cloth around them was discolored with dust and a faint copper stain. Under the bandages, his fingers twitched in small, useless patterns.

“What do they say happened,” he asked.

“Out there?” Bram jerked his chin toward the doorway. “Depends who you ask. Some say a falling building caught itself. Some say the Piercing tore the rules and Saint Merro’s stones are waking up angry. Some say there’s a Forgemaster with molten hands walking the streets.” His eyes went back to Jhace’s bandages. “Plenty say a grid rat in the Underface held a whole corner on his own.”

Jhace’s stomach tightened.

“They’re asking for you,” Bram went on. “The ones who were under that wall. Runners keep sticking their heads in here. Asking if the man who held it is ready to go again.”

“I held a span,” Jhace said. His throat tightened around the word. “It did not fall because the loads settled on another path. They want the wrong thing.”

“Doesn’t change what they want,” Bram said. He set the cup on the floor. “City’s cracked. Council is arguing in the Crown. The guilds are yelling at each other about whose task this is. Meanwhile the stone fails where it pleases. We have work.”

Jhace swung his legs over the side of the pallet. His feet hit cold stone. Tremor raced up his arms in answer to the shift.

Bram reached to steady him. Jhace shook his head once. He pushed himself upright without help. The room lurched, then steadied.

“Bad idea,” Bram muttered. “Very on-brand.”

“Show me what failed,” Jhace said.

Bram held his gaze for a few breaths, then sighed.

“Fine. But I walk beside you. If you fall, you don’t take a brace with you.”

Jhace said nothing. He bent to pull the bandages loose enough for his fingers to move. The cloth stuck in a few places; pain flared. The tremor did not care.

They stepped out into the main tunnel.

This level had become a passage more crowded than it had ever been. People moved in steady lines: runners with messages, guild workers with tools, healers with baskets. A smell of blood and hot metal hung under the usual damp stone. The grid’s hum had shifted; it ran harsher now, edges rough where pipes had taken damage.

As Jhace and Bram merged into the flow, heads turned.

“That’s him,” someone murmured. “The one from the street.”

Jhace kept his eyes on the tunnel ahead. He let their words slide off.

“Where first,” he asked.

“Nearest failures we know about.” Bram pointed river-side. “Two marry points deformed. One small support crack we can’t spare full crews for yet. Figured we’d start there. Test the grid, test your legs.”

“Test my hands,” Jhace said.

Bram’s mouth flattened.

They reached the first damaged junction after a short walk. The brace there had sheared partially away from the wall. Temporary wedges and a strap had been set to carry most of the load until a full crew could replace it. The main itself hummed steady; the bracket, not so much.

Jhace set his fingertips against the stone.

The vibration there told a clear story: stress transferred, not perfect but stable enough for now.

“We move on,” he said.

The next problem sat deeper, in a lower run where the air grew hotter. A side support there had cracked along its base. Someone had braced it with a stack of loose stone, but the contact was poor; gaps showed.

“No one under this line?” Jhace asked.

“Not if they listened,” Bram said. “Street above already cleared.”

Jhace studied the crack. Not immediate collapse. Not harmless either. A failure here in the next Tide would drop a section of tunnel and weaken the wall of the building over it.

It would be days before the guild could spare enough workers.

He put his palm on the broken base.

Stone met skin. Cool under the surface, warmer where it held load. He could feel the fracture: a thin gap, rough edges ready to spread. Tremor in his hand muddied the read.

He closed his eyes.

Hold, he told whatever this new thing was. Hold here. Small span. No one is under it, but they will be. That should have been enough.

Nothing changed.

No heat surge. No copper-gold. Only the familiar push of load against his hand and the tremor in his own fingers.

He pressed harder. Pain flared in his palm, then his wrist. The crack did not answer.

Bram watched from a pace away.

“Anything?” he asked.

“No,” Jhace said.

“Good,” Bram said. “Because if you were about to tell me you can now fix every hairline scratch in this city with your glow, I was going to go lie down in traffic.”

Jhace pulled his hand back. His skin tingled from the effort, but the stone stayed unchanged.

“Not on demand,” he said.

“So we learn something,” Bram said. “Whatever that was, it’s not a new wrench you can hang on your belt. Needs more than a bad seam.”

More than a bad seam. He thought of Chloen’s leg under the beam. The faces under the building corner. His own head and chest had been full of one thing then: do not let this kill them.

He flexed his fingers. Tremor answered, unchanged.

“Mark this for priority reinforcement,” he said. “We do it by hand.”

They climbed back toward the surface.

The next failure found them before they reached open air.

A runner nearly collided with them at a junction. A boy, sweat-slick, chest heaving.

“You Jhace Torrins?” the boy gasped. “The holder?”

Jhace’s shoulders tightened.

“Yes,” Bram said, before Jhace could decide whether to argue with the word. “What’s failing?”

“Small shop front near the Merro kiln corridor,” the boy said. “Wall cracked. Healers set up inside, working triage. Master said to get the holder, or we lose half the wounded when it goes.”

Jhace didn’t look at Bram. He just started moving.

The street near the kilns was a mess.

One whole row of smaller buildings had taken impact from falling debris. Most still stood, but faces were fractured, bits of stone missing, wooden flanges splintered. Someone had chosen a shop with only one outer wall to convert into a healer’s space. The wounded lay on tables and pallets inside. The wall that separated them from the street now showed a visible crack from lintel to ground.

People crowded the doorway. Some tried to brace the wall with poles and stacked debris. The fracture deepened a grain at a time as the crowd jostled.

“Back,” Jhace said.

No one moved at first. His voice came out raw, not loud.

Bram stepped up beside him.

“You heard him,” Bram said. “Back. Give stone room to breathe.”

The crowd parted in jerks and mutters. Jhace stepped to the crack.

Up close, it looked worse. Fine dust leaked from it in a slow stream. The load path for the roof had shifted onto two narrow points instead of a full line. The shop’s inner ceiling beams rested on that.

Several wounded lay directly under them.

Jhace put his palms flat on the wall at the crack’s middle. Cold from outside, heat from bodies inside, pressure from above.

Hold, he thought. They die if you do not hold.

Heat rose under his hands. Not as sharp as under the beam or the street support, but fast enough. Copper-gold bloomed across his skin and slid into the stone, thin lines chasing the fracture up and down. They reached the lintel and the floor, then flowed sideways into the adjoining blocks.

He gritted his teeth. His arms went rigid.

“Clear under the center span,” Bram shouted. “Move the pallets outward. Go!”

Voices answered in fragments. He heard scrambling inside. Cloth dragged. Someone groaned in pain as they were shifted.

The wall pressed against his hands, trying to move. The crack wanted to widen. He could feel every point that carried too much now. It was more information than he had ever wanted. His pulse beat inside his fingertips.

His own shake intensified. The copper-gold in his forearms lit vein-lines down to his elbows. Every heartbeat drove a fresh spike of pain along them.

“Almost,” Bram called. “Hold.”

Jhace held.

Breaths blurred. The glow spread as far as it could find stress. Then it stopped. It did not mend. It only kept the worst from moving.

“Clear,” Bram shouted at last. “Everyone out from center.”

Jhace counted three heartbeats more, then let go.

The copper-gold cut off. The wall shifted a small amount, then caught on a new angle, the crack now filled with fine stone dust and glass-bright residue. Some loose chips fell and bounced off the cleared floor. The ceiling beams groaned but stayed where they were.

Jhace’s hands dropped to his sides. His fingers clawed without his consent, opening and closing in jittering motions.

“Out,” Bram told the crowd. “No one rests under that span. You—” he pointed at a pair of workers “—get poles and a proper brace on it before next Pouring.”

Jhace stepped back until his shoulders hit the opposite wall. The world had gone slightly out of focus at the edges. Sweat dripped down his back. His hands shook hard enough that his nails clicked against his own skin.

“Your arms,” Bram said, low. “Veins went bright all the way up. Then nothing. You still with me?”

“For now,” Jhace said. His tongue felt thick.

He looked down. Faint copper-gold lines still traced his forearms, pulsing in time with the tremor. They faded slowly, but the shake did not.

“Well,” Bram said. “We learned the other half.”

“What half,” Jhace asked.

“Whatever this is,” Bram said, “it answers when people are about to get crushed. It does not answer when you want to fix a crack for later. And every time it listens, it takes more out of you.”

Jhace didn’t answer. He stared at his hands until the copper traces vanished, leaving only red skin and the unsteady twitch.

Someone cleared their throat to his left.

The woman from the street stood there. The one with glass dust in her hair and the logbook clutched to her chest. She had cleaned up some since he’d seen her last; the dust now lined her clothes instead of her face. Her eyes were sharper than before, if that was possible.

“Jhace Torrins,” she said.

He narrowed his eyes.

“You know my name.”

“You shouted it at the wall yesterday,” she said. “Bram yelled it three times. And half the runners are using it now.” She looked at his hands. “May I see where you touched?”

“No,” he said.

Her brows lifted a fraction.

“I need to understand what changed in the stone,” she said. “People are setting up triage in buildings you’ve… supported. If I know where it’s strongest, I can tell them where not to lie down.”

Jhace exhaled through his teeth. That, at least, was an argument he could not throw aside.

He turned back to the cracked wall and set one shaking finger against the point where the glow had felt thickest. The motion was clumsy. His fingertip missed twice before finding the line.

“Here,” he said. “And here.” He traced the path down and across as best he could.

She stepped in closer, careful not to touch him. Her gaze followed his hand, then left it, measuring the stone on its own.

“The temperature is wrong,” she murmured. “Hot along that line, cooler elsewhere. The stress reads different too.” She pressed her palm to the wall, eyes half closed. “It shouldn’t be standing. But it is.”

“It is standing for now,” Jhace said. “Do not trust it for more than that.”

“I don’t trust anything for more than that,” she said. “But I prefer numbers to stories. Stories are already taking root about you.”

“Stories are useless,” Jhace said. “Either the brace holds or it doesn’t.”

“And if it only holds because you touch it?” she asked.

He had no answer.

She looked down at his hands again. The tremor had not eased. He tried to still his fingers; they shook anyway.

“Is that new?” she asked.

“Old injury,” he said. The lie came fast, worn from too much practice over the Tide. “Tools do that.”

“Mm,” she said. She did not sound convinced. “If you keep lying badly, you’re going to make my work harder.”

Before he could reply, someone called her name from up the street. She glanced toward the sound, then back at him.

“I’m Tiffani Merrow,” she said. “Glasswork and structural optics.” She nodded toward the wall. “I intend to make sure people don’t sleep where your grips will fail first. That requires data.”

“I have routes,” Jhace said. “I don’t have data.”

“You have both,” she said. “You just don’t write all of it down yet.”

She stepped away, already scanning the next stack of damaged fronts, already turning the scene into columns in her head. Bram watched her go with a low whistle.

“She’s going to be trouble,” he said.

Jhace opened his mouth to tell him he already had enough trouble and dropped the words.

A bent nail lay near his boot. He bent to pick it up.

His fingers missed it twice. On the third try he managed to trap it between thumb and forefinger, but the tremor jerked it free. It clinked back to stone.

He stared at it.

Bram’s gaze slid toward his hands.

Jhace straightened slowly. He put his hands behind his back, hiding the twitch.

“I’m fine,” he said.

Bram did not answer.

Across the street, Tiffani watched them. Her eyes dropped once to Jhace’s hidden hands, then back to his face. She said nothing either.

The grid hummed at its new pitch under their feet. The wall held. Jhace’s grip did not.

# Chapter 4: After the Shudder  

Jhace woke to stone under his shoulders and a smell of boiled herbs.

For a breath he thought he was still in the street. Then the sounds sorted: not collapse, but low talk. Cloth rustling. Metal in basins. Somewhere a child crying, then hushed. No roar in the walls, only a roughened grid thrum under everything.

His hands hurt.

He tried to flex his fingers. Tremor ran from fingertips to elbow in a fine, constant shake. Pain followed, a dull burn along bone. Someone had wrapped rough cloth around his palms; the bandage edges scraped.

“Stay down,” a voice said. Bram.

Jhace turned his head. He lay on a low pallet in a side room off a wider tunnel. The ceiling sat higher than an Underface run, stone mortared in regular blocks. Someone had cleared space and laid out more pallets in two rows. A triage nook, then. He recognized the top of a ladder shaft in the next room, the one near Bram’s corridor.

Bram sat on an overturned crate a pace away, elbows on his knees, face drawn. A bruise had started along one cheekbone, dark under dust. He held a clay cup in both hands.

“How long,” Jhace said. His voice came out rough.

“Since late Striking,” Bram answered. “We’re in Tide One now. Kindling’s already moving. You were out for most of that last stretch.”

Jhace pushed himself up onto an elbow. The room spun once and steadied. His stomach rolled; he ignored it.

“Chloen,” he said.

“Alive.” Bram’s mouth twitched. “Leg is… odd. She should have lost it. Healer says crushed bone, torn flesh. But when they cleaned it, some of the worst breaks were already knitting. Still bad. Not that bad. She’s under enough chew-root to sleep through a collapse.”

Jhace’s gaze slid past him. Chloen lay three pallets down. Her leg was splinted from hip to ankle, wrapped in clean cloth, stained through in a few places. Her chest rose and fell slow and even. Copper shimmer clung faintly at the edges of the bandages.

Jhace’s fingers jerked. Memory came in pieces: timber weight, heat in his hands, copper-gold sliding into bruised flesh for a flash.

He swallowed.

“Do not do that,” Bram said quietly. “Do not put that on your list. She’s breathing because of you. Whatever else that was.”

Jhace looked down at his own hands. The cloth around them was discolored with dust and a faint copper stain. Under the bandages, his fingers twitched in small, useless patterns.

“What do they say happened,” he asked.

“Out there?” Bram jerked his chin toward the doorway. “Depends who you ask. Some say a falling building caught itself. Some say the Piercing tore the rules and Saint Merro’s stones are waking up angry. Some say there’s a Forgemaster with molten hands walking the streets.” His eyes went back to Jhace’s bandages. “Plenty say a grid rat in the Underface held a whole corner on his own.”

Jhace’s stomach tightened.

“They’re asking for you,” Bram went on. “The ones who were under that wall. Runners keep sticking their heads in here. Asking if the man who held it is ready to go again.”

“I held a span,” Jhace said. His throat tightened around the word. “It did not fall because the loads settled on another path. They want the wrong thing.”

“Doesn’t change what they want,” Bram said. He set the cup on the floor. “City’s cracked. Council is arguing in the Crown. The guilds are yelling at each other about whose task this is. Meanwhile the stone fails where it pleases. We have work.”

Jhace swung his legs over the side of the pallet. His feet hit cold stone. Tremor raced up his arms in answer to the shift.

Bram reached to steady him. Jhace shook his head once. He pushed himself upright without help. The room lurched, then steadied.

“Bad idea,” Bram muttered. “Very on-brand.”

“Show me what failed,” Jhace said.

Bram held his gaze for a few breaths, then sighed.

“Fine. But I walk beside you. If you fall, you don’t take a brace with you.”

Jhace said nothing. He bent to pull the bandages loose enough for his fingers to move. The cloth stuck in a few places; pain flared. The tremor did not care.

They stepped out into the main tunnel.

This level had become a passage more crowded than it had ever been. People moved in steady lines: runners with messages, guild workers with tools, healers with baskets. A smell of blood and hot metal hung under the usual damp stone. The grid’s hum had shifted; it ran harsher now, edges rough where pipes had taken damage.

As Jhace and Bram merged into the flow, heads turned.

“That’s him,” someone murmured. “The one from the street.”

Jhace kept his eyes on the tunnel ahead. He let their words slide off.

“Where first,” he asked.

“Nearest failures we know about.” Bram pointed river-side. “Two marry points deformed. One small support crack we can’t spare full crews for yet. Figured we’d start there. Test the grid, test your legs.”

“Test my hands,” Jhace said.

Bram’s mouth flattened.

They reached the first damaged junction after a short walk. The brace there had sheared partially away from the wall. Temporary wedges and a strap had been set to carry most of the load until a full crew could replace it. The main itself hummed steady; the bracket, not so much.

Jhace set his fingertips against the stone.

The vibration there told a clear story: stress transferred, not perfect but stable enough for now.

“We move on,” he said.

The next problem sat deeper, in a lower run where the air grew hotter. A side support there had cracked along its base. Someone had braced it with a stack of loose stone, but the contact was poor; gaps showed.

“No one under this line?” Jhace asked.

“Not if they listened,” Bram said. “Street above already cleared.”

Jhace studied the crack. Not immediate collapse. Not harmless either. A failure here in the next Tide would drop a section of tunnel and weaken the wall of the building over it.

It would be days before the guild could spare enough workers.

He put his palm on the broken base.

Stone met skin. Cool under the surface, warmer where it held load. He could feel the fracture: a thin gap, rough edges ready to spread. Tremor in his hand muddied the read.

He closed his eyes.

Hold, he told whatever this new thing was. Hold here. Small span. No one is under it, but they will be.

Nothing changed.

No heat surge. No copper-gold. Only the familiar push of load against his hand and the tremor in his own fingers.

He pressed harder. Pain flared in his palm, then his wrist. The crack did not answer.

Bram watched from a pace away.

“Anything?” he asked.

“No,” Jhace said.

“Good,” Bram said. “Because if you were about to tell me you can now fix every hairline scratch in this city with your glow, I was going to go lie down in traffic.”

Jhace pulled his hand back. His skin tingled from the effort, but the stone stayed unchanged.

“Not on demand,” he said.

“So we learn something,” Bram said. “Whatever that was, it’s not a new wrench you can hang on your belt. Needs more than a bad seam.”

More than a bad seam. He thought of Chloen’s leg under the beam. The faces under the building corner. His own head and chest had been full of one thing then: do not let this kill them.

He flexed his fingers. Tremor answered, unchanged.

“Mark this for priority reinforcement,” he said. “We do it by hand.”

They climbed back toward the surface.

The next failure found them before they reached open air.

A runner nearly collided with them at a junction. A boy, sweat-slick, chest heaving.

“You Jhace Torrins?” the boy gasped. “The holder?”

Jhace’s shoulders tightened.

“Yes,” Bram said, before Jhace could decide whether to argue with the word. “What’s failing?”

“Small shop front near the Merro kiln corridor,” the boy said. “Wall cracked. Healers set up inside, working triage. Master said to get the holder, or we lose half the wounded when it goes.”

Jhace didn’t look at Bram. He just started moving.

The street near the kilns was a mess.

One whole row of smaller buildings had taken impact from falling debris. Most still stood, but faces were fractured, bits of stone missing, wooden flanges splintered. Someone had chosen a shop with only one outer wall to convert into a healer’s space. The wounded lay on tables and pallets inside. The wall that separated them from the street now showed a visible crack from lintel to ground.

People crowded the doorway. Some tried to brace the wall with poles and stacked debris. The fracture deepened a grain at a time as the crowd jostled.

“Back,” Jhace said.

No one moved at first. His voice came out raw, not loud.

Bram stepped up beside him.

“You heard him,” Bram said. “Back. Give stone room to breathe.”

The crowd parted in jerks and mutters. Jhace stepped to the crack.

Up close, it looked worse. Fine dust leaked from it in a slow stream. The load path for the roof had shifted onto two narrow points instead of a full line. The shop’s inner ceiling beams rested on that.

Several wounded lay directly under them.

Jhace put his palms flat on the wall at the crack’s middle. Cold from outside, heat from bodies inside, pressure from above.

Hold, he thought. They die if you do not hold.

Heat rose under his hands. Not as sharp as under the beam or the street support, but fast enough. Copper-gold bloomed across his skin and slid into the stone, thin lines chasing the fracture up and down. They reached the lintel and the floor, then flowed sideways into the adjoining blocks.

He gritted his teeth. His arms went rigid.

“Clear under the center span,” Bram shouted. “Move the pallets outward. Go!”

Voices answered in fragments. He heard scrambling inside. Cloth dragged. Someone groaned in pain as they were shifted.

The wall pressed against his hands, trying to move. The crack wanted to widen. He could feel every point that carried too much now: bearing reduced to those two narrow contacts instead of the full length. His pulse beat inside his fingertips.

His own shake intensified. The copper-gold in his forearms lit vein-lines down to his elbows. Every heartbeat drove a fresh spike of pain along them.

“Almost,” Bram called. “Hold.”

Jhace held.

Breaths blurred. The glow spread as far as it could find stress. Then it stopped. It did not mend. It only kept the worst from moving.

“Clear,” Bram shouted at last. “Everyone out from center.”

Jhace counted three heartbeats more, then let go.

The copper-gold cut off. The wall shifted a small amount, then caught on a new angle, the crack now filled with fine stone dust and glass-bright residue. Some loose chips fell and bounced off the cleared floor. The ceiling beams groaned but stayed where they were.

Jhace’s hands dropped to his sides. His fingers clawed without his consent, opening and closing in jittering motions.

“Out,” Bram told the crowd. “No one rests under that span. You—” he pointed at a pair of workers “—get poles and a proper brace on it before next Pouring.”

Jhace stepped back until his shoulders hit the opposite wall. The world had gone slightly out of focus at the edges. Sweat dripped down his back. His hands shook hard enough that his nails clicked against his own skin.

“Your arms,” Bram said, low. “Veins went bright all the way up. Then nothing. You still with me?”

“For now,” Jhace said. His tongue felt thick.

He looked down. Faint copper-gold lines still traced his forearms, pulsing in time with the tremor. They faded slowly, but the shake did not.

“Well,” Bram said. “We learned the other half.”

“What half,” Jhace asked.

“Whatever this is,” Bram said, “it answers when people are about to get crushed. It does not answer when you want to fix a crack for later. And every time it listens, it takes more out of you.”

Jhace didn’t answer. He stared at his hands until the copper traces vanished, leaving only red skin and the unsteady twitch.

Someone cleared their throat to his left.

The woman from the street stood there. The one with glass dust in her hair and the logbook clutched to her chest. She had cleaned up some since he’d seen her last; the dust now lined her clothes instead of her face. Her eyes were sharper than before.

“Jhace Torrins,” she said.

He narrowed his eyes.

“You know my name.”

“You shouted it at the wall yesterday,” she said. “Bram yelled it three times. And half the runners are using it now.” She looked at his hands. “May I see where you touched?”

“No,” he said.

Her brows lifted a fraction.

“I need to understand what changed in the stone,” she said. “People are setting up triage in buildings you’ve… supported. If I know where it’s strongest, I can tell them where not to lie down.”

Jhace exhaled through his teeth. That, at least, was an argument he could not throw aside.

He turned back to the cracked wall and set one shaking finger against the point where the glow had felt thickest. The motion was clumsy. His fingertip missed twice before finding the line.

“Here,” he said. “And here.” He traced the path down and across as best he could.

She stepped in closer, careful not to touch him. Her gaze followed his hand, then left it, measuring the stone on its own.

“The temperature is wrong,” she murmured. “Hot along that line, cooler outside it. The stress reads different too.” She pressed her palm to the wall, eyes half closed. “Load is still carried, but the bearing area has shrunk. It shouldn’t be standing. But it is.”

“It is standing for now,” Jhace said. “Do not trust it for more than that.”

“I don’t trust anything for more than that,” she said. “But I prefer numbers to stories. Stories are already taking root about you.”

“Stories are useless,” Jhace said. “Either the brace holds or it doesn’t.”

“And if it only holds because you touch it?” she asked.

He had no answer.

She looked down at his hands again. The tremor had not eased. He tried to still his fingers; they shook anyway.

“Is that new?” she asked.

“Old injury,” he said. The lie came fast, worn from too much practice over the Tide. “Tools do that.”

“Mm,” she said. She did not sound convinced. “If you keep lying badly, you’re going to make my work harder.”

Before he could reply, someone called her name from up the street. She glanced toward the sound, then back at him.

“I’m Tiffani Merrow,” she said. “Glasswork and structural optics.” She nodded toward the wall. “I intend to make sure people don’t sleep where your grips will fail first. That requires data.”

“I have routes,” Jhace said. “I don’t have data.”

“You have both,” she said. “You just don’t write all of it down yet.”

She stepped away, already scanning the next stack of damaged fronts, already turning the scene into columns in her head. Bram watched her go with a low whistle.

“She’s going to be trouble,” he said.

Jhace opened his mouth to tell him he already had enough trouble and dropped the words.

A bent nail lay near his boot. He bent to pick it up.

His fingers missed it twice. On the third try he managed to trap it between thumb and forefinger, but the tremor jerked it free. It clinked back to stone.

He stared at it.

Bram’s gaze slid toward his hands.

Jhace straightened slowly. He put his hands behind his back, hiding the twitch.

“I’m fine,” he said.

Bram did not answer.

Across the street, Tiffani watched them. Her eyes dropped once to Jhace’s hidden hands, then back to his face. She said nothing either.

The grid hummed at its new pitch under their feet. The wall held. Jhace’s grip did not.

# Chapter 4: After the Shudder

“Doesn’t change that they ask,” Bram said. “City’s cracked. Council is shouting in the Crown. Guilds are blaming each other. The stone still fails where it wants. We have work.”

Jhace swung his legs off the pallet. His feet hit cold stone. Tremor jumped in his arms.

Bram reached to steady him. Jhace shook his head. He pushed upright without help. The room lurched and settled.

“Show me what failed,” Jhace said.

Bram studied him for a few breaths, then sighed.

“Fine. But if you fall, don’t fall into anything load-bearing.”

Jhace loosened the bandages enough for his fingers to move. Pain flared where cloth stuck. The tremor did not ease.

They stepped into the main tunnel.

It had become a busy passage. Runners with messages. Guild workers with tools. Healers with baskets. Blood and hot metal lay under the usual damp-stone smell. The grid’s hum ran rough, edges jagged where pipes had taken damage.

Heads turned as they passed.

“That’s him,” someone said. “The holder.”

Jhace kept walking.

“Where first,” he asked.

“Nearest damage we know,” Bram said. “Two deformed marry points, one small crack we can’t spare crews for yet. Good place to see what still listens to you.”

“Test my hands,” Jhace said.

“Street cleared,” Bram said. “For now.”

Not an immediate drop. Not safe either. If it failed next Tide, a span of tunnel and the wall of the building above would go with it.

Stone met skin. Cool core, warmer where it held load. He felt the fracture: a thin gap, rough edges ready to spread. His own tremor muddied the read.

Hold, he told the new heat. Hold here. Small thing.  

Nothing answered.

No surge. No glow. Only weight against his skin and the uncontrolled shake in his fingers.

He pressed harder. Pain bit into his palm. The crack stayed a crack.

“Anything?” Bram asked.

“No,” Jhace said.

“Good,” Bram said. “If you’d just told me you could fix every scratch in Saint Merro by touching it, I would have gone and lain down in a flow channel.”

Jhace pulled his hand back. His skin tingled. The stone remained the same.

“Mark it for reinforcement,” he said. “We do this one by hand.”

They started back upslope.

The next failure found them first.

A runner nearly hit them at a junction. A boy, sweat-slick, chest heaving.

“You Jhace Torrins?” he gasped. “The holder?”

Jhace’s shoulders went tight.

“Yes,” Bram said. “What’s failing?”

“Small shop near the Merro kiln corridor,” the boy said. “Wall cracked. Healers set up inside. Master says if we don’t get the holder, we lose half the wounded when it drops.”

Jhace moved before Bram could answer.

The kiln street was a mess.

A row of small fronts had taken hits from falling stone. Faces fractured, corners missing, wood supports splintered. One open-front shop had become a healer’s space. Wounded lay on tables and pallets inside. The street wall now held a visible crack from lintel to ground.

People clustered at the doorway, bracing the wall with poles and loose rubble. Each touch made the fracture deepen by grains.

“Back,” Jhace said.

No one moved.

Bram stepped up.

“You heard him,” Bram said. “Back. Give him the wall or it will take you with it.”

That got them moving. They pulled away in stutters. Jhace stepped to the crack.

Fine dust leaked from it in a slow stream. The roof load sat on two thin points instead of a full length. Ceiling beams inside rested on that line. Several pallets lay directly under them.

Jhace put both palms flat against the stone.

Cold from outside. Heat from inside. Weight from above.

Hold. They die if you do not hold.

Heat rose under his hands, quick. Copper-gold spread across his skin and slid into the wall, thin lines chasing the fracture up and down. They reached the lintel and the floor, then ran sideways into adjoining blocks.

His teeth clenched. His arms went hard.

“Clear under the center span,” Bram shouted. “Move the pallets outward. Now!”

Voices answered. Cloth scraped. Someone groaned.

The wall pressed against his hands, trying to move. The crack pushed back. He felt every point with too much load now, every place the stone had narrowed. His pulse beat at the contact.

His shake worsened. Copper-gold vein-lines lit along his forearms to the elbow. Each heartbeat sent pain down those paths.

“Almost,” Bram called. “Hold.”

Jhace held.

The glow ran as far as it could find strain. Then it stopped. It did not mend. It only kept everything in its current shallow angle.

“Clear,” Bram shouted at last. “Everyone out from under the middle.”

Jhace counted three heartbeats more, then let go.

The copper-gold cut off. The wall shifted a short span and caught on a new angle. The crack filled with fine dust and glass-bright residue. Chips dropped and bounced on empty floor. The beams groaned but stayed.

Jhace’s hands dropped. His fingers clawed and released without his say, jittering.

“Out,” Bram told the onlookers. “No one sleeps under that span. You two: poles and a proper brace before next Pouring.”

Jhace stepped back until his shoulders met the opposite wall. The edges of his vision blurred. Sweat ran cold down his back. His hands shook hard enough that his nails clicked.

“Your arms,” Bram said low. “Veins went bright, then dead. You still here?”

“For now,” Jhace said. His tongue felt thick.

He looked down. Faint copper-gold still lined his forearms, pulsing with the tremor. It faded. The shake did not.

“Well,” Bram said. “We learned the other half.”

“What half,” Jhace asked.

“Whatever this is,” Bram said, “it listens when people are about to get crushed. It doesn’t listen when you want to tidy for later. And every time it answers, it takes more out of you.”

Jhace said nothing. He stared at his hands until the copper vanished, leaving red skin and the unsteady twitch.

Someone cleared a throat.

The woman from the street stood near the cracked wall. Glass dust and copper shimmer streaked her hair. The logbook sat against her chest. Her eyes were very awake.

“Jhace Torrins,” she said.

He narrowed his gaze.

“You know my name.”

“You yelled it at the building yesterday,” she said. “Bram yelled it louder. Runners repeat it.” Her eyes dipped to his hands. “May I see where you touched?”

“No,” he said.

Her brows lifted.

“I need to know what changed in the stone,” she said. “Healers are using buildings you’ve… helped. If I know where your grip is strongest, I can tell them where not to lay anyone.”

He let air out through his teeth. That reason, he could not argue with.

He turned and lifted one shaking finger to the wall, to the point where the glow had felt thickest. The tremor made him miss twice. On the third try he put his fingertip against the right strip.

“Here,” he said. “And here.” He traced down and sideways in a rough path.

She stepped closer, careful not to touch him. Her eyes followed his marks, then shifted to her own checks. She laid her palm on the wall, then dragged it along the crack and out into intact stone.

“Temperature skewed,” she murmured. “Hot band where you pointed. Cooler outside it. Stress pattern changed too.” Her fingers pressed in small tests. “Load still carried, but only along this narrow zone. Effective bearing length is much shorter than the wall’s actual span. It should not be holding on that little area. But it is.”

“It is holding for now,” Jhace said. “Do not trust it beyond that.”

“I don’t,” she said. “I prefer numbers to stories. Stories about you are already breeding.”

“Stories are useless,” Jhace said. “Either the brace holds or it doesn’t.”

“And if it only holds when you touch it?” she asked.

He had nothing to answer with.

She looked again at his hands. The tremor still ran, steady as the grid.

“Is that new?” she asked.

“Old injury,” he said. The lie came fast. “Tools do that.”

“Mm.” No belief in it. “If you keep lying badly, you make my work harder.”

Someone called her name from farther up the street. She glanced toward the sound, then back.

“I’m Tiffani Merrow,” she said. “Glasswork. Structural optics. I intend to keep people from sleeping where your grips will fail first. That means I need your routes and what you feel in your hands turned into marks.”

“I have routes,” Jhace said. “I don’t have data.”

“You have both,” she said. “You just don’t write all of it down yet.”

She stepped away, already scanning the next damaged fronts. Bram watched her go.

“She’s going to be trouble,” he said.

Jhace started to answer and stopped.

A bent nail lay near his boot. He bent to pick it up.

His fingers missed twice. On the third try he trapped it between thumb and forefinger. The tremor jerked. The nail snapped free and clinked onto stone.

He stared at it.

Bram’s gaze slid to his hands.

Jhace straightened. He folded his hands behind his back, hiding the twitch.

“I’m fine,” he said.

Bram did not answer.

Across the street, Tiffani looked over. Her eyes dropped once to where his hands had vanished behind him, then back to his face. She said nothing.

The grid hummed at its new pitch under their feet. The wall held. Jhace’s grip did not.

[ASSISTANT]
# Chapter 4: After the Shudder  

Jhace woke on his back with stone under his shoulders and boiled herbs in his throat.

For a few breaths sound came without shape. Then it sorted: low voices, cloth moving, metal in basins. Somewhere a child crying, then quiet. No collapse roar. Only the roughened hum of the grid under everything.

His hands hurt.

He tried to flex his fingers. Tremor ran from fingertips to elbow in a fine constant shake. Pain followed, a dull burn along bone. Rough cloth bound his palms. The edges rasped.

“Stay down,” Bram said.

Jhace turned his head. He lay on a pallet in a side room off a wider tunnel. Ceiling higher than the work runs, stone blocks set regular. Two rows of makeshift beds on the floor. He recognized the top of a ladder shaft in the next chamber: Bram’s level.

Bram sat on an overturned crate a pace away. Dust streaked his hair. A dark bruise marked his cheek. He held a clay cup in both hands.

“How long,” Jhace said. The words scraped.

“Since late Striking,” Bram said. “We’re into Tide One now. You were out most of that stretch.”

Jhace pushed himself up on an elbow. The room tilted once and steadied.

“Chloen,” he said.

Bram’s mouth twitched.

“Alive,” he said. “Leg’s in bad shape, but not gone. Healer says crushed bone, torn flesh, all the words. Some of the worst breaks were already knitting when they opened it. She’s chewed enough root to sleep through a grid failure.”

Jhace’s gaze tracked past him.

Chloen lay three pallets down, under a cleaner blanket than his. Her leg was splinted from hip to ankle, wrapped in bandage cloth that showed dark stains along the middle. Her chest rose slow and even. At the edges of the wrappings, a faint copper shimmer clung to the fibers.

Jhace’s fingers twitched. Memory came in pieces: the beam, the heat in his hands, copper-gold streaking over bruised flesh.

“Do not do that,” Bram said quietly. “Do not put her on the same list as the building. She is still breathing because you refused to let that beam have her.”

Jhace looked down at his own hands. The bandages were stained with dust and a pale copper tint. Under the cloth, his fingers jerked in short, useless bursts.

“What do they say happened,” he asked.

“Out there?” Bram jerked his chin toward the tunnel. “Depends who you ask. Some say a corner of Saint Merro learned to stand on its own. Some say the Piercing cracked the rules and the stone is waking. Some say there’s a Forgemaster walking around with molten hands.” His eyes dropped to Jhace’s bandages. “Plenty say an Underface inspector held up a building.”

Jhace’s stomach tightened.

“They’re asking for you,” Bram went on. “The ones who were under that wall. Runners keep sticking their heads in. Want to know when the holder can walk.”

“I held a short span,” Jhace said. The word caught. “It did not fall because loads shifted. They ask for the wrong thing.”

“Maybe,” Bram said. “City’s still cracked. Council’s shouting in the Crown. Guilds are pointing at each other. Stone keeps failing in the middle of it. We have more to do than argue about what they call you.”

Jhace swung his legs off the pallet. Cold floor met his feet. The shift set his arm muscles jumping.

Bram reached out. Jhace shook his head once and pushed himself up without taking the hand. The walls rolled past his eyes and then settled.

“Show me what failed,” he said.

Bram studied him for a few breaths.

“Fine,” Bram said at last. “But if you fall, try not to hit anything that holds weight.”

Jhace loosened the wraps enough to let his fingers move. The cloth pulled at raw skin; pain bit up his forearms. The tremor did not care.

They stepped into the main tunnel.

The level had turned into a passage of constant motion. Runners carrying slates. Guild workers with coil and brace. Healers moving between makeshift nests with baskets of cloth and jars. Blood, hot metal, and root bitter thickened the usual damp-stone smell. The grid hum ran harsher, with edges where mains had been patched quick.

Heads turned as they passed.

“That’s him,” someone said behind them. “The holder from the kiln street.”

Jhace kept his eyes forward.

“Where first,” he asked.

“Nearest break we can reach,” Bram said. “Two marry points took damage. One side support cracked. No spare crew for a full fix yet.”

“Test,” Jhace said. “We see if that thing listens without people under it.”

The first junction had already been wedged and strapped. The main hummed steady. The brace that had sheared away vibrated on its own note. Jhace touched stone beside it.

Stress had moved into the emergency wedges. Imperfect. Stable enough.

“We move,” he said.

The cracked side support sat deeper, in hotter air. Someone had tried to help by jamming broken stone under its base. Gaps showed. The line over it fed a crowded stretch of houses.

“No one on the floors above?” Jhace asked.

“Cleared after the first tremors,” Bram said. “For now.”

The fracture line at the base ran shallow but long. If it failed during the next Tide, the tunnel and part of the building wall would go with it.

Jhace set his palm on the split.

Stone met skin. Cool away from the crack. Warmer where it carried load. He felt the thin gap and its rough edges. His own shake blurred the reading.

Hold, he thought. Hold here. No one is under you yet, but they will be.

Nothing answered.

He pressed harder. Pain flared in his palm, then his wrist. The crack remained a crack. No heat surge. No copper-gold. Only the steady push of weight and his fingers jumping.

“Anything?” Bram asked.

“No,” Jhace said.

“Good,” Bram said. “I was half afraid you were about to say you can fix every hairline in this city by frowning at it.”

Jhace pulled his hand away. His skin tingled. The stone stayed exactly as it was.

“Mark it,” he said. “We reinforce it with tools.”

They turned upslope.

They did not reach the surface before the next break found them.

A runner shot around a bend and nearly collided with them. A boy, sweat bright on his skin, breath tearing.

“You Jhace Torrins?” he gasped. “The one who held the building?”

Jhace’s shoulders went tight.

“Yes,” Bram said. “What’s failing?”

“Shop near the Merro kiln corridor,” the boy said. “Healers using it. Wall cracked. They say if we don’t get you, we lose half the wounded when it goes.”

Jhace started moving before Bram answered.

The kiln street looked worse than it had when they’d first climbed out.

Dust still hung in the air. A whole row of small fronts showed new cracks and missing corners. Someone had turned a narrow shop into a triage room; pallets and tables filled the inside space. The wall facing the street carried a thick fracture from lintel to ground.

People crowded the doorway. Two workers braced the wall with poles. Every shove made fine dust leak faster.

“Back,” Jhace said.

His voice came rough and low. It did not clear the crowd.

Bram stepped up beside him.

“Back,” Bram repeated, louder. “Give him room, or you can lie under the stone when it comes down.”

The crowd shifted, grudging but real. Jhace stepped to the crack.

Closer, it was worse. The roof load had shifted onto two narrow contact points instead of the full length of wall. Inside, ceiling beams sat on that broken line. Several pallets lay under the center span.

Jhace placed both palms flat on the stone.

Cold outer face. Heat from wounded bodies and lamps inside. Heavy pressure from above.

Hold, he thought. They die if you do not hold.

Heat rose under his skin at once. Copper-gold slid across his hands and into the wall, fine streams chasing the fracture up and down. The glow reached the lintel, the base, then spread sideways into the adjoining blocks.

His jaw locked. His arms went rigid.

“Clear the center,” Bram shouted. “Move the pallets to the sides. No arguments.”

Voices and movement answered from inside. Cloth dragged. Someone cried out as they were shifted, then grunted and went quiet.

The wall pressed into his hands, trying to widen the crack. He felt every point where load had narrowed, every place the stone barely held. His pulse beat into the contact, hard enough to make his fingers sting.

His shake worsened. Copper-gold lit the veins along his forearms, bright down to the elbow. Each heartbeat drove pain down those lines.

“Almost,” Bram called. “Hold.”

Jhace held.

The glow ran as far as it could find strain and stopped. It did not heal. It only pinned the worst angles in place.

“Clear,” Bram shouted. “No one under the middle. Out.”

Jhace counted three heartbeats more, then let go.

The copper-gold cut off. The wall shifted a short span and caught on a new lean. Dust dribbled. Loose chips fell and bounced on bare floor. The beams above groaned but stayed set.

Jhace’s hands dropped. His fingers clawed and opened in fast, useless grabs.

“Out,” Bram told the street. “No pallets under that span. You two—brace it with poles and proper wedges before next Pouring.”

Jhace stepped back until his shoulders met the opposite wall. Sweat ran cold down his back. The edges of his vision went soft. His hands shook hard enough that his nails clicked together.

“Your arms lit,” Bram said, low. “Vein-lines all the way up. You still with me?”

“For now,” Jhace said. His tongue felt thick.

He looked down. Faint copper-gold lines still traced his forearms, pulsing with the tremor. They faded. The shake did not.

“Well,” Bram said. “We learned the other half.”

“What half,” Jhace asked.

“Whatever this is,” Bram said, “answers when people are about to get crushed. It ignores you when you want the nice clean fixes. And every time it answers, it takes more out of you.”

Jhace stared at his hands until the last trace of color vanished, leaving raw skin and an unsteady twitch.

Someone cleared a throat.

The woman from the street stood near the cracked wall. Glass dust and copper shimmer threaded her hair. A logbook pressed against her chest. Her gaze moved from his hands to the stone.

“Jhace Torrins,” she said.

He narrowed his eyes.

“You know my name,” he said.

“You shouted it yesterday,” she said. “Bram shouted it louder. Runners carried it. May I see where you touched?”

“No,” Jhace said.

She watched him for a breath.

“I need to know what changed in the wall,” she said. “Healers are using buildings you have… helped. If I know where your grip holds strongest, I can tell them where not to put beds.”

That, Jhace could not argue with.

He turned back to the wall. One shaking finger rose to the point where the glow had felt thickest. The tremor made him miss twice. On the third try he hit the right strip.

“Here,” he said. “Then down to here, and across.”

She stepped closer, careful not to brush him. Her hand followed his path, then moved on, checking the stone in small presses.

“Hot band along your line,” she murmured. “Cooler outside it. Stress is concentrated there. Load still carried, but the effective bearing length is small compared to the wall’s full span. It should not stand on that narrow zone. But it does.”

“It stands for now,” Jhace said. “Do not trust it past that.”

“I do not trust anything past that,” she said. “But I prefer numbers to stories. Stories about you are already spreading.”

“Stories are useless,” Jhace said. “Either the brace holds or it does not.”

“And if it only holds when you are here?” she asked.

He had no answer.

Her eyes dropped to his hands. The tremor still ran, fine and constant.

“Is that new?” she asked.

“Old injury,” he said. “Tools.”

She made a quiet sound that was not agreement.

“If you keep lying this badly,” she said, “you make my work harder.”

A voice called from farther up the street.

She glanced that way, then back.

“I am Tiffani Merrow,” she said. “Glasswork and structural optics. I intend to keep people away from the first places your grips will fail. I need your routes and what you feel when you touch stone turned into marks.”

“I have routes,” Jhace said. “I do not have data.”

“You have both,” she said. “You just leave some of it in your bones.”

She moved away, already reading the next damaged fronts. Bram watched her go.

“She will be trouble,” he said.

Jhace did not answer.

A bent nail lay near his boot. He bent and reached for it.

His fingers missed twice. On the third try he caught it between thumb and forefinger. The tremor snapped. The nail jumped from his grip and tapped back onto stone.

He stared at it.

Bram’s gaze slid to his hands.

Jhace straightened. He folded his hands behind his back, hiding the twitch.

“I am fine,” he said.

Bram said nothing.

Across the street, Tiffani looked over. Her eyes dropped once to his hidden hands, then back to his face. She held his gaze for a breath, then turned away.

The grid hummed at its new pitch under their feet. The wall held. Jhace’s grip did not.

## Chapter 5: Lines That Don’t Lie

Tiffani had to rebuild her map of the street before she could trust what she saw. [file:0a2311af-b53e-4e20-9d0d-c7854ffa99a5][file:3f75f15a-1ea1-4a1a-afd2-6fe88e6bada9]

The kiln corridor had shifted in ways her mind kept trying to refuse. Shop fronts she knew at one spacing now sat closer together, stone seams crushed. A drainage channel she had measured three Tides ago no longer ran straight; it curved where the paving had sunk in a shallow arc. Cracks traced along the walls in a pattern that was not random. They bent toward a single node where load had once moved cleanly through the district. [file:adcc755e-2d4d-4e6c-9f5a-fade1acfde28][file:43bdf5f2-3f0e-4f8f-a81b-ece575e6600f]

She stood in the middle of the broken street with her logbook open, ink already drying in its last column from the previous Tide. The air still held fine dust and the metallic tang that had come with the Piercing. Her instruments were gone with the observatory, but she still had a straight-edge, a length of chalk, and her own eyes. [file:3f75f15a-1ea1-4a1a-afd2-6fe88e6bada9][file:c6e86552-223c-4995-a5d2-2b11adae00ca]

“Ref point,” she murmured to herself. “Merro shop lintel, old level. Drop from there.” [file:3f75f15a-1ea1-4a1a-afd2-6fe88e6bada9]

She marked the old lintel height on the page from memory, then the new one from sight. The difference wasn’t much in span terms, but stone did not have to move far before it hurt people. She sketched the visible cracks, then overlaid the remembered map of main supports beneath the street. The lines did not match. They curved around a gap that had not existed before the white mark cut the sky. [file:0a2311af-b53e-4e20-9d0d-c7854ffa99a5][file:adcc755e-2d4d-4e6c-9f5a-fade1acfde28]

She turned a slow circle, counting under her breath. Every third crack showed the same faint copper shimmer she had seen under the building when the man with the shaking hands touched it. Those lines stopped abruptly, never reaching full contact lengths. Effective bearing zones, she wrote in the margin. Too small for safety. [file:9a4d8dfe-4d92-4951-b01e-4468bbe871ed][file:43bdf5f2-3f0e-4f8f-a81b-ece575e6600f]

“Merrow!” [file:08b7f1e8-8b2b-47bf-b279-b9c04853c2ba]

The shout came from up-slope. Tiffani finished the last stroke of a crack and looked up. Two figures in guild vests picked their way through the rubble, followed by a man with the RRO’s blue sash at his shoulder. The sash was dusty but still neatly tied. The guild marks were hers: Merro Glass. She resisted the urge to wipe her own hands on her tunic before they saw how dirty they already were. [file:c6e86552-223c-4995-a5d2-2b11adae00ca][file:04c084e9-c169-4161-b5fe-2389183ec6ff]

“Master Merrow,” the taller of the guild pair called again when he drew close enough not to shout. “You’re a hard woman to find.” [file:08b7f1e8-8b2b-47bf-b279-b9c04853c2ba]

“Stones keep moving,” she said. “I keep following.” [file:3f75f15a-1ea1-4a1a-afd2-6fe88e6bada9]

He grimaced, glancing at the cracked facades and the copper-smudged seams. “Yes. About that.” He nodded toward the RRO man. “Inspector Harlen. Came to the guild. Asked for someone who can read breaks. We told him you were already in the mess.” [file:04c084e9-c169-4161-b5fe-2389183ec6ff][file:adcc755e-2d4d-4e6c-9f5a-fade1acfde28]

Harlen stepped forward, hand on the edge of his sash, as if to remind them it was there. He took in her open logbook, the chalk smears on her fingers, and the line she had drawn from wall to street. His gaze paused for a breath on the copper glints along one crack. [file:c6e86552-223c-4995-a5d2-2b11adae00ca]

“Master Merrow,” he said. “The RRO is assembling a committee to survey structural hazards post-Piercing. You were recommended. We would prefer your attention on that instead of...” He gestured loosely at her page. “Independent mapping.” [file:0a2311af-b53e-4e20-9d0d-c7854ffa99a5][file:04c084e9-c169-4161-b5fe-2389183ec6ff]

Tiffani shut the logbook halfway, thumb marking her place. “The city is already performing its own survey,” she said. “Cracks mark the map for us. I am only drawing what stone has decided.” [file:3f75f15a-1ea1-4a1a-afd2-6fe88e6bada9]

Harlen’s jaw tightened at the word decided, but he let it pass. “The difference,” he said, “is that a coordinated effort can prioritize repairs. We need lists, not personal notebooks. The Crown wants numbers. How many supports compromised. How many households at risk. If you join formally, you will have resources. Assistants. Access to full plans.” [file:04c084e9-c169-4161-b5fe-2389183ec6ff][file:2db39d5a-8660-49cb-80cc-aacf669012c5]

“And constraints,” she said. “Assignment orders. Approved routes.” [file:2db39d5a-8660-49cb-80cc-aacf669012c5]

“Guidelines,” he corrected. “We must manage panic as much as stone. If every craftsman follows their own sense of urgency, we lose coherence. The RRO needs your precision, Master Merrow. In a framework.” [file:c6e86552-223c-4995-a5d2-2b11adae00ca]

She looked back at the nearest crack instead of his face. It bent away from where the official street plan claimed the main support should run. It bent toward the spot where the man with copper-gold hands had braced the wall the previous Tide. The heat there had not faded fully. Her palm still felt a slight temperature rise when she placed it on the stone. [file:9a4d8dfe-4d92-4951-b01e-4468bbe871ed]

“You will prioritize by district,” she said. “By tax value. By how loud the Council screams.” [file:c6e86552-223c-4995-a5d2-2b11adae00ca]

Harlen did not deny it. “We will prioritize where failure risks the greatest loss,” he said. “Load density, population, critical services. The Crown district feeds more mains than the Dregs. A collapse there—” [file:c2df7e75-e586-4cb9-b2d0-7cb6c5a2effe][file:04c084e9-c169-4161-b5fe-2389183ec6ff]

“Kills different names,” she said. “Not more bodies.” [file:08b7f1e8-8b2b-47bf-b279-b9c04853c2ba]

One of the guild men shifted his weight. “Tiffani,” he said, dropping the formality. “The workshop needs you too. Orders backed up. People want replacement glazing, safe mirrors, anything that feels less broken than this.” He gestured at the street. “You could move your bench up-slope for a few Tides. Let the RRO handle the cracks and come back when the shouting dies down.” [file:f95a9974-0111-4675-b8e0-c475881d1b12][file:04c084e9-c169-4161-b5fe-2389183ec6ff]

There it was: the easier line. Make pretty things. Replace what smashed on other people’s heads. Pretend the pattern had not been visible in the glass before it broke. [file:3f75f15a-1ea1-4a1a-afd2-6fe88e6bada9]

She saw Marisse in her mind the way she had found her after that earlier failure, under a sheet of glass that had not been rated for the loads Tiffani had waved away as unlikely. The notes in her old logbook where she had written noise in the margin and turned the page. [file:08b7f1e8-8b2b-47bf-b279-b9c04853c2ba]

“I have already watched one pattern until it killed someone,” she said. “I will not do that again because it is tidier for the Crown.” [file:08b7f1e8-8b2b-47bf-b279-b9c04853c2ba]

Harlen’s expression cooled. “Refusing a formal role means you will have no say when we set priorities,” he said. “You can shout from the street, but decisions will be made in the chambers.” [file:c6e86552-223c-4995-a5d2-2b11adae00ca]

“Then I will give you numbers from here,” she said. “You want lists? I will send lists. Holds, durations, estimated loads. Copper residue bands. You can stack them against your council minutes and see which one predicts who lives.” [file:43bdf5f2-3f0e-4f8f-a81b-ece575e6600f][file:9a4d8dfe-4d92-4951-b01e-4468bbe871ed]

He stared at her logbook, then back at her. “You are making this harder than it needs to be, Master Merrow.” [file:2db39d5a-8660-49cb-80cc-aacf669012c5]

“It was hard the moment the sky split,” she said. “Everything since is just choosing where to stand.” [file:c6e86552-223c-4995-a5d2-2b11adae00ca]

Harlen exhaled through his nose, a short, contained sound. “Very well. The offer stands if you change your mind. Send your lists to the RRO hall if you insist on working alone. We will—review them.” He did not quite promise to act. [file:04c084e9-c169-4161-b5fe-2389183ec6ff]

He nodded to the guild men and turned away, boots crunching on broken stone. They followed, one of them giving Tiffani a last, pleading look that said workshop, pay, simpler problems. She let it slide off. [file:f95a9974-0111-4675-b8e0-c475881d1b12]

For a few breaths, the street noise filled back in around her: hammer strikes, shouted measurements, a cart wheel complaining over a new ridge. She reopened the logbook and added a new header on a blank page. [file:3f75f15a-1ea1-4a1a-afd2-6fe88e6bada9]

Post-Piercing Holds, she wrote. Source unknown. [file:9a4d8dfe-4d92-4951-b01e-4468bbe871ed]

Under it, she drew a narrow rectangle for the healer’s wall she had just inspected. She marked the hot band along Jhace’s touch line, shaded the cooler stone outside it, and noted effective support width less than half original span. Survived initial event. Unsafe for long-term occupancy. [file:43bdf5f2-3f0e-4f8f-a81b-ece575e6600f][file:9a4d8dfe-4d92-4951-b01e-4468bbe871ed]

Then she turned the page again and began a table: Location, apparent load, visible copper, estimated hold duration, observed cost on subject. The last column stayed mostly blank for now. She had seen his hands shake and the vein-lines glow, but she did not yet have counts or Tides between use and failure. She meant to get them. [file:9a4d8dfe-4d92-4951-b01e-4468bbe871ed][file:2db39d5a-8660-49cb-80cc-aacf669012c5]

Pella found her three spans later, legs gray with dust up to the knee. Her apprentice carried two blankets and a satchel that bulged with wrapped bread and stoppered jars. [file:08b7f1e8-8b2b-47bf-b279-b9c04853c2ba]

“You weren’t at the workshop,” Pella said without preamble. “So I followed the shouting. Also the runners saying ‘the glass woman with the book is down there.’ Eat.” She thrust a wrapped parcel toward Tiffani’s chest. [file:3f75f15a-1ea1-4a1a-afd2-6fe88e6bada9]

Tiffani caught it one-handed, barely saving her logbook from the same impact. “You are very polite today,” she said. “Did the world ending soften you?” [file:3f75f15a-1ea1-4a1a-afd2-6fe88e6bada9]

“No,” Pella said. “The world ending made you forget to chew. Someone has to stop you from turning into a chalk sketch.” She glanced at the latest diagram. “That a new one?” [file:08b7f1e8-8b2b-47bf-b279-b9c04853c2ba]

“Yes,” Tiffani said. “Healer’s wall. Held by our new variable.” She tapped the copper-shaded band. “Load concentrated along here. Temperature elevated. It should not stand with that little bearing length, but it does.” [file:9a4d8dfe-4d92-4951-b01e-4468bbe871ed]

Pella squinted. “New variable being the man with the light in his hands?” [file:08b7f1e8-8b2b-47bf-b279-b9c04853c2ba]

Tiffani broke the bread parcel open with a thumb, more for something to do than from hunger. “Yes,” she said. “Subject: Jhace Torrins. Underface inspector. Currently pretending his hands always shake.” [file:08b7f1e8-8b2b-47bf-b279-b9c04853c2ba][file:43bdf5f2-3f0e-4f8f-a81b-ece575e6600f]

Pella’s eyebrows rose. “You got his name.” [file:08b7f1e8-8b2b-47bf-b279-b9c04853c2ba]

“He yelled it at a wall,” Tiffani said. “His friend yelled it louder. The city does not keep its miracles quiet.” [file:f95a9974-0111-4675-b8e0-c475881d1b12]

She took a bite. The bread tasted of ash and root, but it steadied her enough to keep talking.

“He touches a failing point,” she said around the mouthful. “Copper-gold spreads along stress lines. Heat rises. For a short window, the structure behaves as if its failure thresholds moved. Not erased, just… shifted. Enough to let people flee.” She pointed with the crust at her table header. “I intend to know how long those windows last, and how narrow they are.” [file:9a4d8dfe-4d92-4951-b01e-4468bbe871ed]

Pella watched her for a breath. “So,” she said slowly. “The guild is asking you to polish glass for rich people. The RRO is asking you to sit at a table and write lists for them. And you want to chase a shaking man around broken streets and count how much he is breaking himself to keep other people alive.” [file:f95a9974-0111-4675-b8e0-c475881d1b12][file:2db39d5a-8660-49cb-80cc-aacf669012c5]

“Yes,” Tiffani said. “That sums it.” [file:3f75f15a-1ea1-4a1a-afd2-6fe88e6bada9]

Pella huffed air that might have been a laugh if it had more strength. “Then I will bring more bread,” she said. “And extra ink. You are going to need both.” [file:08b7f1e8-8b2b-47bf-b279-b9c04853c2ba]

She set the second blanket on a low wall, then moved off to help a group of workers shift debris away from a still-occupied doorway. Tiffani watched her go, feeling the small knot in her chest that always eased when Pella was within sight. Then she bent back over the page. [file:08b7f1e8-8b2b-47bf-b279-b9c04853c2ba][file:f95a9974-0111-4675-b8e0-c475881d1b12]

She added a note at the bottom of the table: Hypothesis: effect tied to protective intention, not simple will. Activation only when lives in immediate danger. Cost accumulative in subject. Data incomplete. [file:9a4d8dfe-4d92-4951-b01e-4468bbe871ed]

The next hours blurred into a pattern of walk, measure, mark. She visited three more sites where witnesses swore they had seen copper-gold in the stone. At one, the residue had already cooled. At another, the glow still clung faintly, enough to make her skin prickle when she held her palm near it. Each time, she sketched the cracks, traced the probable load paths, and compared them to what she knew of standard construction. Each time, the numbers came back with the same message: the holds were inefficient, messy, and barely enough—and still better than collapse. [file:9a4d8dfe-4d92-4951-b01e-4468bbe871ed][file:adcc755e-2d4d-4e6c-9f5a-fade1acfde28]

Late in Pouring, she claimed a corner of a mostly intact shop whose owner had given up selling anything that day and turned his counter into a writing surface. She spread her pages out, weighting corners with bits of broken masonry. Outside, the street still rumbled with carts and shouted orders. Inside, she had three paces of relative quiet. [file:adcc755e-2d4d-4e6c-9f5a-fade1acfde28][file:43bdf5f2-3f0e-4f8f-a81b-ece575e6600f]

She began listing patterns.

- All confirmed holds occurred with people directly in harm’s path.
- No holds recorded yet on empty structures.
- Copper intensity correlated roughly with number of people saved, not with total building mass.
- Subject cost shook her pen enough to dot the margin. [file:9a4d8dfe-4d92-4951-b01e-4468bbe871ed]

“Not sustainable,” she said under her breath. “Not at that load.” [file:3f75f15a-1ea1-4a1a-afd2-6fe88e6bada9]

She circled the word cost three times.

When she lifted her head, her neck ached and the light outside the doorway had shifted color. Voices in the street had changed note as well—from the sharp edges of immediate crisis to the flatter, strained marks of people settling into the idea that this was not ending in a single Tide. The grid’s hum under the floor still sat at its new, roughened pitch. [file:c2df7e75-e586-4cb9-b2d0-7cb6c5a2effe]

She closed the logbook and pressed the leather cover against her sternum for a breath. This, she thought, is the work. Not the committee rooms. Not the polished glass. Find the lines that do not lie and follow them, wherever they cut through guild comfort. [file:04c084e9-c169-4161-b5fe-2389183ec6ff][file:f95a9974-0111-4675-b8e0-c475881d1b12]

When she stepped back into the street, her eyes went at once to the healer’s wall. Workers had added poles and wedges just where she had marked load risk earlier. Someone had listened to that much, at least. The crack still held its new lean. No fresh dust drifted from it. [file:adcc755e-2d4d-4e6c-9f5a-fade1acfde28]

Beyond it, a small knot of people had gathered near another run entrance. Their attention all pointed the same direction. Tiffani followed their gaze. [file:43bdf5f2-3f0e-4f8f-a81b-ece575e6600f]

Jhace Torrins stood there with Bram at his shoulder.

He looked worse in full light than he had when he was pressed against the broken wall the previous Tide. The tremor in his hands showed even at this distance, fingers making small, constant corrections as if the air itself unbalanced them. His shoulders carried the set of someone who had already done more than his body approved of and planned to do more anyway. [file:08b7f1e8-8b2b-47bf-b279-b9c04853c2ba][file:9a4d8dfe-4d92-4951-b01e-4468bbe871ed]

He reached down for something at his feet—a tool, maybe, or a scrap of metal. He missed the first time. The second. On the third attempt he caught it, only to have it jolt free when his hand jumped. He straightened with his hands tucked behind his back, the motion too smooth to be unplanned. Bram’s face turned toward him with a look she recognized: concern wrapped in stubborn patience. [file:0a2311af-b53e-4e20-9d0d-c7854ffa99a5][file:08b7f1e8-8b2b-47bf-b279-b9c04853c2ba]

Tiffani flipped her logbook open again and made one last note on the cost column: Subject unable to perform fine grip on small object post-hold. Hiding symptom. [file:9a4d8dfe-4d92-4951-b01e-4468bbe871ed]

Then she shut the book, tucked it under her arm, and started toward him.

Data, she thought. And choices. If the city insisted on growing stories around him, someone needed to anchor those stories to numbers before they led him into holds that would tear him apart. [file:43bdf5f2-3f0e-4f8f-a81b-ece575e6600f][file:2db39d5a-8660-49cb-80cc-aacf669012c5]

She walked with measured steps, counting cracks as she went. When she reached him, she would ask pointed questions. And this time, she would not let him dodge them with one-word answers and bad lies about old injuries.
