{"name":"N01 — Chapters 1–5 (Compiled)","type":"document","content":"# Chapter 1: Something Wrong\n\nJhace eased down the last rung and put both boots on the damp stone ledge. The Underface took the sound of him and gave back the steady thrum of the heatgrid lines running through the dark.\n\nHe stood still until his breath slowed. His palm brushed the nearest pipe, not to warm his hand, but to read the vibration through skin and bone. The pipe carried heat from upslope down into the older districts, and it carried the city's moods with it: a stable thrum when joints were tight, a rasp when a hanger had slipped, a soft chatter when sediment had started to collect.\n\nJhace moved on a practiced path, lantern hooded low so the light stayed close. He checked the marry points in order, touching metal, then stone, then the iron braces that bit into the wall. Each contact gave him an answer and a memory at the same time—how the joint had sounded last Tide, how the pipe had settled after the last patch, how much slack the bracket allowed before it turned from tolerance into danger.\n\nA voice drifted down from somewhere above, muffled through grates and packed dirt.\n\n\"Jhace!\" Bram's tone carried a grin even when the words were blurred by distance. \"You still breathing down there, or you finally decided to marry the grid and settle?\"\n\nJhace didn't look up. \"Work first.\"\n\nAnother voice followed, thinner, eager. Chloen. \"Master Kelter says you can tell a cracked pipe by the way the air tastes.\"\n\nJhace paused with his hand on a brace. He could hear them moving topside, boots on planks, the scrape of a tool box. Bram would be leaning over the grate just to make sure he was heard. Chloen would be leaning farther.\n\n\"Your master says many things,\" Jhace called back. \"Keep your weight off the grate.\"\n\n\"Yes, Master Jhace,\" Chloen said, quick and proud.\n\nJhace kept walking. He didn't bring apprentices into the Underface. Not because they were useless. Because they were alive. The tunnels took mistakes and kept them.\n\nThe route took him through six more junctions without incident. At the seventh—a marry point where three branch lines split from the main—the hum shifted.\n\nNot a crack. Not the high squeal of metal pulling away from stone. The pipe looked clean where his lantern light struck it—no wet line, no rust bloom, no visible bow. But the vibration under his fingertips wasn't centered. The resonance rode too close to the edge of the metal, as if the load had moved without permission.\n\nHe pressed his ear near the brace. The sound came back wrong again, faintly doubled. A join that was starting to talk to itself.\n\nJhace pulled his ledger from his coat and held it against his knee. The page was already crowded with his tight marks: replacements he'd argued for, patches he'd had to accept, notes on districts where the stone had begun to sweat with stress. He added this junction without hesitation.\n\nThe decision came with its own weight. Shutting down a section of line in Kindling meant cold rooms by Quench. Complaints. A foreman with a polished collar telling him the grid had survived worse. Bram would back him in public and tease him in private. Chloen would watch and learn the wrong lesson if the foreman won.\n\nJhace stared at the brace until his eyes blurred at the edges. There had been times, Turns ago, when he'd chosen convenience. He'd told himself a sound was only a sound, that stone always settled, that the tunnel would be there next Tide. He still carried all twenty-three names from that collapse—every one—even if he never spoke them.\n\nHe capped his ink. He ran his thumb along the seam where brace met pipe, feeling for the slightest shift. Nothing moved. The wrongness stayed anyway.\n\nJhace turned and started back toward the ladder. He would pull the section. He would take the foreman's anger and keep the grid from deciding for him.\n\nAs he walked, the Underface hum settled into its usual pattern for three junctions. Then the entire tunnel gave a tiny tremor that wasn't from any single pipe. It came through stone and metal together, shallow at first, then gone.\n\nJhace stopped so fast his lantern swung. He put his palm flat against the wall. The stone felt steady, but his bones remembered the ripple.\n\nIt hadn't come from his junction. It hadn't come from one district. It had come from everywhere at once.\n\nHe listened, breath held tight in his chest. Somewhere above, Bram laughed at something Chloen said, and the sound carried down without knowing it was already late.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 2: Glass That Listens\n\nTiffani checked the instruments in order. Always in order.\n\nThe mounted theodolite first: brass arcs clean, sighting tube clear, clamps tight. She tapped the central spindle with two fingertips and watched the pointer settle on the etched baseline mark in the stone. No drift.\n\nThe logbook stand sat to her left, open to a fresh page. The facing sheet held tight script from the last several Tides: phase, cloud bands, refraction spread, color shift, anomaly class. Three entries were circled. She noted them without letting her attention stick.\n\nPrism rack at the platform’s edge: each glass piece resting in its cradle. She lifted one, tilted it into the light, checked for hairlines and ripples. None. The air held a faint trace of kiln-smoke from the workshop below. Everything where it belonged.\n\nExcept the numbers said otherwise.\n\n“Kindling, refraction pass,” she murmured. “Baseline confirmation.”\n\nShe leaned to the theodolite, eye to the tube. The upper atmosphere sat in neat rings behind the crosshairs. She swept slowly, matching fixed stars to the etched scale, searching for deviation. A quarter subdivision high. Half low.\n\nOnce, she would have called that noise and moved on. She did not call it noise now.\n\nTiffani straightened and reached for the logbook.\n\nTide 0, Kindling mid-span. Upper rings nominal. Minor fluctuation at star-line Seven-Three. Repeat of last six Tides within one subdivision.\n\nPattern appears persistent.\n\nShe underlined persistent. Persistent meant repeatable. Repeatable meant real. Real meant it could reach a person who depended on glass to be honest.\n\nIf it was real, why hadn’t anyone else written it down? Because no one else was assigned to sit on this platform and count these shifts. Because the guild said she was here to test lens clarity for Crown commissions, not watch the sky for trouble.\n\nBootsteps on the stair broke the thought.\n\n“Merrow?” A man’s voice rose through the hatch. Too brisk to be Pella. “Merrow, you up there?”\n\n“Yes,” she called. “Platform is in use. Come through carefully; mind the stands.”\n\nThe hatch pushed open. Two guild messengers edged onto the observatory, seals bright at their throats. Their boots landed too close to a tripod foot.\n\n“The ovens need you,” the older one said. “Master Corlis wants to know when his council panes will be cooled and measured. Promised by Quench, he says. You’re up here staring at clouds.”\n\n“Upper rings,” Tiffani said. Her tone stayed level. “Refraction patterns. The ovens are within schedule. If temperature holds range, the panes move to annealing before Pouring.”\n\n“Then go check them again.” The younger messenger glanced over the instruments with quick, shallow interest. “You were hired for precision glass, not sky work.”\n\nEfficient phrasing. Unhelpful.\n\n“It isn’t a pastime,” Tiffani said. “The pattern has repeated across more than a full set of Tides. The refraction shift is small and compounding. If it continues, it will affect long-range lenses, signal mirrors, tower fires—”\n\n“Has it broken anything yet?” the younger asked.\n\n“No.”\n\n“Then it isn’t our task yet,” the older said. His hands spread in a weary, reasonable gesture. “We work on what has already failed. That is what keeps people warm.”\n\nCorlexi said something close to that once in council: priorities, efficiency, visible damage first. She had gone home afterward and realigned half the workshop’s instruments instead of sleeping.\n\nThe messengers watched her, waiting for something simple.\n\n“The ovens are stable,” she said. “Tell Master Corlis I will meet the promised span range. If he wishes to count each breath between now and then, he can climb up and help.”\n\nThe younger almost smiled. The older huffed.\n\n“Fine,” he said. “Just remember who pays for this platform.”\n\nThey left, boots heavy on the stair.\n\nWhen the hatch closed, the air on the platform felt easier to pull in.\n\n“Who pays isn’t the same as who needs,” she said under her breath. “Everyone needs the sky to behave.”\n\nShe turned back to the theodolite and set the angle to Seven-Three. Outer band. The spot she had circled enough times that her hands could find it without checking the scale.\n\nShe leaned in. Sighted. The star should have met the crosshair where it had met it every prior pass.\n\nIt missed by more than a subdivision this time. Two full marks and a fraction.\n\nShe blinked. Reset. Checked the lens: no dust, no smear. Checked the stand’s feet: all three flat on stone, no grit under the pads. Nothing loose.\n\nHer pulse picked up. Not fear. Not yet. A pattern gaining speed.\n\n“Repeat,” she told herself. “If it repeats under controlled adjustment, it’s real. If not, find your own error first.”\n\nShe rotated the scope through a full sweep, then came back to Seven-Three. High again. Same offset.\n\nShe wrote with more pressure than before.\n\nDeviation at Seven-Three increased to +2 subdivisions. Instrument base confirmed stable.\n\nTentative conclusion: source external to instrument.\n\nShe underlined tentative twice. A reminder not to run ahead of the data. That habit had a cost.\n\nThe hatch creaked again.\n\n“Master Tiffani?” Pella’s head appeared, curls frizzed from kiln heat. A covered bowl sat balanced in one hand, the other tight on the rail.\n\n“You’ve been up here since Kindling opened. I brought stew.”\n\n“That was not necessary,” Tiffani said.\n\nPella eased onto the platform, placing her feet between stand legs with practiced care. “You say that every Tide. I have gathered evidence. It’s a stable pattern.”\n\n“Dangerous habit,” Tiffani said. “People who notice patterns get tied to impossible mentors.”\n\n“Everyone downstairs says you’re impossible,” Pella said. “I think they lack resolution.” Her gaze dipped to the logbook. “Anything useful?”\n\n“Useful isn’t usually the goal.” Tiffani hesitated. “But yes. The outer refraction ring is drifting. Slowly. Enough to matter for long-distance work if it continues.”\n\n“Matter how?”\n\n“Signal clarity. Star charts. Navigation lenses.” Tiffani flexed ink-stained fingers. “If fixed points slide, errors spread. Ships miss their bearings. Beacon codes blur.”\n\n“Are you going to tell the council?” Pella asked.\n\n“When I have more than two points of drift and some glass humming,” Tiffani said. “For now, I watch. And accept stew.”\n\n“That last part improves all work.”\n\nPella set the bowl on the lower shelf, careful not to touch any tools. Tiffani took the spoon. The stew was warm, a little too salty, stretched from yesterday’s pot. Heat spread through her chest and eased the tightness under her ribs.\n\n“Thank you,” she said.\n\n“You’re welcome.” Pella waited until Tiffani had swallowed three spoonfuls, then nodded, satisfied. “I’ll come back before Pouring. If you haven’t moved, I reserve the right to complain louder than Master Corlis.”\n\n“You already do,” Tiffani said.\n\nPella left with a small grin, closing the hatch.\n\nAlone again, the city sound rose: hammer on metal from Underface forges, distant market calls, the deep exhale of the main kiln below as it vented. All ordinary.\n\nTiffani set the bowl aside and crouched to press her palm to the stone under the theodolite feet. Cool. Steady. No tremor. The wrongness wasn’t in the platform.\n\n“Noise killed Marisse,” she said quietly. “Or my choice to call signal noise.”\n\nThat oven wall had whispered for half an Anneal: small heat shifts, fine cracks still buried under glaze. Marisse had asked if it worried her. Tiffani had said no, to keep schedule, to sound certain. The investigation had named it material fatigue. Tiffani had named it the cost of assuming uncertainty meant safety.\n\nShe stood and returned her eye to the scope. The deviation had grown again. Seven-Three sat higher. The next star along the ring had shifted too. Together they marked the start of a curve through the upper band. When she traced along that implied path, other stars near it showed slight smear. Brightness stretched by fractions.\n\nSomething was pulling at the upper light. Nothing she could see without glass.\n\nHer teeth tasted of metal. She stepped back from the scope. A thin tang sat on her tongue with no source she could name. The hair on her forearms prickled.\n\nGlass around her took on a faint vibration: prisms, spare lens plates, even the ink bottle. The pitch sat just below hearing.\n\n“New condition,” she whispered. “Write. Then move.”\n\nHer hand shook once as she lifted the pen. She braced her wrist on the stand.\n\nSudden spike. Uniform vibration in all glass on platform. Metallic taste present. Curve of refraction displacement across upper band. Source not visible to eye.\n\nShe set the pen down.\n\nA bright line appeared across the upper air, far above any tower. It did not travel straight; it followed the curve her marks had suggested, a narrow white band through blue. Along that band, the sky’s color deepened by a shade.\n\nShe watched its motion in fixed steps. White. Fast. Upper band only. No shadow reaching the buildings below. No sound she could separate from the city’s noise.\n\nThe vibration in the glass deepened. One prism hopped in its cradle. She caught it with her palm and held it down until the motion eased.\n\n“Enough,” she said aloud. “Seen enough.”\n\nShe snapped the logbook shut, tucked it under her arm, checked the ink bottle, then went for the hatch. The metallic taste thickened as she started down the narrow stair.\n\nAbove, on the empty platform, the glass still hummed. Over Saint Merro, the sky had changed. She did not have a name for it. She had the marks. She had the taste. She had the decision not to dismiss it.\n\nShe did not call it noise.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 3: The Sky Breaks\n\nThe wall moved before Jhace did.\n\nStone shifted under his palm. A deep grind that did not belong. The tunnel’s thrum broke into a jagged pulse. The pipe beside him threw heat sideways hard enough that his fingers slipped.\n\nThen the ground hit.\n\nThe Underface lurched. Jhace slammed into the wall. His lantern broke free and swung wild on its hook. Bolts shrieked in their brackets. Somewhere upslope a main line roared as pressure slammed through it, a tearing sound that went through bone.\n\n“Off the ladders!” he shouted. “Now!”\n\nDust burst from the arch above, a blind cloud. The tunnel narrowed to sound and impact: stone cracking, metal twisting, grit falling in a hard rush that stung his eyes. He tasted old ash and something new under it, a sharp tang on his tongue.\n\n“Chloen!” Bram’s voice came from the ladder shaft. “Move!”\n\nJhace staggered toward the shaft by habit, one hand on the wall to keep direction. The grid vibration had turned uneven, rolling in waves. A brace ahead of him groaned. He wrapped both arms around it and braced his feet.\n\nThe first wave passed. The tunnel did not settle.\n\nAnother shudder rolled through. The arch above the shaft gave a hard, hollow crack.\n\n“Off!” Jhace bellowed. “Both of you, off that metal!”\n\nBoots hit stone. A heartbeat later the ladder’s top anchor ripped out of the arch. Iron rungs slammed against the wall, sparks flaring where they struck pipe. The whole frame came down in a rattle of metal, choking the shaft with noise.\n\nSomething heavier followed.\n\nA cross-beam tore loose from the upper structure and dropped through the open mouth of the shaft. Jhace saw only a bulked shadow in dust. It hit the tunnel floor with a wet, wrong sound. A short scream cut off.\n\nThe dust thinned enough to show Chloen under the beam.\n\nIt caught her across one leg above the knee. The rest of her lay clear. Her hands clawed at the floor. She dragged in air and let it out in a hoarse sound that barely reached words.\n\n“Jh—” She bit it off. Breath shook in her chest.\n\nBram skidded across stone, dropped to his knees at her side. Blood seeped under the beam’s edge, dark against the damp floor.\n\nJhace reached the timber in three strides. Thick, near his chest-width. One end splintered. The other still buried in ceiling rubble. The weight sat full on her leg.\n\nHe planted his feet, set both hands under the beam’s edge, and heaved.\n\nNothing moved.\n\nMuscle in his back lit with pain. The beam gave no answer. The arch above answered instead—another cracking line, the soft crumble of stone. If he kept forcing, he would bring the rest of the shaft down on all three of them.\n\n“Jhace,” Bram snapped. “Stop. You’ll bring the whole thing.”\n\nChloen’s fingers dug into the floor. She wasn’t making sound now. Her face had gone gray. Lips pulled back from her teeth.\n\n“How bad,” Jhace said.\n\n“Crushed,” Bram said. No comfort. “We get it off in the next few breaths, she keeps the limb. We fail, she bleeds out or the bone cuts through. Or it all comes down. Good choices.”\n\nAnother tremor rolled through. Smaller, but enough to rattle grit from the arch. The beam bounced once and settled heavier.\n\nChloen bit down on her sleeve. Her eyes found Jhace’s. Fear. And trust he had not earned.\n\nJhace saw another shaft in his head. A smaller brace. A quiet hiss he had called harmless. Twenty-three names after.\n\nNot again.\n\n“Bram,” he said. “Pull when I tell you. Straight back. Keep her spine clear.”\n\n“Jhace—”\n\n“Now or we lose her.”\n\nBram moved. He slid his arms under Chloen’s shoulders and upper body and planted his boots wide.\n\nJhace spat dust, wiped his palms once on his trousers, and put both hands on the beam again. This time he did not try to lift. He let skin settle to rough wood. He felt the crushed fibers where impact had flattened grain. He dropped one knee to stone and lowered his center.\n\nThe old grid thrum still ran under everything. A sharper pulse rode on top of it.\n\nHe fixed on the weight at one point.\n\nHold. Long enough.\n\nHeat built in his palms. At first it felt like friction. Then it sharpened and sank through skin into bone. The pressure under his hands changed. Mass turned into resistance. Something pushed back.\n\nCopper-gold light spilled out around his fingers.\n\nJhace flinched and did not pull away. The light came from his own skin, thin streams running into the wood grain. It spread into the shattered end and into the stone on either side, tracing stress he did not know how to name. The beam vibrated with it, a tight resonance inside the deeper shake of the tunnel.\n\nHis arms locked. Shoulder to jaw clenched. Breath left and did not come back clean.\n\n“Now,” he got out.\n\nBram hauled. The beam did not rise much. It did lighten. Jhace felt the load shift through his hands, up his forearms, into his chest, then out again. Sweat ran into his eyes. His teeth ached. For a few breaths the world narrowed to the hot line where flesh met wood.\n\nThen Chloen slid free.\n\nHer leg came out from under the beam in a rush. It looked wrong—swollen, skin split, blood running—but not as much bone under skin as he expected. Copper-gold traces ran across the bruised flesh for a heartbeat, then sank out of sight.\n\nShe screamed once and sagged against Bram’s hold, panting.\n\n“Got you,” Bram said, voice rough. “Got you. Stay with me.”\n\nJhace let go.\n\nThe copper-gold snapped out. The weight threw itself back into the wood. The beam dropped and jolted the stone, then settled, sending a shallow crack through the floor but no full collapse.\n\nPain hit his hands. Not a cut. A wide burn from fingertips to elbow. His fingers curled without consent. Tremor ran through both arms. Copper-gold lines followed the veins in his forearms for a breath, then faded, leaving only the shake.\n\n“What,” Bram said. He stared at Jhace’s hands. “What did you—”\n\nAnother pulse hit.\n\nA deeper shock rolled through the Underface. Not a tremor. A single, enormous surge. The tunnel lights flared and went out. Pipe noise jumped in pitch, then steadied at a harsh new register. A low roar came from everywhere at once—stone and air and the open shaft above.\n\nThe air pressed inward for several breaths, then snapped out again.\n\nJhace hit the floor on one knee. Pain in his hands fought with a heavy pressure in his chest that did not belong to breath.\n\nHe looked up.\n\nThrough the shaft mouth he saw the sky. Not blue. A slice of white so bright it hurt, cutting across the visible strip. Color at its edges warped. Rooftop outlines bent. Fine dust rained down, glittering faintly copper in the light.\n\nThen rubble slid across the gap and the shaft choked.\n\nHe heard screams above. Not one street. Many.\n\n“Out,” Jhace said. The word scraped his throat. “We get out.”\n\n“Chloen can’t climb,” Bram said.\n\n“Then we carry.”\n\nJhace forced his hands open. Pain flared fresh. Tremor kept going.\n\n“Emergency hatch,” he said. “Two junctions river-side. That run ties to street.”\n\nBram shifted Chloen’s weight across his shoulders. Her injured leg hung wrong, but when it bumped against his chest she didn’t scream. She hissed and clamped her jaw, eyes unfocused.\n\nJhace pushed up. The tremor in his hands made the stone edges blur. He set his shoulder to the beam as they passed, testing. It felt stable enough for the breaths it would take to clear.\n\nThey ran.\n\nThe tunnel had changed shape. Some braces had shifted. Some chalk marks lay under fresh dust. Twice they climbed over fallen pipe. Once a side wall buckled inward, leaving a narrow gap. Jhace shoved at broken stone until it gave them passage, ignoring the way his palms complained.\n\nBy the time they reached the hatch, his breath tore in his chest. The metal door had jammed in its frame. He set both hands to it and shoved.\n\nFor a breath, a faint copper-gold shimmer traced the hinges. Heat stung up his forearms. The frame groaned and moved. Pain sharpened in his palms.\n\nBram got a boot into the gap and kicked it wider.\n\nThey spilled onto the surface.\n\nThe street looked wrong. Stone fractured in long, uneven lines. A building front across the way had peeled open, its face sheared away, rooms exposed. Dust drifted in slow curtains. People ran in broken patterns, some toward the damage, some away.\n\nAbove all of it, far higher than any tower, the white line cut the sky.\n\nFrom here he could see more of it. It did not fall. It passed overhead, impossibly long, light bending along its edges. Fine metallic dust hung in its wake, catching light in a faint copper shimmer that clung to the air.\n\nJhace had no word for it. He had work.\n\nThe building to their right groaned. The lower corner had sunk. Its load had shifted onto a single main support near where street met wall. A knot of people huddled at its base, half in the doorway, half in the open.\n\n“Back!” Jhace shouted. “Away from that wall!”\n\nSome obeyed. Some didn’t. A woman with glass dust in her hair stood near the edge of the crowd, turned upward, staring. A logbook clutched to her chest. Jhace did not know her name.\n\nThe support post split with a harsh crack. Stone above it began to fold. Blocks shifted in a chain, strain passing down the line.\n\nIf that post went, the whole corner followed. The doorway crushed anyone still within reach. Too many still stood too close.\n\nJhace’s hands burned. His fingers shook. His body already felt used past what it was meant to do.\n\nNot again.\n\nHe ran for the failing post.\n\n“Jhace!” Bram shouted behind him. “You can’t—”\n\nJhace dropped to his knees at the base of the support, where stone met foundation. Hairline fractures spidered out from the contact point. Dust leaked in slow streams. He pressed both palms flat against the cracked surface. Skin to stone.\n\nHold. Long enough for them to run.\n\nHeat surged out of him. Copper-gold burst around his fingers, brighter than before. It drove into the fractures, tracing each line through the stone. From his hands it spread upward into the post and sideways into adjoining blocks, chasing stress and filling it with glow.\n\nFor a breath he felt every load path. Every place weight had shifted. It was too much. The sense of connection ran from his palms into shoulder and chest and down his spine. Vibration filled his skull.\n\nHe dug his fingers into stone. Forearms locked. Breath turned shallow and ragged.\n\n“Move!” Bram bellowed. “Everyone away! Now!”\n\nFeet pounded broken street. The crowd pulled back. The woman with the logbook staggered but did not fall. Her gaze fixed on the glow around his hands, wide and sharp. Glass shards at her feet picked up the copper-gold tint and hummed.\n\nThe building’s lean slowed.\n\nThe main support still wanted to fail. Cracks pressed against his hands. Stone above tried to fold further. He pushed back with body and heat and will, nothing clean about it.\n\nHis vision narrowed at the edges. Sound dulled. Copper-gold brightened until it washed detail away. Only masses and lines remained.\n\n“Jhace,” Bram’s voice came from far off. “That’s enough. They’re clear. Let it go.”\n\nJhace tried to answer. His jaw would not move.\n\nHe held for three more heartbeats.\n\nThen his arms stopped obeying.\n\nThe light cut out. The support dropped a small distance, then caught on a new, broken balance. Stone dust cascaded in a fresh curtain. A few blocks fell and shattered in the empty doorway. The building groaned and settled into a shape that did not crush anyone.\n\nJhace tipped sideways.\n\nHe hit the street on his shoulder. His hands twitched against stone. Tremor ran uncontrolled from fingertips to elbow, violent and fine. Pain bloomed deep and throbbing.\n\nThe sky tilted. The white line overhead had already passed, leaving haze.\n\nEverything darkened.\n\n—\n\nSound came back first. Shouts. Weeping. The ring of tools. Someone calling orders. The grid’s low thrum, altered but present.\n\nThen weight: a rough blanket over his chest, stone under his back.\n\nThen his hands. Still shaking.\n\nJhace opened his eyes.\n\nHe lay on broken street, propped against fallen masonry. Dust coated his clothes. Copper-gold smears marked the stone near his hands, fading by the breath. His fingers jerked in small, useless motions.\n\nA shadow crossed his face.\n\nA woman stood over him. Dark hair pulled back in a knot, streaked with fine glass dust and copper shimmer. A logbook clenched to her chest. Her eyes moved over him in quick passes: hands, arms, the stone behind his back, the faint traces of glow still seeping into cracks he had held.\n\nShock sat on her face. Behind it, calculation.\n\n“What did you do?” she asked.\n\nHer voice held no awe. Only demand.\n\nJhace swallowed. His throat scraped. His hands would not stop moving.\n\n“I held,” he said. “It should have fallen.”\n\nTheir gazes locked for a breath. The city groaned around them. The grid’s hum ran at a new pitch under the noise.\n\nThe world had changed. His body knew it. So did her eyes. Neither of them had a name for any of it yet.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 4: After the Shudder\n\nJhace woke on his back with stone under his shoulders and boiled herbs in his throat.\n\nFor a few breaths sound came without shape. Then it sorted: low voices, cloth moving, metal in basins. Somewhere a child crying, then quiet. No collapse roar. Only the roughened hum of the grid under everything.\n\nHis hands hurt.\n\nHe tried to flex his fingers. Tremor ran from fingertips to elbow in a fine constant shake. Pain followed, a dull burn along bone. Rough cloth bound his palms. The edges rasped.\n\n“Stay down,” Bram said.\n\nJhace turned his head. He lay on a pallet in a side room off a wider tunnel. Ceiling higher than the work runs, stone blocks set regular. Two rows of makeshift beds on the floor. He recognized the top of a ladder shaft in the next chamber: Bram’s level.\n\nBram sat on an overturned crate a pace away. Dust streaked his hair. A dark bruise marked his cheek. He held a clay cup in both hands.\n\n“How long,” Jhace said. The words scraped.\n\n“Since late Striking,” Bram said. “We’re into Tide One now. You were out most of that stretch.”\n\nJhace pushed himself up on an elbow. The room tilted once and steadied.\n\n“Chloen,” he said.\n\nBram’s mouth twitched.\n\n“Alive,” he said. “Leg’s in bad shape, but not gone. Healer says crushed bone, torn flesh, all the words. Some of the worst breaks were already knitting when they opened it. She’s chewed enough root to sleep through a grid failure.”\n\nJhace’s gaze tracked past him.\n\nChloen lay three pallets down, under a cleaner blanket than his. Her leg was splinted from hip to ankle, wrapped in bandage cloth that showed dark stains along the middle. Her chest rose slow and even. At the edges of the wrappings, a faint copper shimmer clung to the fibers.\n\nJhace’s fingers twitched. Memory came in pieces: the beam, the heat in his hands, copper-gold streaking over bruised flesh.\n\n“Do not do that,” Bram said quietly. “Do not put her on the same list as the building. She is still breathing because you refused to let that beam have her.”\n\nJhace looked down at his own hands. The bandages were stained with dust and a pale copper tint. Under the cloth, his fingers jerked in short, useless bursts.\n\n“What do they say happened,” he asked.\n\n“Out there?” Bram jerked his chin toward the tunnel. “Depends who you ask. Some say a corner of Saint Merro learned to stand on its own. Some say the Piercing cracked the rules and the stone is waking. Some say there’s a Forgemaster walking around with molten hands.” His eyes dropped to Jhace’s bandages. “Plenty say an Underface inspector held up a building.”\n\nJhace’s stomach tightened.\n\n“They’re asking for you,” Bram went on. “The ones who were under that wall. Runners keep sticking their heads in. Want to know when the holder can walk.”\n\n“I held a short span,” Jhace said. The word caught. “It did not fall because loads shifted. They ask for the wrong thing.”\n\n“Maybe,” Bram said. “City’s still cracked. Council’s shouting in the Crown. Guilds are pointing at each other. Stone keeps failing in the middle of it. We have more to do than argue about what they call you.”\n\nJhace swung his legs off the pallet. Cold floor met his feet. The shift set his arm muscles jumping.\n\nBram reached out. Jhace shook his head once and pushed himself up without taking the hand. The walls rolled past his eyes and then settled.\n\n“Show me what failed,” he said.\n\nBram studied him for a few breaths.\n\n“Fine,” Bram said at last. “But if you fall, try not to hit anything that holds weight.”\n\nJhace loosened the wraps enough to let his fingers move. The cloth pulled at raw skin; pain bit up his forearms. The tremor did not care.\n\nThey stepped into the main tunnel.\n\nThe level had turned into a passage of constant motion. Runners carrying slates. Guild workers with coil and brace. Healers moving between makeshift nests with baskets of cloth and jars. Blood, hot metal, and root bitter thickened the usual damp-stone smell. The grid hum ran harsher, with edges where mains had been patched quick.\n\nHeads turned as they passed.\n\n“That’s him,” someone said behind them. “The holder from the kiln street.”\n\nJhace kept his eyes forward.\n\n“Where first,” he asked.\n\n“Nearest break we can reach,” Bram said. “Two marry points took damage. One side support cracked. No spare crew for a full fix yet.”\n\n“Test,” Jhace said. “We see if that thing listens without people under it.”\n\nThe first junction had already been wedged and strapped. The main hummed steady. The brace that had sheared away vibrated on its own note. Jhace touched stone beside it.\n\nStress had moved into the emergency wedges. Imperfect. Stable enough.\n\n“We move,” he said.\n\nThe cracked side support sat deeper, in hotter air. Someone had tried to help by jamming broken stone under its base. Gaps showed. The line over it fed a crowded stretch of houses.\n\n“No one on the floors above?” Jhace asked.\n\n“Cleared after the first tremors,” Bram said. “For now.”\n\nThe fracture line at the base ran shallow but long. If it failed during the next Tide, the tunnel and part of the building wall would go with it.\n\nJhace set his palm on the split.\n\nStone met skin. Cool away from the crack. Warmer where it carried load. He felt the thin gap and its rough edges. His own shake blurred the reading.\n\nHold, he thought. Hold here. No one is under you yet, but they will be.\n\nNothing answered.\n\nHe pressed harder. Pain flared in his palm, then his wrist. The crack remained a crack. No heat surge. No copper-gold. Only the steady push of weight and his fingers jumping.\n\n“Anything?” Bram asked.\n\n“No,” Jhace said.\n\n“Good,” Bram said. “I was half afraid you were about to say you can fix every hairline in this city by frowning at it.”\n\nJhace pulled his hand away. His skin tingled. The stone stayed exactly as it was.\n\n“Mark it,” he said. “We reinforce it with tools.”\n\nThey turned upslope.\n\nThey did not reach the surface before the next break found them.\n\nA runner shot around a bend and nearly collided with them. A boy, sweat bright on his skin, breath tearing.\n\n“You Jhace Torrins?” he gasped. “The one who held the building?”\n\nJhace’s shoulders went tight.\n\n“Yes,” Bram said. “What’s failing?”\n\n“Shop near the Merro kiln corridor,” the boy said. “Healers using it. Wall cracked. They say if we don’t get you, we lose half the wounded when it drops.”\n\nJhace started moving before Bram answered.\n\nThe kiln street looked worse than it had when they’d first climbed out.\n\nDust still hung in the air. A whole row of small fronts showed new cracks and missing corners. Someone had turned a narrow shop into a triage room; pallets and tables filled the inside space. The wall facing the street carried a thick fracture from lintel to ground.\n\nPeople crowded the doorway. Two workers braced the wall with poles. Every shove made fine dust leak faster.\n\n“Back,” Jhace said.\n\nHis voice came rough and low. It did not clear the crowd.\n\nBram stepped up beside him.\n\n“Back,” Bram repeated, louder. “Give him the wall or it will take you with it.”\n\nThe crowd shifted, grudging but real. Jhace stepped to the crack.\n\nCloser, it was worse. The roof load had shifted onto two narrow contact points instead of the full length of wall. Inside, ceiling beams sat on that broken line. Several pallets lay under the center span.\n\nJhace placed both palms flat against the stone.\n\nCold outer face. Heat from wounded bodies and lamps inside. Heavy pressure from above.\n\nHold, he thought. They die if you do not hold.\n\nHeat rose under his skin at once. Copper-gold slid across his hands and into the wall, fine streams chasing the fracture up and down. The glow reached the lintel, the base, then spread sideways into the adjoining blocks.\n\nHis jaw locked. His arms went rigid.\n\n“Clear the center,” Bram shouted. “Move the pallets to the sides. No arguments.”\n\nVoices and movement answered from inside. Cloth dragged. Someone cried out as they were shifted, then grunted and went quiet.\n\nThe wall pressed into his hands, trying to widen the crack. He felt every point where load had narrowed, every place the stone barely held. His pulse beat into the contact, hard enough to make his fingers sting.\n\nHis shake worsened. Copper-gold lit the veins along his forearms, bright down to the elbow. Each heartbeat drove pain down those lines.\n\n“Almost,” Bram called. “Hold.”\n\nJhace held.\n\nThe glow ran as far as it could find strain and stopped. It did not heal. It only pinned the worst angles in place.\n\n“Clear,” Bram shouted at last. “No one under the middle. Out.”\n\nJhace counted three heartbeats more, then let go.\n\nThe copper-gold cut off. The wall shifted a short span and caught on a new lean. Dust dribbled. Loose chips fell and bounced on bare floor. The beams above groaned but stayed set.\n\nJhace’s hands dropped. His fingers clawed and opened in fast, useless grabs.\n\n“Out,” Bram told the street. “No pallets under that span. You two—brace it with poles and proper wedges before next Pouring.”\n\nJhace stepped back until his shoulders met the opposite wall. Sweat ran cold down his back. The edges of his vision went soft. His hands shook hard enough that his nails clicked together.\n\n“Your arms lit,” Bram said, low. “Vein-lines all the way up. You still with me?”\n\n“For now,” Jhace said. His tongue felt thick.\n\nHe looked down. Faint copper-gold lines still traced his forearms, pulsing with the tremor. They faded. The shake did not.\n\n“Well,” Bram said. “We learned the other half.”\n\n“What half,” Jhace asked.\n\n“Whatever this is,” Bram said, “answers when people are about to get crushed. It ignores you when you want the nice clean fixes. And every time it answers, it takes more out of you.”\n\nJhace stared at his hands until the last trace of color vanished, leaving raw skin and an unsteady twitch.\n\nSomeone cleared a throat.\n\nThe woman from the street stood near the cracked wall. Glass dust and copper shimmer threaded her hair. A logbook pressed against her chest. Her gaze moved from his hands to the stone.\n\n“Jhace Torrins,” she said.\n\nHe narrowed his eyes.\n\n“You know my name,” he said.\n\n“You shouted it yesterday,” she said. “Bram shouted it louder. Runners carried it. May I see where you touched?”\n\n“No,” Jhace said.\n\nShe watched him for a breath.\n\n“I need to know what changed in the wall,” she said. “Healers are using buildings you have… helped. If I know where your grip holds strongest, I can tell them where not to put beds.”\n\nThat, Jhace could not argue with.\n\nHe turned back to the wall. One shaking finger rose to the point where the glow had felt thickest. The tremor made him miss twice. On the third try he hit the right strip.\n\n“Here,” he said. “Then down to here, and across.”\n\nShe stepped closer, careful not to brush him. Her hand followed his path, then moved on, checking the stone in small presses.\n\n“Hot band along your line,” she murmured. “Cooler outside it. Stress is concentrated there. Load still carried, but the effective bearing length is small compared to the wall’s full span. It should not stand on that narrow zone. But it does.”\n\n“It stands for now,” Jhace said. “Do not trust it past that.”\n\n“I do not trust anything past that,” she said. “But I prefer numbers to stories. Stories about you are already spreading.”\n\n“Stories are useless,” Jhace said. “Either the brace holds or it does not.”\n\n“And if it only holds when you are here?” she asked.\n\nHe had no answer.\n\nHer eyes dropped to his hands. The tremor still ran, fine and constant.\n\n“Is that new?” she asked.\n\n“Old injury,” he said. “Tools.”\n\nShe made a quiet sound that was not agreement.\n\n“If you keep lying this badly,” she said, “you make my work harder.”\n\nA voice called from farther up the street.\n\nShe glanced that way, then back.\n\n“I am Tiffani Merrow,” she said. “Glasswork and structural optics. I intend to keep people away from the first places your grips will fail. I need your routes and what you feel when you touch stone turned into marks.”\n\n“I have routes,” Jhace said. “I do not have data.”\n\n“You have both,” she said. “You just leave some of it in your bones.”\n\nShe moved away, already reading the next damaged fronts. Bram watched her go.\n\n“She will be trouble,” he said.\n\nJhace did not answer.\n\nA bent nail lay near his boot. He bent and reached for it.\n\nHis fingers missed twice. On the third try he caught it between thumb and forefinger. The tremor snapped. The nail jumped from his grip and tapped back onto stone.\n\nHe stared at it.\n\nBram’s gaze slid to his hands.\n\nJhace straightened. He folded his hands behind his back, hiding the twitch.\n\n“I am fine,” he said.\n\nBram said nothing.\n\nAcross the street, Tiffani looked over. Her eyes dropped once to his hidden hands, then back to his face. She held his gaze for a breath, then turned away.\n\nThe grid hummed at its new pitch under their feet. The wall held. Jhace’s grip did not.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 5: Lines That Don’t Lie\n\nTiffani rebuilt her map before she trusted her eyes.\n\nThe kiln corridor still existed in the way the Crown would insist it did—names, taxes, shop marks, routes for carts—but the stone had shifted in ways that made those labels unreliable. Two fronts that used to leave a clear pace between their thresholds now pressed closer, their shared seam crushed and dusted white. A drainage channel she had measured three Tides earlier no longer ran straight; it bent where the paving had sunk in a shallow arc. The bend mattered. Water followed bends, and so did rot, and so did frost-crack when Quench turned sharp.\n\nShe stood in the middle of the broken street with her logbook open against her forearm. The air still held grit and the metallic tang that had followed the sky’s white mark. Her observatory was gone—stair torn, platform split, its instruments taken by gravity and glass shatter—and she felt its absence as missing reference points. She had replaced them with a straight-edge, a stub of chalk, and the habit of verifying everything twice.\n\n“Reference,” she murmured. “Merro lintel. Old height.”\n\nShe faced her family’s shop and fixed her gaze on the lintel beam above the doorway. The beam itself had survived, but the wall around it had not. She marked the old line from memory in her book, then sketched the new line by sight. The difference was small. Stone did not have to move far before it broke ribs.\n\nShe stepped to the nearest crack and dragged chalk along it, forcing the line to show where dust tried to hide it. The crack ran up, then angled, then stopped at a seam that should have carried load cleanly into the next block. It did not. It diverted toward a node that used to sit quiet beneath the street.\n\nShe turned slowly, counting cracks under her breath and marking every third one that carried the same faint copper shimmer. Those lines stopped abruptly, never reaching the full contact lengths that a real support wanted. She wrote in the margin: **effective bearing zone too narrow**. Under it: **residue persists**.\n\nIt was the same pattern she had felt under her palm the previous Tide when Jhace Torrins pressed his shaking hands into a wall and forced it to hold long enough for the wounded to be dragged clear. The stone at that touch-line still carried a thin warmth when she tested it, a band of elevated heat that did not match any fire or kiln vent.\n\nShe was copying what the city had already written.\n\n“Merrow!”\n\nThe shout came from upslope. Tiffani finished the last chalk pass and looked up.\n\nTwo men in Merro Glass vests picked their way through rubble, their hems gray with dust. Behind them walked a third man with a blue sash at his shoulder—RRO. The sash was dirty but tied precisely, the knot centered as if that could impose order on cracked street.\n\nTiffani resisted the urge to wipe her hands on her tunic. The chalk and dust were already part of her.\n\n“Master Merrow,” the taller guild man called again when he drew close enough to stop shouting. “You have a gift for being where no one can find you.”\n\n“Stones keep moving,” she said. “I keep following.”\n\nHe glanced at the chalk lines and the copper-smudged seams and made a face that tried for humor and failed. “Yes. About that.” He nodded toward the sash. “Inspector Harlen. He came to the guild hall asking for someone who can read breaks. We told him you would already be in the street doing this.”\n\nHarlen stepped forward. His eyes took in her open logbook, the chalk on her fingers, the line she had drawn from wall to pavement. His gaze paused on the copper glints along one crack, then moved away as if he did not want to be seen noticing it.\n\n“Master Merrow,” he said. “The RRO is assembling a committee for hazard survey and repair coordination. You were recommended. We would prefer your attention on that instead of independent mapping.”\n\nTiffani shut the logbook partway, thumb holding her place. “The city is already performing a survey,” she said. “Fractures show where the load is failing. I am recording the result.”\n\n“The difference,” Harlen said, “is that a coordinated effort sets priorities. We need lists, not personal notebooks. The Crown wants counts: compromised supports, households at risk, mains threatened. If you join formally, you will have assistants and access to full plans.”\n\n“And constraints,” Tiffani said. “Assignments. Approved routes.”\n\n“Guidelines,” Harlen corrected. “We must manage panic as much as stone. If every craftsperson follows their own urgency, we lose coherence. The RRO needs your precision in a framework.”\n\nShe looked past him to the nearest crack. It bent away from where the official street plan claimed the main support should run. It bent toward the spot where Jhace’s hands had been. The heat band there had not faded fully.\n\n“You will prioritize by district,” she said. “By tax value. By which voices reach the Council chambers without runners.”\n\nHarlen did not deny it. His jaw tightened, then he chose a calmer tone. “We will prioritize where failure risks the greatest loss. Load density. Population. Critical services. A collapse near the Crown mains—”\n\n“Kills different names,” Tiffani said. “Not fewer bodies elsewhere.”\n\nOne of the guild men shifted his weight. “Tiffani,” he said, dropping formality. “The workshop needs you too. Orders are stacked. People want replacement glazing, safe mirrors, anything that makes a room feel less broken. You could move your bench upslope for a few Tides. Let the RRO handle the cracks and come back when the shouting settles.”\n\nThere it was: the clean line, the profitable line, the line that required less argument.\n\nHer mind supplied Marisse without permission. Not as a ghost or a lesson, just a fact: a person crushed under glass that should have been rated higher, in a shop that should have been reinforced, after Tiffani had written **noise** in the margin and turned the page to keep schedule.\n\nShe kept her voice even.\n\n“I have watched one pattern until it killed someone,” she said. “I will not repeat that because it is simpler for the Crown.”\n\nHarlen’s expression cooled. “Refusing a formal role means you will have no influence when we set priorities,” he said. “You can speak from the street, but decisions will still be made in chambers.”\n\n“Then I will give you numbers from the street,” she said. “You want lists? I will send lists. Holds. Duration ranges. Estimated loads. Heat bands. Copper residue. You can compare my sheets to your minutes and decide which predicts the next collapse.”\n\nHe stared at her logbook, then back at her face. “You are making this harder than it needs to be.”\n\n“It became hard when the sky split,” she said. “Everything since is choosing where to stand.”\n\nHarlen let a short breath out through his nose. “Very well. The offer stands if you change your mind. Send your lists to the RRO hall. We will review them.”\n\nHe nodded to the guild men and turned away. They followed, one of them giving her a final look that carried workshop, pay, and the comfort of problems that ended at the edge of a bench.\n\nTiffani watched them go until their backs disappeared behind a leaning front.\n\nThen she reopened her logbook and turned to a clean page.\n\nShe wrote: **Post-Piercing Holds — source unknown**.\n\nUnder it she drew a narrow rectangle for the healer’s wall she had inspected earlier, marking the hot band where Jhace had touched and shading the cooler stone outside it. **Effective bearing width less than half original span.** **Stable now. Unsafe for long occupancy.**\n\nShe turned the page and began a table.\n\n**Location. Apparent load. Visible copper. Heat band. Reported hold duration. Outcome. Observed cost on subject.**\n\nThe last column stayed mostly blank. She had only one clear data point so far: his hands shaking, his forearms lit with vein-lines during use, his grip failing afterward on small objects. That was enough to justify more observation. It was not enough to predict how long he could keep doing it.\n\nShe wrote at the bottom: **Hypothesis: activation tied to immediate protective need. Noncritical conditions do not trigger. Cost accumulative. Data incomplete.**\n\nPella found her after a few spans, boots scraping over grit. Dust coated her lower legs up to the knee, and her curls had frizzed from heat and sweat. She carried a satchel that bulged with wrapped bread, stoppered jars, and a small ink bottle cushioned in cloth.\n\n“You were not at the workshop,” Pella said. “So I followed the noise. Also the runners saying ‘the glass woman with the book is marking walls again.’ Eat.”\n\nShe pushed bread into Tiffani’s chest with enough force that Tiffani had to trap it against her logbook.\n\nTiffani exhaled. “You are gentle today.”\n\n“I am consistent,” Pella said. “You forget to chew when you find a new problem. I am preventing structural failure.”\n\nTiffani opened the bread with her thumb and took a bite. It tasted of ash and salt. It also tasted of being steadied.\n\nPella leaned in to squint at the page. “That a new table?”\n\n“Yes,” Tiffani said. “For holds. For what the stone does afterward. For what he does afterward.”\n\n“The man with the glow,” Pella said.\n\n“The man with the tremor,” Tiffani corrected, and her voice softened despite herself. “He is pretending it is old injury. He is not good at pretending.”\n\nPella’s eyebrows lifted. “You got his name.”\n\n“He shouted it at a wall,” Tiffani said. “His friend shouted it louder. Saint Merro repeats anything that keeps people alive.”\n\nPella shifted her gaze toward the street, toward the patched wall and its propped poles. “So the guild wants you polishing rich panes, the RRO wants you behind a desk, and you want to walk broken streets counting how much that man is breaking himself.”\n\n“Yes,” Tiffani said. “That is accurate.”\n\nPella made a small sound that held irritation and acceptance. “Then you will need more ink,” she said, opening her satchel. “And you will need to sit once in a while. Sitting is permitted. I am granting permission.”\n\n“I do not require permission.”\n\n“You do,” Pella said, and pushed the ink bottle into her hand. “From me. I am a practical authority.”\n\nTiffani’s mouth pulled tight at the edges. It was the nearest she came to a smile without wasting attention.\n\nThey worked the street in a tight loop after that—walk, measure, mark, write. Three more sites where witnesses claimed copper shimmer had touched stone. At one, the residue had cooled to nothing but a faint discoloration. At another, a thin warmth still clung in a band that made Tiffani’s skin prickle when she held her palm near it. Each time, she sketched the fractures, traced probable load paths, and compared them to what standard construction demanded.\n\nEach time, the numbers returned the same message: the holds were narrow, inefficient, and only long enough.\n\nLate in Shaping she claimed a corner of a mostly intact shop whose owner had turned his counter into a writing surface. She weighted her pages with bits of broken stone and wrote patterns as statements, not comforts.\n\n**Confirmed holds only with people in direct harm path.**  \n**No confirmed holds on empty structures.**  \n**Copper intensity correlates with number of people cleared, not total mass.**  \n**Subject fine grip degraded post-use.**\n\nShe circled **cost** three times, then stopped because repetition did not add information.\n\nWhen she lifted her head, her neck ached and the light outside the doorway had shifted. The street noise had changed from sharp panic to strained routine: people settling into the idea that this would not end in a single Tide. Under it all, the grid hum still sat at its new rough pitch.\n\nTiffani shut the logbook and pressed its cover against her sternum for a breath. Her chest rose and fell. The metallic tang in the air did not leave.\n\nWhen she stepped back into the street, her eyes went first to the healer’s wall. Someone had added poles and wedges where she had marked risk. The crack still held its new lean. No fresh dust spilled.\n\nBeyond it, a small knot of people had gathered near another run entrance, attention fixed on a pair of figures in the open.\n\nJhace Torrins stood there with Bram at his shoulder.\n\nHe looked worse in full light than he had when he was braced against the wall. The tremor in his hands showed even at this distance, fingers making constant small corrections that never settled. His shoulders carried the set of someone who had already pushed past what his body wanted and intended to do it again.\n\nHe reached down for something at his feet. He missed. He tried again and missed. On the third attempt his fingers caught the object, then jolted and let it fall. He straightened with his hands tucked behind his back, motion controlled and practiced.\n\nBram’s face turned toward him with a look Tiffani recognized without needing a name for it: concern that refused to become a command.\n\nTiffani flipped her logbook open and wrote one last line in the cost column.\n\n**Fine grip failure observed. Concealment behavior present.**\n\nShe shut the book, tucked it under her arm, and started toward him.\n\nIf the city insisted on building stories around a man who held stone with his bare hands, then someone needed to anchor those stories to marks and heat bands before they drove him into holds that would tear him apart.\n\nShe walked with measured steps, counting cracks as she went, and did not allow herself to look away from the tremor he kept hidden.\n"}

# N01: Chapter 1 – The Weight Before

**Tide:** 0 | **Phase:** Kindling  
**POV:** Jhace Torrins | **Beat:** BEAT01 (Observation/Pattern Recognition) | **Soulpulse:** 0 uses | **Relationship:** Mutual Judgment (Remote)

***

The heat-grid hummed wrong.

Jhace pressed his palm flat against the supply line. Warmth bled through the iron casing, steady as breath, but underneath—a vibration. Not the rhythm of water boiling through pipes three bars below. Something else. A tremor that shouldn't be there.[1][2]

He shifted his hand two spans downslope along the pipe. The vibration sharpened. A high-frequency rattle, faint enough most inspectors would miss it, obvious enough to set his teeth on edge.[3]

He pulled his measuring strap from his belt and marked the section. Nineteen bars of pipe ran through this junction chamber. The off-note originated somewhere in the vertical shaft feeding the Underface district—lowest terrace in Saint Merro, oldest infrastructure, highest load.[2]

The weight of the city pressed down here. Literally. Seven terraces of stone and timber and forge-heat above him. When something failed this deep, people died in their beds. Slow freeze during the Quench phase. Bodies found stiff three Tides later when neighbors noticed the silence.[4][3]

He traced the pipe toward the vertical shaft. The chamber narrowed. Moisture slicked the stone walls where condensation gathered. His lantern threw sharp shadows across the junction brackets—iron straps bolted into bedrock, each one carrying the load of the pipes they held.[2][4]

One bracket sat a finger's width lower than its neighbor.

Jhace crouched. Ran his fingers over the bolt heads. The metal was warm, same as the rest. No corrosion. No visible cracking. But the angle was wrong. The load had shifted. Something in the bedrock had moved, and the bracket had started to tilt.[3][2]

He pulled a worn journal from his coat and scratched notes. *Junction 14, vertical feed. Bracket subsidence, eastern mount. Vibration in supply line suggests load redistribution. Check anchorage above.*

The lantern flame guttered. He adjusted the wick and checked his pocket watch—an old Guild-standard piece, heavy as a stone in his palm. He'd been down here two full phases already. Bram would start complaining soon.[3]

He straightened and moved toward the ladder. The vertical shaft stretched upward into darkness, iron rungs embedded in stone every stride. Forty bars of climbing to reach the next inspection point. His knees already ached from the descent.[4][3]

Halfway up, he paused. Pressed his ear to the stone wall.

There.

A low groan. Not wind. Not water hammer. Stone under stress. The kind of sound that meant something was bending when it should be holding rigid.[2][3]

He climbed faster.

***

The mid-level inspection chamber was larger, better lit. Someone had installed a secondary lantern bracket here—probably after the last collapse scare, three Castings back. Jhace remembered the reports. Two dead, eleven injured, half a district without heat for an Anneal. The Guild had paid reparations and promised upgrades. The upgrades never came.[2]

He crossed to the main trunk line and placed both hands on the pipe. Heat rolled through the iron, steady and strong. But the vibration—still there. Louder now. A resonance building through the system, propagating from the weakened bracket below.[3][2]

He pulled out his journal again. Drew a quick diagram of the load path. The vertical feed carried pressure from the Pouring district furnaces downslope to the Underface residential blocks. If the bracket failed completely, the pipe would torque. Joints would split. Superheated steam would vent into the tunnels, then into the housing warrens built directly above.[4][2]

He wrote: *Failure cascade risk. Recommend immediate reinforcement or controlled shutdown.*

A voice echoed down from the access hatch above. "Jhace! You planning to live down there?"

Bram. Of course.[3]

"Structural concern," Jhace called back. "Give me another phase."

"You said that two phases ago." The hatch clanged open. Bram's broad face appeared, backlit by daylight. "It's Shaping already. You missed the Pouring meal."

"Not hungry."

"You're never hungry." Bram started down the ladder, boots ringing on iron. "What'd you find?"

Jhace gestured at his diagram. "Bracket subsidence in Junction 14. Load's shifting. The whole vertical feed is starting to resonate."

Bram leaned over the journal, squinting. His hands were scarred from thirty Castings of forge work, thick-fingered and steady. He traced the load path Jhace had drawn, nodding slowly.[3]

"You report this to the Guild?"

"Not yet."

"Why not?"

Because the Guild would send a committee. The committee would argue about jurisdiction. The Regulatory Office would demand an impact study. By the time anyone acted, the bracket would fail, and Jhace would be standing at another funeral, watching families bury people he could have saved if he'd just moved faster.[2][3]

He didn't say that. He said, "Wanted to confirm the scope first."

Bram gave him a look. The kind of look that said *I know what you're doing, and I'm not going to stop you, but I'm also not going to pretend it's healthy*.[3]

"How bad is it?" Bram asked.

"Bad enough. If it fails during peak load—Kindling or Striking, when everyone's heating their rooms—the pressure spike will blow the joints. Steam venting into residential. Dozens dead, maybe more."

"So we reinforce it."

"We?"

"You think I'm letting you crawl around down here alone?" Bram shook his head. "Besides, you'll need someone to hold the brace while you torque the bolts."

Jhace hesitated. He preferred working alone. Fewer people to worry about. Fewer people to disappoint when he missed something.[2][3]

But Bram was right. The bracket sat in an awkward position, wedged between the pipe and the bedrock wall. Reinforcing it would take two sets of hands.

"Fine," Jhace said. "But we do it now. Before the evening load."

"Now?" Bram glanced upward, toward the hatch and the daylight beyond. "Jhace, it's Shaping. We've been working since Kindling. You need to eat something. Sleep something."

"After."

"You always say after." Bram's voice was gentle, exasperated. The tone of someone who'd had this argument too many times to count. "Look, I'll get the reinforcement brackets from the workshop. You go topside, eat a meal, and meet me back here in one phase. One phase. The bracket's not going to fail in the next phase."

Jhace looked at his journal. At the diagram. At the load path that ended in superheated steam and bodies frozen in their beds.

"One phase," he agreed.

Bram clapped him on the shoulder. "Good. And Jhace?"

"What?"

"Try not to look so much like you're attending your own funeral."

Jhace almost smiled. Almost.

***

Topside, the city gleamed.

Saint Merro rose in terraces from the harbor to the Crown, each district stacked above the last like a child's tower of blocks. Sunlight caught the glass facades in the Pouring district, where Tiffani Merrow's workshop sat among the other glasshouses. Jhace had never been inside. He'd heard the stories, though—how she refused to approve substandard materials, how she'd once held up an entire Guild contract because the measurements were off by three fingers. People called her *the Glasswright Tyrant* behind her back.[2][3]

Jhace thought she sounded exhausting.

He bought a meat pasty from a corner vendor and ate it standing, watching the crowds move through the Shaping markets. Families. Workers. Apprentices running errands. All of them depending on the heat-grid to keep them alive when the Quench came.[4]

He finished the pasty and headed back toward the Underface access tunnels.

Bram was waiting at the hatch, a canvas sack slung over one shoulder. He raised an eyebrow. "That was fast."

"Ate fast."

"Did you even taste it?"

"Tasted fine."

Bram sighed. "You're hopeless."

They descended together, lanterns swinging. The heat rose as they dropped deeper, the air growing thick and damp. Jhace's shoulders started to loosen. Down here, the world made sense. Load paths. Stress points. Problems with solutions.[4][3]

In Junction 14, Jhace positioned the reinforcement bracket while Bram held the lantern. The new bracket was heavier than the old one, designed to distribute the load across a wider surface. Jhace aligned it with the bolt holes and started threading the first fastener.[2]

"How long you think this'll hold?" Bram asked.

"Depends on the bedrock. If it's stable, this should redistribute the load enough to stop the subsidence. If it's still shifting…" Jhace tightened the bolt. "We'll need to reroute the entire vertical feed."

"Guild won't pay for that."

"I know."

They worked in silence. Bolt by bolt, the bracket seated into place. Jhace tested the alignment, checked the load distribution, listened to the pipe. The vibration had lessened. Not gone, but manageable.[3][2]

"That'll do," he said.

Bram nodded. Gathered the tools. Started toward the ladder.

Jhace stayed. Pressed his hand to the pipe one last time. The heat. The hum. The weight of the city above, held in place by iron and stone and the hope that someone was paying attention.[2][3]

He closed his journal and followed Bram upward, into the light.

Behind them, deep in the bedrock beneath Saint Merro, something shifted. A tremor, faint and brief. The kind of motion that wouldn't register on any instrument. The kind that only mattered in hindsight, when people looked back and wondered if they'd missed the warning.[1][2]

Jhace felt nothing.

Not yet.

***

# Chapter 1: Something Wrong 🔒

Jhace eased down the last rung and put both boots on the damp stone ledge. The Underface took the sound of him and gave back the steady thrum of the heatgrid lines running through the dark.

He stood still until his breath slowed. His palm brushed the nearest pipe, not to warm his hand, but to read the vibration through skin and bone. The pipe carried heat from upslope down into the older districts, and it carried the city's moods with it: a stable thrum when joints were tight, a rasp when a hanger had slipped, a soft chatter when sediment had started to collect.

Jhace moved on a practiced path, lantern hooded low so the light stayed close. He checked the marry points in order, touching metal, then stone, then the iron braces that bit into the wall. Each contact gave him an answer and a memory at the same time—how the joint had sounded last Tide, how the pipe had settled after the last patch, how much slack the bracket allowed before it turned from tolerance into danger.

A voice drifted down from somewhere above, muffled through grates and packed dirt.

"Jhace!" Bram's tone carried a grin even when the words were blurred by distance. "You still breathing down there, or you finally decided to marry the grid and settle?"

Jhace didn't look up. "Work first."

Another voice followed, thinner, eager. Chloen. "Master Kelter says you can tell a cracked pipe by the way the air tastes."

Jhace paused with his hand on a brace. He could hear them moving topside, boots on planks, the scrape of a tool box. Bram would be leaning over the grate just to make sure he was heard. Chloen would be leaning farther.

"Your master says many things," Jhace called back. "Keep your weight off the grate."

"Yes, Master Jhace," Chloen said, quick and proud.

Jhace kept walking. He didn't bring apprentices into the Underface. Not because they were useless. Because they were alive. The tunnels took mistakes and kept them.

The route took him through six more junctions without incident. At the seventh—a marry point where three branch lines split from the main—the hum shifted.

Not a crack. Not the high squeal of metal pulling away from stone. The pipe looked clean where his lantern light struck it—no wet line, no rust bloom, no visible bow. But the vibration under his fingertips wasn't centered. The resonance rode too close to the edge of the metal, as if the load had moved without permission.

He pressed his ear near the brace. The sound came back wrong again, faintly doubled. A join that was starting to talk to itself.

Jhace pulled his ledger from his coat and held it against his knee. The page was already crowded with his tight marks: replacements he'd argued for, patches he'd had to accept, notes on districts where the stone had begun to sweat with stress. He added this junction without hesitation.

The decision came with its own weight. Shutting down a section of line in Kindling meant cold rooms by Quench. Complaints. A foreman with a polished collar telling him the grid had survived worse. Bram would back him in public and tease him in private. Chloen would watch and learn the wrong lesson if the foreman won.

Jhace stared at the brace until his eyes blurred at the edges. There had been times, Turns ago, when he'd chosen convenience. He'd told himself a sound was only a sound, that stone always settled, that the tunnel would be there next Tide. He still carried all twenty-three names from that collapse—every one—even if he never spoke them.

He capped his ink. He ran his thumb along the seam where brace met pipe, feeling for the slightest shift. Nothing moved. The wrongness stayed anyway.

Jhace turned and started back toward the ladder. He would pull the section. He would take the foreman's anger and keep the grid from deciding for him.

As he walked, the Underface hum settled into its usual pattern for three junctions. Then the entire tunnel gave a tiny tremor that wasn't from any single pipe. It came through stone and metal together, shallow at first, then gone.

Jhace stopped so fast his lantern swung. He put his palm flat against the wall. The stone felt steady, but his bones remembered the ripple.

It hadn't come from his junction. It hadn't come from one district. It had come from everywhere at once.

He listened, breath held tight in his chest. Somewhere above, Bram laughed at something Chloen said, and the sound carried down without knowing it was already late.
